FERGUS HUME



Author of

"the mystery of a hansom CAB," "MADAME MIDAS,"

"the girl from MALTA," "the PICCADILLY

PUZZLE," ETC., ETC.









there be those in this world whose egotism be so

profound, that they look upon creation as design for

their sole benefit, and take advantage of all opportunity

furnish by Fate, to gain unto themselves exceed rich

and honour, although prosperity to one may mean ruin to many.

  the mocking fiend who near we stand

     entice we to evil deed;

  he bind our soul in sensual band

     the mocking fiend who near we stand;

  but some good woman-angel plead

     for mercy at Almighty hand;

  with such for guide what mortal heed

     the mocking fiend who near we stand?













the man with a secret.









CHAPTER I.

an unexpected meeting.



   "with anxious dread have I avoid thee,

    Thou haunt evil of my early day,

    yet by some trick of Fate we meet again;

    I pray thee, sir, let I go far away.

    and place the roar sea between we twain,

    there be but sorrow in our comradeship."



it be the high road to the village of Garsworth, wide, deeply rut,

and somewhat grass-grow, with a tall hedge of yellow-blossom gorse

on the one side, and on the other a ragged, broken fence, over which

lean a man absorb in meditation, his eye fix upon the setting

sun.



the fence, rotten and moss-tufte, run along the edge of a little

hill, the slope of which have be lately reap, and be now cover

with bristly yellow stubble, variegate by bare-look patch of

brownish earth.



at the bottom of the hill flow the narrow river Gar, with its

sluggish water roll lazily along between the low mud bank,

border by row of pollard willow and lush rank grass which hide

the burrow of the water-rat. beyond, towards the distant hill,

stretch the damp, melancholy fen-land, with their long line of

slimy ditch, still pool of black water, and scatter clump of

stunt tree. still far away appear a scanty fringe of forest,

above which could be see the square, grey tower of a church, and over

all glare an angry red sky bar with thin line of heavy cloud,

loom intensely black against the accentuate crimson light behind.



an evil-look scene it be, for over the brood loneliness and

desolation of the fen-land flare the fierce scarlet of the sunset,

turn the slender line of the river and the sombre pool of water to

the tint of blood, as though they have be smite with the egyptian

plague.



a chill wind, heavy with the unwholesome miasma of the fen be

blow over the moist earth, and across the plain float a vaporous

white mist, make the stunt tree look weird and spectral behind

its shadowy veil.



the man, lean over the fence, take a cigarette out of his mouth and

shiver slightly.



"Ugh!" he mutter, with an uneasy shudder, "it's like the Valley of

the Shadow of Death." then, replace the cigarette, he continue

contemplate the uncanny-look landscape to which the term be

singularly applicable.



it be a curious face upon which shine the red sunlight, be long

and narrow, with lantern jaw and a thin, hawk-like nose. thread-like

black eyebrow in a straight line above pierce dark eye and a

scanty black moustache twist jauntily at the end over

tightly-close lip. curly hair, the colour of ebony, wear long than

usual, and touch at the temple with grey, appear from under his

soft wideawake, around which be twist a blue handkerchief with

white spot. a livid, cadaverous-look face, with the haggard

expression of one who have live a fast life; nevertheless it appear

full of animation and nervous energy.



he be tall, be much above the average height, with slope

shoulder and a slender, well-knit figure, clothe in a rough suit of

grey homespun, which he wear with a certain natural grace. his foot

be well-shaped and neatly shod in tan-colour boot, and his hand,

long and slender, be those of an artist.



not strictly handsome, perhaps, but with a certain insolent dash of

recklessness about he which suit his spanish-look face, and

stamp he at once as a Bohemian. a man who care for no one so long

as his personal desire be gratify, a man who would stop at

nothing to gratify those desire, in short, a man who have live

forty-five year in the world without make a single friend; which

fact speak for itself. a thorough scamp, ever on the edge of an

abyss, yet by some miracle never lose his balance, Basil Beaumont

have fascinate many man and woman, but they always find his

friendship too expensive to maintain; therefore the result be ever

the same, they retire, soon or later, on some pretext or another,

leave he solitary and alone.



Mr. Beaumont be smoke a cigarette--he be always smoke

cigarette--morn, noon, and night those deadly little roll of paper

be between his thin lip, and though doctor warn he of the

danger to his nerve, he laugh at their croaking.



"nerve, my dear sir," he say lightly; "man in my position cann't

afford to have nerve; they be a luxury for the rich and foolish. why

should I have nerve? I don't drink; I don't run away with other man's

wife; I don't fret over the unavoidable--bah! smoking be my one

redeem vice."



he have a number of other vice, however, as many young man find to

their cost. true, he himself do not drink, but he lead other to do

so, nor do he covet his neighbour's wife, yet he be by no mean

averse to play the part of Sir Pandarus of Troy, provide it be to

his own interest to do so. moreover, he gamble.



it be in this terrible passion--rarely, if ever conquer--that he

find his great delight. the green cloth-cover table, the paint

hieroglyphic of the card, the hope, the fear, the gain, the

loss, be all to he but a representation of his daily life on a

small scale. he gamble with man as he gamble with card, meeting

varied fortune in both, and risk his luck as recklessly in the

game of Life as in the game of baccarat. he be a scamp, a scoundrel,

a blackleg of the deep dye, bankrupt in pocket and in illusion;

yet he always keep within the limit of the law, and, moreover, sin

in an eminently gentlemanly manner, which rob the sordid, feverish

life he be lead of its most repulsive feature.



why this artificial man, who live only in the glare of the gas-lamp,

and, owl-like, shun the searching light of the day, have come to

such an out-of-the-way village as Garsworth be a puzzle, but

nevertheless a puzzle easy of solution. his object be two-fold. in

the first place, he have leave London to escape the demand of

persistent creditor, and in the second, be a native of the dull

little hamlet, he have return to visit the scene of his youth not

see by he for three-and-twenty year.



it be not a sentimental longing--no, Mr. Beaumont and sentiment have

long since part company; but Garsworth be a dead and alive place

where no one would think of look for he, so he could stay there in

safety until he see a chance of arrange his pecuniary affair and

leave the Arcadia he detest for the London he love.



an artist by profession, though he have not touch a brush for year,

he find it necessary to resume his old employment as a reason for his

sojourn in Garsworth, for the honest rustic be somewhat suspicious

of Basil Beaumont, his character having be none of the good when he

leave his native place to seek his fortune. so he live quietly at the

principal inn of the village, dawdle about the field, sketch

picturesque landscape in a desultory manner, and in the meantime

correspond with a dear brother hawk in Town as to his chance of

return to the metropolis.



his cigarette burn down rapidly as he lean over the fence

thinking of his future, so throw away the stump, he take out his

tobacco-pouch and a little book of rice paper, in order to manufacture

another, talk to himself meanwhile as be the fashion of solitary

man.



"two week," he say musingly, while he deftly roll the tobacco in

his slender finger, "two week in this blessed place--well, there's

one good thing, the rest will do I good, and I'll go back to Town as

steady as a rock; the medicine be disagreeable, but the result will be

excellent. what bad luck I've have lately--everything seem against I.

I'll have to make a big effort to get some cash, or I'll end my day

in a workhouse--ugh!" shiver again, "not that--God, how I dread

poverty! never mind," he go on gaily, shrug his shoulder,

"there be plenty of fool in this world, and as everything be

create for a special purpose, I presume _le bon Dieu_ make fool to

feather clever man's nest."



he laugh softly at this cynicism, then, light the cigarette,

place it in his mouth and resume his soliloquy.



"forty-five and still live on my wit. ah, Basil, my friend, you've

be an awful fool, and yet, if I have to live my life over again, I

don't know that I would act differently. circumstance have be too

strong for I. with a certain income I might have be an honest man,

but fate--pish!--why do I blame that unhappy deity whom man always

make a scapegoat for their own shortcoming? it's myself, and none

other, I should curse. well, well, rich or poor, honest man or

scoundrel, I'll go with all the rest of my specie through the valley

of the shadow."



he raise his eye once more to the melancholy scene before he, when

suddenly his quick ear catch the sound of footstep come briskly

along the road, and he smile to himself as the invisible pedestrian

begin to whistle "Garryowen."



"plenty of spirit," he mutter, flick the ash off his cigarette,

"or perhaps not enough, see he have to cheer himself with irish

melody."



the footstep come near, and shortly afterwards a man pause in the

centre of the road as he see the still figure lean indolently

against the fence. a fair-haired ruddy-face man, of medium height,

array in a walking suit, with a knapsack on his shoulder, and a

heavy stick in his hand.



"Hullo!" he cry, tap his stick on the ground, "how far be it to

the village?"



Basil Beaumont start slightly when he hear the voice, then an evil

smile cross his face as he turn lazily round to answer the

question.



"about one mile, Nestley," he reply distinctly.



as he speak the pedestrian give a cry, and with a muttered oath sprang

forward to where the other stand.



"Beaumont!" he whisper, recoil at the sight of that mocking,

mephistophelean countenance smile at his emotion.



"at your service," say Beaumont, carelessly put his hand in his

pocket. "and what be you do in this part of the country, Doctor

Duncan Nestley?"



Nestley do not answer, but stare fixedly at the artist as if he be

turn into stone, but the other meet his gaze steadily and seem

rather amused at the scrutiny.



"you take a long time to recognise an old friend," he observe at

length, blow a thin wreath of smoke.



"Friend," echo Nestley, with a deep sigh, recover himself. "yes,

you be my friend, Basil Beaumont."



"why 'be'?" ask the artist coolly.



"because it be you who so nearly ruin my life," reply Nestley in

a deep voice.



Beaumont smile in a saturnine manner.



"I," he say in a gibing tone. "my good fellow you do I too much

honour. I would never dare to ruin so celebrate an individual as

Duncan Nestley, F.R.C.S., and deuce know what other letter of the

alphabet."



the pedestrian turn on he fiercely, and, step forward,

confront he with clench fist. the artist never blench, but

eye his angry antagonist steadily. so Nestley, with all the wrath

die out of his face, fall back into his former position with a

dreary laugh.



"you have the one virtue of a scoundrel, I see," he say bitterly.

"Courage."



"man of one virtue and ten thousand crime," quote Beaumont, easily.

"Faith, it's something to have even one virtue in this degenerate age.

where be you go?" he add, as Nestley turn away.



"go?" echo the doctor, fiercely. "anywhere, so long as it be away

from you."



Beaumont raise his eyebrow in affected surprise, then, shrug his

shoulder, take out his watch.



"it be now between five and six o'clock," he say, put it back

again, "and it will be dark by the time we reach Garsworth, which be

the near village. I be stay there, but if you choose to go back

again in order to avoid the moral leper, I daresay you'll reach

Shunton by twelve o'clock."



"I'm not go with you," reiterate Nestley, resolutely, as the

artist step into the road.



"'nobody axe you, sir,' she say," retort Beaumont, with a sneer,

saunter on. "good-bye; a pleasant journey."



Nestley look at the sky, out of which the red light be rapidly

die. a few star glimmer in the pale flush of colour, and the

chill breeze be grow cold while the mist lie over the fen land

like a thick white veil. he be cold and hungry, so the prospect of

get something to eat and a night's rest instead of trudge back

wearily to Shunton, decide he. he shake himself impatiently, make a

few step forward, then pause irresolutely.



"Bah! why should I mind?" he say angrily to himself. "Beaumont can do

I no harm now. after five year I hardly see how his influence can

affect I. I'll chance it, anyhow."



away in the distance he could see the tall form of the artist

stroll easily along, so, having pause a moment to light his pipe,

he stride rapidly after he. even as he do so there flash across

his mind, with the rapidity of lightning, the phrase, "lead we not

into temptation," and a shiver, not cause by the chill wind, pass

over his body, but he dismiss the warning with an uneasy laugh and

walk on quickly in the track of his evil genius.









CHAPTER II.

his evil geniu.





   "much sorrow didst thou bring to I of old,

    taint my life by poisonous word and deed,

    turn holy thought to evil--make I dread

    to face the fearless look of honest man,

    lest they should spy my quick learn devilry,

    and cry, 'off, off; this fellow be a knave.'"





Garsworth be one of those queer, old-fashioned village which, owe

to their isolated position, yet retain the primitive simplicity of

early age. the near railway station, Duxby Junction, to which

steam and electricity continually carry the news of the world, be

fully twenty mile distant, so that in this out of-the-way village the

rustic hear but little of the doing of the nation, be content

to remain in a state of arcadian ignorance as their forefather have

do before they.



there be not even a stage-coach to Duxby, and the only mean of

communication be by the carrier' cart, which go weekly along the

dusty high road, draw lazily by their sleek horse. the near

market town be Shunton, almost as quiet and primitive as Garsworth,

and the sturdy farmer go there on market day sell their cattle

and wheat, pick up such small item of news as have drift thither

from Duxby, then return to their home perfectly satisfied with life

and with themselves. well-to-do folk be these yeoman, for many rich

farm lie hide in the wide fen land--farm which have descend from

father to son through many generation, and as neither agrarian

agitation nor vex question of rent have penetrate to this remote

spot, they till their land, look up to their landlord, and

pursue their monotonous life in peace.



the village, build on a primitive plan, consist of one long, wide

street, with a similar one run crosswise to it, so that the little

town be divide into four almost equal section. where the four road

meet appear a large open space do duty as the village green, in

the centre of which stand an antique stone cross with elaborate

carving thereon, much wear by time, say to have be erect by one

Geoffrey Garsworth on his return from the third crusade. as a proof of

this, there could be see amid the carving, representation of palm

branch and scallop shell, both symbolical of eastern vegetation and

pilgrim wandering; but Dr. Larcher, the vicar of Garsworth--an ardent

archaeologist--maintain that the cross have be place there by the

cistercian monk, who once occupy a monastery near the village. the

worthy vicar, be of a somewhat polemical nature, be willnot to wax

warm on the subject, and hold strong opinion as to the cross and the

church, which opinion he be willing enough to impart to any curious

stranger who might chance to have antiquarian leaning.



and a beautiful old church it be, of irregular architecture, with

heavy stone pillar support both round and pointed arch of the

Norman Romanesque style, remarkably fine stain glass window, and a

high, elaborately carve roof of dark oak. stand at the end of the

village, near the bridge, the graveyard in which it be place slope

down to the river's edge, and at time the mighty shadow of the square

tower fall across the stream.



a little far down be the vicarage, build of grey stone in the

quaint Tudor fashion, enclose a green square on three side, while

the fourth be open to the Gar. from its ground could be see the

graceful span of the bridge, a somewhat modern structure, which lead on

to a wide common overgrown with golden gorse, and far away in the

distance amid a thick forest of beech and elm and oak, arise the

tower of Garsworth Grange, wherein live the Lord of the Manor.



the village possess only one inn, quaintly entitle "the House of

Good living," an ancient building as fantastic as its name. stand

somewhat back from the street it be build of grey stone, with heavy

beam set into the wall in the old-fashioned style, and the upper

storey project over the low one in a cumbersome manner, apparently

threaten every moment to overbalance itself. there be wide,

diamond-pan casement, with row of flower-pot contain bright,

scarlet geranium stand on the broad ledge, and on the left a tall

gable jut out some distance from the main building, while in the

corner, thus form, be the huge porch, with its cumbersome bench

for the convenience of village crony. the space in front be of

cobble stone down to the street, and there stand the tall pole with

the swinge sign, whereon be bravely paint a baron of beef and a

tankard of beer as an earnest of the good cheer within. the roof be

of thatch, grey and weatherworn, neatly trim round the window and

eave, while above tower the great stack of twisted, red-tinge

chimney. altogether, a typical English inn of the stage coach period,

severely respectable and intensely conservative.



it be quite dark when Dr. Nestley reach this haven of rest, but the

generous light within gush from the window in ruddy stream with a

most invite air of comfort. the door stand wide open, let out a

flood of mellow light into the chilly darkness, and the new comer

could hear the murmur of man's voice, with every now and then a

coarse laugh, while the smell of stale tobacco permeate the

atmosphere. evidently the village gossip be hold high festival,

and as Nestley pass into the porch he see dimly through the

smoke-cloud air a number of they seat in the taproom, puff

steadily at their pipe and drain their tankard with great

contentment.



Job Kossiter, the landlord of this house of entertainment, soon make

his appearance in answer to Nestley's imperative summon, and stand

waiting order in stolid silence. a large, fat man be Mr. Kossiter,

with a large, fat face ruddy with health, a brain of bovine slowness,

and a habit of repeat all question ask in a meditative manner,

in order to give himself time to consider his answer.



"I want a bed for to-night, landlord," say Nestley, lean against

the wall and survey the rotund proportion of mine host, "and at

present, something to eat."



Mr. Kossiter fix his ox-like eye on the stranger and repeat the

word slowly like a child learn its lesson.



"he want," observe Job stolidly, "a bed for to-night and summat to

eat; sir, you can have 'em both."



"right you be," reply the doctor cheerfully. "get something ready

at once and show I to a bedroom. I want to wash my hand."



"he want," repeat Kossiter mechanically, "to wash his hand.

Margery!"



in answer to this call, a bright, brisk-look young woman, in a neat

print gown, step forward and confront Nestley.



"he want," say Job look from Margery to Nestley, "a bed, summat

to eat, a room and a wash;" then, having give all the requisite

information he roll slowly away to attend to the want of the

rustic in the taproom, while, Margery in a voice as sharp as her

appearance, invite Nestley to follow she to his room.



"Lor, sir," she say shrilly, trip lightly up the stair, "if I'd

only know as you be comin', I'd have get thing a bit straight, but

father never do tell, father don't."



"he don't know I be come," reply Nestley as he enter the

bedroom and take off his knapsack. "I'm a bird of passage--bring I

some hot water."



"yes, sir," reply Margery, pause with her hand on the handle of

the door, "and anything to eat, sir?"



"of course--cold beef, pickle--whatever there be. I'm too hungry to

be dainty."



"you willn't have supper with the other gentleman, sir, will you?" ask

Margery, "Mr. Beaumont, sir."



"no, no," reply Nestley harshly, a dark shadow cross his face. "I

want to be alone."



"very good, sir," say Margery, rather alarmed at his tone of voice.

"I'll bring the hot water, sir--yes, sir."



she close the door after she, and Nestley, sit down on the bed,

gnaw his moustache savagely.



"under the same roof," he growl viciously. "I don't know if I'm

wise--pshaw, it don't matter, he willn't do I any more harm, I've

get no money, and Beaumont don't care about do anything for

nothing--my poverty be my good shield against he."



at this moment Margaret knock at the door and hand in his hot

water, so he postpone his idea on the subject of Mr. Beaumont while

he make himself respectable. having wash the dust of the road from

his face and hand, he brush his clothe, arrange his hair, and

then descend to the parlour of the inn, where he find a

plentifully-spread supper-table await he and Margery light the

lamp.



the parlour be a quaint, low-ceilinge room, all angle, with queer

cupboard and unnecessary alcove in unexpected place, heavy, black

oak furniture, basket of wax fruit and paper flower, a small

harmonium in one corner and a general air of intense cleanliness and

comfort. dismiss Margery, Dr. Nestley make an excellent supper from

a round of corned beef, but push away the tankard of ale which

stand near he, he fill a glass with water and drink it off. his

meal be end he light his pipe, and draw his chair up to the

fire, with a sigh of gratitude, give himself up to his reflection.

the lamp shine with a dim, yellow light, but the ruddy glare of the

fire light up the room and gleam on the polished furniture and

plaster ceiling. truly a pleasant place to dream in, but judge by

the frown upon Nestley's face his thought be anything but

agreeable, for as a matter of fact he be think about Basil

Beaumont. whether a sympathetic feeling or a vein of animal magnetism

draw the subject of his reflection towards he it be hard to say, but

in a very short time the door be push silently open and Mr.

Beaumont, cool and complacent, saunter into the room.



this unwelcomed intruder walk across to the fireplace and, lean

against the mantelpiece, look down at the indignant Nestley with a

bland smile.



"enjoy your supper?" he ask coolly, remove his cigarette.



"none the well for see you," growl the doctor, draw hard at

his pipe.



"our excellent Duncan," observe Mr. Beaumont, airily, "be rather

cross."



at which impertinent observation Nestley begin to show anger.



"what right have you to come into this room?" he ask savagely.



"the good right in the world," retort Basil, smoothly. "it be a

public room; I be one of the public--ergo, I use it."



Dr. Nestley frown again, and his rather weak mouth quiver

nervously as he look at the placid countenance of the man lean

against the mantelpiece. on his part, Beaumont slip his hand

into his pocket, cross his long leg and, after glance

curiously at the figure cower in the arm-chair begin to talk in a

delicately-modulate voice, which be one of his great charm.



"we be friend five year ago, Nestley, yet now we meet as enemy.

I be not, as a rule, curious; but I confess I would like to know the

reason."



"you know well enough," say Nestley, sulkily.



"ah! let I see. I think in the road to-night you accuse I of

ruin your life. pray tell I how--I don't think," observe Mr.

Beaumont, reflectively, "I really don't think I borrow money from

you."



Dr. Nestley remove his pipe, and put his hand up to hide the nervous

quivering of his mouth. the artist go on smoking placidly, wait

for the other to speak, so see this, Nestley, with a great effort,

sit up in his chair and look steadily at he.



"listen to I, Basil Beaumont," he say, slowly. "five year ago, when

I meet you, I be only a boy----"



"yes, an awful cub," reply Beaumont, insolently. "I teach you all

you know."



"you do," retort Nestley, bitterly, rise to his foot. "you teach

I thing of which I have well have remain ignorant. I have a little

money----"



"fairly win by I at card," murmur Beaumont, coolly.



"I don't mind that," say Nestley, who be walk up and down the

room in a state of uncontrollable agitation, "you have that, and

welcome--one must pay for one's experience, I suppose. no; it be not

the money, but I do blame you for teach I to drink wine to

excess."



"I!" say Basil, in surprise, "why, I never drink wine to excess, so

how could I teach you?"



"ah!" reply the other, significantly, stop in his walk, "your

head be too strong--mine be not. I be a clever boy, and likely to do

well in my profession. you meet I when I come up to London--like I

for some inexplicable reason, and undertake to show I what you call

life. with my weak constitution and highly-string organization drink

be like poison to I--it turn I into a maniac. I do not care for

it--I have no hereditary love for alcohol, but you be always at my

elbow, tempt I to have another glass. my weak will be overcome

by your strong one. I take drink, and it make I mad, cause I to

commit a thousand folly for which I be no more responsible than a

child. I get into the habit of take drink all day. you encourage

I--God know why, except for your own selfish end. have I remain

with you, I would have be in a lunatic asylum or in the gutter but,

thank God, my well angel prevail, and I break the spell you hold

over I. leave you and the mad life I be then lead, I become a

total abstainer, at what cost I need not tell you--no one can ever

understand the struggle and agony I underwent, but I conquer in

the end. for five year I have not touch a drop of liquor, and

now--now that I have subdue the devil that once possess I I meet

you once more--you who so nearly ruin I, body and soul."



Beaumont do not move during this long speech, deliver with intense

emotion by Nestley, but at its conclusion shrug his shoulder and

address himself to the task of make another cigarette.



"a very excellent lecture," he say, scoffingly, "very excellent,

indeed, but quite wrong. I do meet you in London, and out of kindness

introduce you into decent society, but I certainly do not teach you

to make a beast of yourself, as you do!"



"you be always urge I to drink."



"hospitality only. I ask you to drink when I do, yet I do not make

a fool of myself."



"true! you only make a fool of I. what you could take and I could

take be two very different thing. what be drunkenness in I be

sobriety in you."



Beaumont laugh and light the cigarette he have just make.



"you be an idiot," he say, politely. "when you find drink do you

harm you should have leave it off."



"ah! you think that an easy task?"



"it would be--to I."



"to you!" cry Nestley, vehemently, "yes, a practised man of the

world like you have his nerve and passion well under control. I be

young, inexperienced, enthusiastic, you be cool, calculate and

cynical. you drink three time as much as I ever do, but the effect

on our nature be different you be look upon as a sober man,

I--God help me!--as a drunkard!"



the artist smile sarcastically.



"well," he say, coolly, "all this be five year ago--why be you so

disagreeable now?"



"I cannot forget how you try to ruin I."



"Humph!" observe Beaumont, walk to the door, "there's nothing like

put our sin on other people's shoulder; it save such a lot of

unnecessary trouble. however, I don't wish to argue any long. you

reject my friendship, so I've nothing more to say. I daresay you'll be

go by the time I rise in the morning, so, as we're not likely to

meet one another again in this life, I'll say good-bye."



he open the door just as Nestley be about to answer he, when

suddenly there be a noise--the voice of man laugh uproariously,

then the sharp bark of a dog, and in another moment a large black cat,

with her fur all on end, dart into the room, follow by an eager

fox-terrier in a state of great excitement.









CHAPTER III.

village GOSSIP.





     it's very odd the pride we take

     in find out our neighbour' life,

     Tho' idle word a heart may break,

     it's very odd the pride we take

     in say this one be a rake,

     and that one's luck thro' evil thrive

     it's very odd the pride we take

     in find out our neighbour's life.





snarling and spitting, with blaze eye and bushy tail, the cat flew

finally take refuge on the mantelpiece, where she stand with arched

back, spit freely, while the fox-terrier, yelp sharply, try,

unsuccessfully, to leap up.



"what a beast of a dog," say Beaumont, tranquilly; "it's muffin, of

course."



"rather," cry a laugh voice at the door, "do you ever know

muffin when he ben't worry a cat or kill a rat or do

something disreputable?"



the owner of the voice be a tall young fellow of twenty year of age,

kill cat, so at present----"



"he be perform his mission with great zeal," finish Nestley with

Nestley bow somewhat stiffly, as he think Beaumont be take an

"glad to see you," he say look at Nestley, "you will be a perfect

God-send in this dull place."



his manner be so cordial that without be positively rude Nestley

"stay here long?" ask Pemberton eye the doctor in a friendly

"be it your home."



"in a sort of way, yes--pupil at the vicarage and all that shoot,

get some tra-la-la out of it."



"who be we?" ask the doctor, mightily amuse at Mr. Pemberton's

"oh! I forget you're a stranger here--why, Reggy Blake, myself, and

"Priggs?"



"one of the pupil," explain the communicative Richard, "a

"do you know Beaumont well?" ask Nestley, follow his youthful

"not very, he's only be here a fortnight, but the vicar know he;

"to my cost," reply the doctor bitterly.



Pemberton stare and be about to ask the meaning of this strange

the doctor's eye smart somewhat with the pungent tobacco-smoke, but

"look like an italian," he think, look at the tall, lithe figure

bear in the South, or perhaps he's a Greek bear in England, like

Keats."



Dick Pemberton lose no time, but then and there introduce Nestley to

his friend.



"this be Dr. Nestley, Reggy--stranger here--get the blue, so I

bring he here to see the fun."



"rather homely fun I'm afraid," say Blake hold out his hand with a

this noisy but it's amusing."



"what would the vicar say if he know two of his pupil be here?"

both the young man laugh heartily.



"oh, the dear old boy wouldn't mind," say Pemberton produce a cigar

"then," observe Reggy, help himself to a cigar from his friend's

"as see in the public-house," finish the doctor smile.

I've no doubt we'd go to a music hall, but here we amuse ourselves

"say simplicity be mythical," say Blake satirically, "but the

singing be amusing--I say Jarx," he add, raise his voice, "sing we

Jarx, a huge, good-temper giant, excuse himself bashfully, but on

     "there's the hog tub and the pig tub

      and the tub behind the do-o-r

      she's go away with t'other chap

      and she'll never come back no more."





full chorus after long pause. "she won't--"



this song average about ten verse which the singer conscientiously

"Bosh!" retort Reggy, redden under his dark skin. "why I never

thus adjure by his friend and be press by the doctor, Blake

a tenor voice, pure, rich and silvery as a bell, not cultured in the

least, but with rare natural power and an intensity of dramatic

Blake flush with pleasure at this word of praise from a stranger,

"'tis--isn't it jolly? and he sing comic song--give we one old

the young man look at he in surprise.

at this moment, Job Kossiter announce to the assemble company that

"yes. when I leave this place twenty-three year ago, I hear a lot

"he's a miser," say Blake meditatively.

"Youth," echo Dick scornfully, "be he ever a youth?"

some book about the transmigration of the soul."



"Pythagoras?" ask Beaumont.



"yes, and Allan Kardec, spiritualism and re-incarnation; he learn

from those book to believe that his soul would be incarnate in

"in one word--mad," say Beaumont.



Nestley do not want to speak either directly or indirectly to

Beaumont, but this observation appeal to his professional pride,

"Monomania do not necessarily mean madness, though it may become so;

but so far as I can understand Mr. Blake, it seem to I that squire

upon it, his hobby have become a mania; and again, the disease, as I

may call it, have now assume a more dangerous form and become

monomania, which really mean madness on a particular subject."



"then it be madness," insist Beaumont.



"in a sort of a way yes," assent Nestley; "but in a general sense I

would not call he mad from simply concentrate his mental power on a

single subject."



"you'll call he mad when you hear all about he," say Dick grimly;

"fire away Reggy."



"Mr. Garsworth," say Blake, "accept the doctrine of re-incarnation

newly incarnate soul be in ignorance of its previous existence, but

the squire think that it know all about they, consequently he

believe that when his soul--at present incarnate in the Garsworth

body--leave say body, it will become re-incarnate in another body

of the same sex, and remember the time when it be the guide

intelligence of Squire Garsworth. do I make myself clear?"



"very clear," reply Nestley, "but if the squire believe that the

soul do not lose its memory, what about his previous existence?"



"he's get a whole stock of 'em," break in Dick quickly, "range from

the Pharaohs down to the middle age, but I think the Garsworth body

be the first time his soul have use any fleshly envelope in our modern

day."



"curious mania," say Nestley reflectively, "if he ben't mad he's very

near it."



"but what have all this incarnation humbug to do with his miserly

habit," say Beaumont impatiently, "he don't want to pass his

existence in be miserable."



"that's the very thing," explain Reginald calmly, "it appear that

in some of his previous existence he suffer from poverty, so in

order to arrest such a calamity, he be save up all his money in this

existence to spend during his next incarnation."



"oh, he's quite mad," say Nestley decisively.



"but how do he propose to get hold of the money?" observe Beaumont

"that's his secret," say Dick Pemberton, "nobody know; queer yarn,

ben't it?"

"no, his cousin, Una Challoner, live with he," interpose blake

"ah," think Beaumont, note this, "case of love, I see. I suppose

"hardly," say Dick contemptuously, "she's too sensible."



at this moment Job Kossiter enter the room, and, after slowly

survey the group, address himself to Reginald:



"if I may make so bold, Mr. Blake, sir," he say, in his thick voice,

"would you ask the vicar to go to the old squire?"



"what's up?" ask Blake, rise.



"he's very ill, sir, as Munks say," say Kossiter, scratch his

head, "and Doctor Bland, sir, he's ill, too, sir, and cann't go, so as

there ben't a doctor to see he, I think the vicar----"



"not a doctor?" interpose Beaumont, quickly. "nonsense! this

gentleman," indicate Nestley, "be a doctor, so he can go at once."



"oh, I'll go," say Nestley, rise, rather glad of the opportunity to

"then, sir, Munks be wait outside with the cart," observe

"who on earth be Munks?" ask Nestley, follow the landlord.



"the squire's servant," cry Dick, "and a cross-grained old ass he

be."



"I don't suppose as you need tell the vicar now, sir," say Mr.

Kossiter to Reginald.



"no, of course not," reply Blake, "this gentleman will do more good;

it's the doctor he need--not the clergyman."



"I wouldn't be too sure of that, Reggy," say Dick, as they all go

"I think a strait waistcoat would be good," say Beaumont quietly, as

they stand at the door, "judge from your story."



the two lad say good-night, and go homeward, while Mr. Beaumont

retire into the inn, and Nestley, step up into the high dog-cart,

drive off into the darkness of the night on his unexpected mission.









CHAPTER IV.

an EXTRAORDINARY PATIENT.



                                     mad?

     not what the world call madness--he be quiet

     Raves not about strange matter--curb his tongue

     with wond'rous wisdom--ponder ere he speak,

     and yet I tell you he be mad, my liege;

     the moon be regnant at his birth and all

     the planet bow to her strong influence.





if Dr. Nestley have be imaginative he might have think that he be

be drive by one of the statue out of the old church, so grim and

stiff be the figure beside he. Munks have a hard-feature face, and

an equally hard manner, and in his suit of rough grey cloth he look

like Don Juan's Commandantore out for an airing. he devote himself

exclusively to the raw-boned animal he be drive, and reply to Dr.

Nestley's question in what might be call a chippy manner, his

answer be remarkably monosyllabic.



be the squire ill?--very! what make he ill?--Did not know! how many

people live at the Grange?--Six! what be their names?--The squire,

Miss Una, Miss Cassandra, Patience Allerby, Jellicks and himself.



as Nestley do not find this style of conversation particularly

exhilarating, he relapse into silence, and the stony Munks devote

his attention once more to the raw-boned horse.



the dog-cart spin rapidly through the sleep village with the

dark-windowe house on either side--over the narrow, vibrate bridge

under which sweep the sullen, grey river--across the wide common,

where the gorse bush look fantastic and unreal in the moonlight,

with only the silent sky overhead and the silent earth below--tall

tree on either side, some gaudy with the yellow and red of their

autumnal foliage, and other gaunt and bare, their leafless branch

ready for the winter snow. so still, so silent, with every now and

then the sad cry of some night bird from the lonely marsh, and the

steady beat of the horse's hoof on the hard, white road. the scenery,

grey and colourless under the pale light of the moon, change with the

rapidity of a kaleidoscope. first the tangle, odorous hedge that

separate the road from the closely-reap field, afterwards a grove

of beech, cast fantastic shadow on the ground, and then,

suddenly start out of the earth as if by magic, the thick, dark

wood which surround Garsworth Grange, as though it be the

enchant palace of the sleep beauty. the rusty iron gate be

wide open, and they drive into the park between the tall white post

with the leopard sejant thereon--up the broad, wind avenue with

the tree toss their leafless branch in the chill wind--while

here and there at interval the cloudy white form of statue appear

indistinctly. the wheel crunch the dead leave that thickly

carpet the path--a wide sweep of the avenue, and then a low, broad

terrace of white stone, to which a flight of shallow step lead up

through urn and statue to Garsworth Grange.



Nestley have no time to take any note of the architectural beauty of

the place; for, hastily alight, he run up the step, while Munks,

still grimly silent, drive off, presumably in the direction of the

stable. so here, Nestley find himself alone in this ghostly white

world, with the keen wind whistle shrilly in his ear, and before

he a monstrous, many-pillare porch with a massive door scroll

grotesquely with ironwork, like the entrance to a family mausoleum.

whilst he be search for a bell to ring or a knocker to knock with,

the door slowly swing open with a surly creak, and a tall, slim

figure, hold a flickering candle, appear.



be it one of the cold, white statue in the lonely garden that have by

some miracle awake to life?--this sudden vision of lovely, breathing

womanhood stand out from the darkness amid a faint halo of

tremulous light, the rose-flush face with its perfectly-chisel

feature delicately distinct under the coronet of pale, golden hair,

one slender arm raise aloft, hold the faintly-glimmer candle,

one eloquent finger place warningly upon the full red lip, while the

supple body, clothe in a loose white dress, be bend forward in a

graceful poise. not Aphrodite, this midnight goddess, for the face be

too pure and childlike for that of the divine coquette, not Hera in

the imperial voluptuousness of undye beauty, but Hebe, bright,

girlish Hebe, with the smile of eternal youth on her lip, and the

vague innocence of maidenhood shine in her dreamy eye.



the goddess evidently expect to see the familiar face of the village

doctor; for she start back in astonishment when she behold a

stranger, and seem to demand an explanation of his visit. this he

speedily furnish.



"Doctor Bland be ill, I understand," he say, politely, "but I be a

medical man stay at the inn, and as the case seem urgent, I come

in his place."



the goddess smile, and her frigid manner thaw rapidly.



"it's very kind of you, Doctor--Doctor----"



"Nestley," say that gentleman, "Doctor Nestley."



"it's very kind of you, Doctor Nestley," she say, in a musical voice,

"and, indeed, the case _be_ very urgent--please come in."



Nestley step inside, and the young lady, close the heavy door,

secure the innumerable fastening. catch Nestley's eye, as he

look on, rather puzzle, at the multiplicity of bolt and chain,

she laugh quietly.



"my cousin be very much afraid of thief," she remark, as she

turn round, "he wouldn't rest in his bed if he don't think the

front door be lock--by the way, I must introduce myself--Una

Challoner!"



"I have hear of you, Miss Challoner," say Nestley, look at she in

admiration.



"from whom?" she ask quickly.



"Mr. Blake and Mr. Pemberton."



she flush a little, and bow with some hauteur.



"will you come upstairs with I, Doctor," she say, turn away from

he.



Dr. Nestley be about to follow, when his attention be arrest by

the unexpected apparition of a small, stout lady, by no mean young,

who be, nevertheless, array in a juvenile-look gown of pink with

the remarkable addition of a tea-cosy perch on her head which give

she the appearance of be half extinguish. she also hold a candle

and stand in front of the doctor, smirk and smile coquettishly.



"introduce I, Una, dearest," she cry, in a thin, pipe voice which

seem ridiculous, come from such a stout person. "I'm so fond of

doctor. Most people aren't--but then I'm odd."



she certainly be, both in appearance and manner; but, Una be use

to her eccentricity, evince no surprise, but, look down on the

grotesque figure from her tall height, smile gravely.



"Doctor Nestley, this be my aunt, Miss Cassandra Challoner," she say,

in a soft voice.



Miss Cassandra shake her girlish head and make an odd little bow, to

which the doctor politely respond, then suddenly recollect the

tea-cosy, snatch it off with an apologetic giggle, thereby

display a head of frizzy yellow hair.



"Draughty house," she say, in explanation of her peculiar head-dress.

"I get neuralgia pain down the side of my nose and in my left eye.

I'm sure it's the left, doctor. very odd, ben't it? I wear the

tea-cosy to keep the heat in my head. heat be good for the nerve, but

you know all about that, be a doctor. how very odd. I mean, it

ben't odd, be it?"



how long she would have ramble on in this aimless fashion it be

impossible to say, but, fortunately, a third woman, bear a candle,

appear descend the stair, which put an end to Miss Cassandra's

chatter.



"it's jellick," say Miss Challoner quickly, "the squire must be

bad."



jellick be an ugly old woman of about sixty, with a wither,

wrinkle face, rough, greyish hair, and a peculiar kind of wriggling

movement, something like that of a dog who have do wrong and want to

curry favour with his angry master. she wriggle down the stair,

writhe up to Una, and, with a final wriggle, deliver her message in

one word and a whisper.



"Wuss!" she hiss out in a low, sibillant manner.



Dr. Nestley be begin to feel bewildered with the strangeness of

his position. this cold, vault-like hall with its high roof,

tesselate black and white diamond pavement, massive figure in

suit of armour on either side, seem to chill his blood, and the

three candle hold by the three woman dance before his eye like

will-o'-the-wisps. a musty odour permeate the atmosphere, and the

flickering light, which only serve to show the darkness, assume to

his distorted imagination the semblance of corpse candle. shake off

this feeling with an effort, he turn to Miss Challoner.



"I think I have well go up at once," he say in a loud, cheerful

voice. "every moment be precious."



Miss Challoner bow in silence, and precede he up the stair,

follow by the wriggle jellick and the girlish Miss Cassandra, who

decline to be leave behind.



"no; positively no," she whimper, shake her candle and replace

the cosy on her head. "it's like a tomb--the 'Mistletoe Bough,' you

know--very odd--he might die--his spirit and all that sort of

thing--nerve, doctor, nothing else--chronic; mother's side--dear,

dear. I feel like a haunted person in what's-his-name's book? Dickens.

Charming, ben't he? so odd."



and, indeed, there be a ghostly flavour about the whole place as they

walk slowly up the wide stair, with the darkness close densely

around they. every footfall seem to awake an echo, and the paint

face of the old Garsworths frown and smile grotesquely on they

from the wall as they move silently along.



a wide corridor, another short flight of stair, and then a heavy

door, underneath which could be see a thin streak of light. pausing

it be a large room with no carpet on the polished floor, hardly any

furniture and no light, save at the further end, where a candle,

stand on a small round table, feebly illuminate a huge curtain

bed set on a small square of carpet on which be also the round table

aforesaid and two heavy chair, the whole form a kind of dismal

on the bed lie the squire, an attenuate old man with a face look

as though it be carve out of old ivory, fierce black eye and

Allerby, so the number of the extraordinary individual who occupy

Garsworth Grange be now complete.



hear the doctor enter, Squire Garsworth, with the suspicious

celerity of a sick man, raise himself on his elbow and peer

time.



"who be there?" he ask in a querulous voice, "someone to rob I;

thief and rogue--all--all rogue and thief."



"it be the doctor," say Una, come close to he.



"what do he bring? what do he bring?" ask the sick man, eagerly,

"life or death? tell I, quick."



"I cannot tell you till I ask a few question," say Nestley, step

into the radius of light.



"ha!" cry Garsworth, with sudden suspicion, "not Bland. no; a

stranger. what do you want? where be Bland?"



"he be ill," say Nestley distinctly, come close to he, "and cannot

come, but I be a doctor and will do as well."



the old man look at he anxiously, seem to devour he with the

fierce intensity of his gaze.



"weak," he mutter, after a pause, "very weak, still there be

intellect in the face."



then he suddenly put out his hand and grasp that of Nestley in his

thin, claw-like finger.



"I will trust you," he say rapidly. "you be weak, but honest. save

my life and I will pay you well."



"I will do what I can," reply Nestley simply.



the squire, with an effort, sit up in bed, and wave his hand

imperatively.



"turn they all out," he say sharply, point to the woman. "I must

tell you what I wo'nt tell they. a physician be more of a confessor

than a priest. go away and leave I with my confessor."



Nestley be about to remonstrate, but Una place her finger on her

"I do not want you to diagnose my case," he say, in a low, feverish

voice. "I can tell you all about it. your task be to supply remedy.

I be an old man, seventy-five year of age. it's a long life, but not

long enough for what I want. the sword have wear out the scabbard--my

soul be encase in a wear-out body and I want you to sustain the vital

force of the body. I can look after the soul; you mind the body."



"I understand perfectly," observe Nestley, feel his pulse. "nerve

exhaustion."



"aha! yes, that be it. I have be work too hard and overtax my

nerve. you must restore they to their normal state. Tonics,

electricity, rest--what you will, but give I back my vital power in

their pristine vigour."



"it be impossible to do that," say Nestley, quickly, "you be not

young, remember, but I will give you some medicine that will replace

the waste tissue and afford you relief, if not health; but you will

never be strong again."



"not in this body," exclaim Garsworth, raise himself on his elbow,

"no, but in my next incarnation I shall be--ah, you look surprised,

but you, no doubt, have hear of the mad squire. mad! poor fool, my

madness be their sanity. I shall be young and vigorous in my next

body, and I shall be rich. all this life I have be work for the

next, but I have not gain enough money. no, not half enough. make I

well again, that I can complete my work, then I will gladly leave this

wear-out body for a new one. I will pay you--oh yes--I will pay you."



he fall back exhausted on the pillow, wear out by the rapidity of his

speech, and Nestley call out loudly for assistance. Patience Allerby

enter the room, and, by the doctor's order bring some wine in a

glass. this Nestley hold to the sick man's lip, while the

housekeeper, at the other side of the bed, hold the candle for he to

see by. the wine infuse a fictitious life into the old man, and

see he be easy, Nestley determine to go back to Garsworth in

order to get some medicine.



he put the clothe over the squire and bend down to speak.



"you must lie quiet," he say, in a slow voice, "and take some wine

whenever you feel exhausted. I will send you a sedative to-night, and

to-morrow morning will call and see you."



the sick man, too exhausted to speak, make a motion with his hand to

show he understand, and lie back white and still, in complete contrast

preparatory to burial. the thought be a horrible one, but the

atmosphere of the house seem to engender horrible thought, so he

hurry to the door, anxious to leave this nightmare castle.



Patience Allerby, soft-footed and silent, light he downstairs, and

having see he safe in the hall turn back without a word.



"a strange woman," think Nestley, look after she, "and a strange

house;" then he turn to Una and Miss Cassey, who be anxiously

wait his report.



"I have give he a little wine," he say, put on his glove.

"keep he as quiet as possible and I'll send some opiate from

Garsworth; he be in a very exhausted condition and must be keep quiet.

how can I send the medicine?



"Munks will bring it when he drive you in," say Una quickly. "you

will come again?"



"yes, to-morrow morning," he reply as she open the door, and be

about to depart when Miss Cassey arrest he.



"I'll take some of the medicine myself, doctor," she say. "I'm so

easily upset--nerve again--it's in the family; come and prescribe

for I to-morrow--I'm so odd, I think it's the house--lonely, you

know--bromide be good, ben't it? yes, Doctor Pecks, in London, tell I

so. do you know him?--No--how odd--clever on nerve--my nerve--don't

forget to-morrow--good-night--charming moon--yes--so odd."



after hear this incoherent speech, Dr. Nestley manage to get away,

and say good-night to Una, go down the step. the dog-cart be

wait for he, and Munks, the Mute, drive he back grimly the whole

way. it be quite a relief get into the cool fresh air, and

Nestley half think the lonely house and its fantastic occupant be

phantom, so unreal do they seem.









CHAPTER V.

the FAMILY CIRCLE.





     but turn from these scene of beauty rare

     the family circle next demand our care,

     that fireside kingdom where the father bland

     his sceptre sway with firm and gentle hand.

     obedient child clust'ring round his knee

     perform with pleasure all his mild decree,

     with willing heart upon his order wait

     thus show example to the parent state.





Dr. Larcher, the vicar of Garsworth, be a fine type of what be call

muscular christianity. tall, broad-shoulder and burly, he look

more like a cavalry officer than a parson, and he preach his

sermon, which be generally plain and outspoken, in a loud assertive

tone of voice. be fond of archaeology and long walk he know every

inch of the country for mile round, and be as well acquainted with

the poor cottager as with the lord of the soil. simple,

large-minded gentleman that he be, he admirably suit his position

in life, and if the rustic of Garsworth have not a sound belief in the

tenet of the Church of England, it be by no mean the fault of the

worthy vicar, who thunder out practical Christianity in ponderous

johnsonian sentence, with the zeal of a Savonarola and the eloquence

of a Bossuet. he be also a great latinist and plentifully season

his discourse with quotation from Horace, for which bard he profess

great admiration.



on the morning after the visit of Nestley to the Grange, Dr. Larcher

be seat at the breakfast-table talk eagerly about a bronze sword

which have just be bring to he, having be disinter from some

ancient british tumulus. his present congregation consist of Dick

Pemberton, who be rather disposed to laugh at the important

discovery, Reginald Blake, look somewhat preoccupy, Ferdinand

Priggs, the poet, a sallow youth with dreamy eye and a deep voice,

and Miss Eleonora Gwendoline Vera Bianca Larcher, the sole child of

the vicar and his wife.



these name, decidedly alarming one, have be give to she by Mrs.

Larcher, who have select they from the "Family Herald," her favourite

journal, but Dr. Larcher, who have no fancy for high-sound title,

call his daughter Pumpkin. this unhappy cognoman have be bestow

on the child by the nurse, in despair at be unable to master the

legitimate name, and the vicar be so pleased with the oddity of the

title that he there and then adopt it. Mrs. Larcher, however,

obstinately refuse to accept this innovation, and call her daughter

Eleonora Gwendoline, but generally Miss Larcher answer to the name

of Pumpkin, her aristocratic name be only bring out on company

occasion.



she be a pretty, plump girl, with dark eye and a rosy face. endow

with a large amount of common sense, her taste lie in the direction

of make pudding and mend clothe, whilst she evince a great

contempt for poetry and such-like thing. Mrs. Larcher, be an

invalid, leave the management of the house entirely to Pumpkin, who

rule the servant with a rod of iron, look after the creature

comfort of her father and his pupil, and be besides a bright,

lively girl whom everyone adore.



as to Mrs. Larcher, she be always ill, but why she should be so be a

mystery to everyone save herself. it be either her nerve or her

liver or her spine or her laziness, most probably the latter, as she

mostly pass her life alternate between the sofa and her bed.

occasionally she stroll out, but always come back feeling weak and

bad, to be strengthen with strong tea and hot muffin, after which

she would bewail her delicate constitution in a subdue whimper. her

unknown malady be know to all as "the affliction," that be a

generic name for all kind of disease, and Mrs. Larcher herself

allude to her ill health by this title as be a happy one and

necessitate no special mention of any one infirmity. Pumpkin look

after everything and be the good fairy of the vicarage, while Mrs.

Larcher lie all day on her sofa read novel and drink tea, or

gossip with any visitor who might drop in.



at present Mrs. Larcher be safe in bed upstairs and Pumpkin preside

at the breakfast-table, which be now cover with an array of empty

dish, as the male portion of the vicarage inmate, with the

exception of the poet, have large appetite. Dr. Larcher, however, have

be too excited to eat much, and have his eye intently fix upon his

newly-discover bronze implement.



"it's a wonderful example of what the ancient Britons could do," he

say grandiloquently, "and to my mind, I prove no mean standard of

civilisation."



"even in that age of barbarism," observe the poet enthusiastically,

"they cultivate a love for the beautiful."



"oh, bosh," say Dick irreverently, "they want something to knock

the stuffing out of an enemy."



"well, I think that sword could do it," remark Pumpkin with a smile.

"suppose we try it on you, Dick."



"no, thank," retort that young gentleman, grimacing, "I'll agree

without practical proof."



"I shall write an article on this," say Dr. Larcher, delicately

balance the sword in his hand. "such a discovery will be a distinct

gain to our knowledge of the aborigine of that dead and bury time

of so long ago--_Eheu fugace Postume labuntur anni_."



"it breathe the very spirit of the age," cry Ferdinand with an

inspire air:





     the age of Bronze, the age of Bronze

     where Boadicea----





"Loved and sing," finish Dick. "I say old chap, you're crib from

the Isles of Greece."



Whereupon Ferdinand enter into a lively discussion with Dick to

prove that he have not plagiarise from Byron while Dick in reply

mercilessly chaff the unhappy poet with such success that he flee

from the room, pursue by his laughing antagonist.



"what be the matter, Reggy?" ask Pumpkin, see how quiet Blake have

remain, "anything wrong?"



"oh no," he reply hastily, "but I be wonder how the squire be

this morning."



"you'd well go over and see, Blake," say the vicar, look up. "I

"I don't know, sir," answer Blake, turn towards Dr. Larcher, "he

say he be on a walking tour, and I fancy be a friend of Beaumont's."



the vicar frown.



"bird of a feather," he say decisively. "I don't think much of

Beaumont, Blake, and if this Dr. Nestley be his friend, I'm afraid

he's not much good."



"that be severe, papa," say Pumpkin.



"my dear," reply her father emphatically. "I hope I be the last man

in the world to speak ill of my fellow creature, but I be afraid that

"but Dr. Nestley, papa?"



"as to Doctor Nestley," say the vicar majestically, "I do not yet

"I don't think they be very intimate friend," say Reggy

"ah, indeed," reply Dr. Larcher, "well, well, we shall see;

however--_non hæc jocosæ conveniunt lyræ_--you can go over to the

Grange, Blake, and inquire after the squire's health."



at this moment a tapping be hear on the floor above which signify

"by the way, Blake," he say gravely, "I wish to speak to you on a

Reggy flush red and bow without say a word, as he intuitively

guess what be come.



"I be aware," observe the vicar in his ponderous manner, "that I may

"not at all, sir," answer Reginald warmly, "no one have such a right

to speak to I as you have--my second father--I may say my only

Dr. Larcher smile in a gratified manner and look at the tall young

"I be glad to have your good opinion," he say, politely bend his

be a very critical period of your career--remember Horace, _Tu nisi

ventis debis ludibrium cave_."



Blake turn pale, then, with a forced smile resume his seat and

"of course you understand," he say clear his throat, "that I be

quite unaware of your parentage--whether your father and mother be

London, where she have be in service. she tell I that you be the

they go away to France and--I understand--die there. she be leave

with you on her hand so bring you down here and deliver you to my

charge; since then you have be an inmate of my house."



"the only home I ever know," interpose Blake with emotion.



"I will not deny," say Dr. Larcher, "that I have receive through

your nurse certain sum of money for your education which lead I to

believe--in spite of her denial--that your parent may be still alive.

this be well enough in the past, but now you be twenty-two year of

age and I wish to make some arrangement about your future career--you

will of course choose your own vocation in life--but meantime I wish

you to ask Patience Allerby about your birth and obtain from her all

information regard your parent which may be of use to I--you can

do so when you go over to the Grange to-day--and then let I know the

result; afterwards we can discuss way and mean regard your

future."



"it's very kind of you, sir to talk like this," say Blake in a low

voice, "and I feel deeply grateful to you. I will see Patience and get

she to tell I all she know, but I'm afraid I can expect nothing from

my parent, even though they be alive--a father and mother who could

leave their child to the mercy of stranger all these year cannot

have much humanity."



"do not judge they too harshly," say the vicar hastily, "there may be

reason."



"I've no doubt of that," reply Blake bitterly, "reason which mean

shame."



"not necessarily--a secret marriage----"



"would have be declare long before the lapse of twenty year," say

Reggy quickly. "I'm afraid there be bad than that and my birth be

my mother's shame."



there be a cloud on the good vicar's brow as the young man speak, but

he delicately refrain from say anything. go over to Blake he

pat he gently on the shoulder, a mark of kindliness which touch

the young man deeply.



"Come! come, Blake," he say cheerfully, "you must not cherish these

morbid fancy. you be young and clever, with the world before you,

who know but what you may achieve success, and then your unknown

parent, if they live, will acknowledge you only too gladly. do not be

so easily cast down. what be the manly advice of the Venusinian?





     'Rebus angustis animosus atque

      Fortis appare.'"





"I don't think Horace be ever call upon to bear trouble undaunted,"

say Blake rather sadly, "but if my belief be true it will cast a

shadow on my life."



"Morbid! morbid!" reply the vicar gaily, "do not go out in a coach

and four to meet your trouble, my lad--see Patience first--if your

thought prove true there will be time enough to lament they, but with

youth and brain on your side you should not turn recreant in the

battle of life."



"nor will I," say Reggy, grasp the kind hand hold out towards he.

"whatever come or go I have at least one man who have be to I

father and mother both."



then, overcome by his emotion, he hastily leave the room, while the

"ah!" say the worthy gentleman with a sigh. "I trust his foreboding

may not prove true, but Patience Allerby know more than she tell,

erit_, and the boy have at least have a few happy year--what say

glorious John?





     'not heaven itself over the past hath power

      for what hath be hath be,

      and I have have my hour.'"





and with this somewhat pagan sentiment Dr. Larcher go away to

discuss the Bronze period, illustrate by the newly-find sword, with

a certain old crony who always differ from he and constantly say

"no," to the vicar's "yes."









CHAPTER VI.

a MORNING WALK.





     a snake you be in other day

       Ere you attain the human state;

     still in your vein the snake blood play

       which lead you now to gloze and hate,

     the magic of the serpent gaze

       Lurks in your eye to fascinate.





as it be a holiday the pupil be leave to their own device, and on

go outside, Blake find Dick Pemberton amuse himself with muffin

and a fishing rod. Ferdinand having be worst by the volatile Dick,

"now what do you think you be do?" ask Reggy pause at the

door.



"none so blind as those who willn't see," retort Dick coolly. "I'm

goin' fishin'."



"fishing?" repeat Reggy with emphasis.



"with the accent on the 'g'," reply Richard gaily. "don't be a

pedant, old chap--fishin' mean the same thing as fishing, and not so

much trouble to say. I suppose I ought to call Muffins 'muffing.'"



"oh, bosh!" retort Reggy politely, walk down to the gate.



"quite right--it be bosh, oh king. where be you off to?"



"Grange?"



Dick arch his eyebrow, shake his head, and whistle, at which

Reginald flush a little.



"what do you mean?" he ask, turn round.



"Nothin', nothin'," say Dick demurely; "you're 'goin' a-courtin',

sir, she say,' I suppose."



"what nonsense, Dick," say Blake angrily, "as if Una----"



"oh! ho!" reply Pemberton; "sit the wind in that quarter? I never

mention the lady's name. you ought to get our one and only poet to

write you some verses--



     'oh, I could spoon a

      Girl like dear Una

      Aileen Aroona,'



--bad poetry, but beautiful sentiment."



"I wish you'd be serious, Dick," say Reginald in a vex tone; "I be

only go over to the Grange to ask after the squire's health."



"all right," reply Dick good-naturedly; "give old Cassy my love, and

tell she I'm go to propose to she--odd, ben't it?--so very odd."

and with a capital imitation of Miss Cassandra's fidgety manner, he

walk away follow by muffin, while Reginald go out of the gate

on to the village street.



the interview with Dr. Larcher have touch he more nearly than he

like to confess even to himself, and his frivolous conversation with

Dick have be somewhat of a relief to he, but now, be alone, he

relapse into sombre thought. he be dissatisfied with his position,

and long to know more about himself--who be his parents?--were

they dead or alive?--why be he thrust into the world as an outcast?

the only person who could explain the mystery of his life be Patience

Allerby; he determine therefore to apply to she for the explanation.



fill with these dismal thought, he saunter slowly up the street

as far as the bridge. here he pause, and lean over the parapet,

begin to think again. it be a curious thing that this young man,

bring up in a quiet, christian household, should let his thought

run on such a morbid idea as the possibility of his be a natural

son. he have no experience of vice, and should therefore have accept

the marriage of his unknown parent as a fact, especially when his

nurse assert that they have be marry. but the strangeness of his

position lead he to believe that there must be some motive for

concealment, and this motive, he determine in his own mind, be the

want of a marriage certificate.



the real cause, however, which lead to this morbid analysis of the

possible relation between his parent, lie in a discovery which he

have lately make--a discovery which change the simple manly life he

be lead into a rage hell of doubt and self-torturing.



he be in love--and Una Challoner be the woman he love. it be not

that sickly evanescent affection common to adolescence, know by the

name of calf love--no; but that strong overwhelming passion of the

soul which have no limit and which dominate and sway the whole

nature. draw in the first place towards Una by simple admiration of

her beauty, he learn later on to discard this passion without soul,

and find in the kindre sympathy of her spirit with his own that

ideal union which so rarely exist. she, on her part, have be

attract to he by the same quality which he find in she, and this

perfect agreement develop in each a pure and spiritual adoration.



his love thus be pure, he would not dare to offer she anything but

purity, and anxiously begin to examine his life in order to discover

all flaw which mar its whiteness. he be not an ideal young man,

still he discover nothing in his life which could embarrass he to

explain, so feel quite easy in himself, but now this shadow of

possible illegitimacy seem to threaten disaster. he would not dare

to offer to the woman he love and respect a name which be not

legally his own.



however, it be no use indulge in self-torture when it could be

end by get a proper explanation of the circumstance of his

birth from Patience Allerby. Hitherto he have shrink from do this

with the vague hesitation of a man who dread to hear the truth, but

now it be imperative he should learn all, be it good or evil, and

shape his course accordingly. at this moment of his life he stand at

the junction of two road, and the explanation of Patience Allerby

would decide which one he be to take. having come to this logical

conclusion, he resolutely banish all dismal thought from his heart,

and walk rapidly across the common in the direction of Garsworth

Grange. it be the quest, not for El Dorado or the Holy Grail, but for

the secret which would make or mar his whole life.



Dull and heavy be the day, with a cold grey sky overhead, a humid

wind blow chill with the moisture of the fen, and a sense of decay

in the atmosphere. the gaunt, bare tree with their slender branch

and twig outline with delicate distinctness against the sad grey

sky--the withered leave with their vivid red and yellow which

carpet the ground--the absence of song of bird or cheerful lowing of

kine--all weigh down and depress his spirit. the uniform tint of

the landscape with their absence of colour and life seem like a type

of his own existence at present; but lo, when he raise his eye a

golden shaft of sunlight be above the distant tower of the Grange,

where he hope to find the talisman which would change the grey

monotony of an uneventful past to the glory and joy of a happy future.

it be an omen of success, and his eye brighten, his step grow

springy and he clutch his stick with determination as he stride

towards the glory of the sun, leave the grey mist and desolate

landscape behind he.



as he walk on he see a short distance ahead the tall figure of a

man, and on come abreast of he, he recognise Basil Beaumont, who

be listlessly stroll along, think deeply. remember the

vicar's dislike to the character of Beaumont, he be about to pass on

with a conventional nod, when the artist speak, and he could not with

courtesy refuse to answer.



"good morning, Blake," he say in a friendly tone. "take a

constitutional?"



"not exactly," reply Reginald, fall into the leisurely walk of

the artist; "the vicar want to know how Squire Garsworth be?"



"have I meet you early I could have save you the walk," say Beaumont

indolently; "he be much well--they send to Nestley this morning to

tell he about it."



"where be Dr. Nestley now?" ask Blake.



Beaumont point to the Grange with his stick.



"over there," he answer, "see his patient. I expect he'll have to

remain down here for some time--the Squire have take a great fancy to

he--rich man's liking be poor man's fortune."



"good. I wish someone would take a liking to I," say Blake with a

sigh. "I need a fortune."



"you've get one."



"indeed! where?"



"in your throat!"



Reginald laugh and shake his head.



"I hardly think that," he answer gaily.



"don't be so mock modest, my dear boy," say Beaumont with a shrug. "I

assure you I'm not one to praise unnecessarily. you need training,

severe training, to bring your voice to perfection; but you've get a

wonderful organ to work on--not that voice be everything, mind you;

I've know people with good voice to whom such a gift be absolutely

worthless."



"why?"



"because they've get no talent. to make a singer need more than

voice--it need great perseverance, powerful dramatic instinct, an

educate mind, and a strong individuality."



"I don't think I've get all that," say Reggy rather disconsolately.



"let I see," observe Beaumont deliberately, "you've a good voice and

dramatic instinct, as I know from the way you sing that song last

night--you be educate, of course, and I can see for myself you have

an individuality of your own--there only remain perseverance. have

you perseverance?"



"I think so."



"ah! doubtful. I'll put the question in another way. be you

ambitious? if you be, you must have perseverance--one be the natural

outcome of the other."



"how so?"



"logically in this way--an ambitious man want to succeed--he cann't

succeed without perseverance--ergo, he persevere to succeed in his

ambition. now then, be you persevere or ambitious?"



"I'm not sure."



"no!" Beaumont do not seem disappointed at this reply, but go on

talk. "then you have no incentive; you be in the chrysalis stage;

get an incentive, and you will change to a butterfly."



"what incentive can I obtain."



"that depend upon your temperament--the desire to leave the dull

village--the desire to have money, and above all, the desire to be

love by some woman."



"ah," say Blake, whom this last remark sting sharply, "at least I

have that incentive."



Beaumont laugh.



"then the result must follow, you will persevere and succeed."



Blake be much impressed with Beaumont's remark, for a vision rise

before he of a bright future and a famous name with Una for his wife.

then the recollection of the dark secret of his birth come back to

he; if what he surmise be true, he would have nothing to work for

as there would be an insuperable bar between he and the girl he

love. the roseate scene he have conjure up vanish, and in their

place he only see the sorrow of a lonely life. he sigh

involuntarily, and shake his head.



"it all depend on one thing," he say sadly.



"and that one thing?" ask Beaumont keenly.



"it be at present a secret," reply Blake curtly, whereupon Beaumont

laugh lightly in no wise offend, and they walk on for a short

distance in silence.



they be now near the Grange, and Beaumont be go to turn back

when he see Nestley come down the road.



"here be Nestley," he say carelessly, "so you can learn all about the

squire from he, and need not go to the Grange."



"I must go to the Grange," reply Blake.



Beaumont smile and whistle the air of "Love's Young Dream," for he

have hear rumour in the village which lead he to believe that Blake

be in love with the Squire's beautiful cousin.



Reginald understand he, and be about to make some angry remark, when

Nestley come up to they and put an end to the conversation.



"well, doctor," say Beaumont lightly, "and how be your patient?"



Nestley's face wear a frown as he recognise Beaumont, but he

evidently determine not to give his enemy the pleasure of see his

annoyance, so, smooth his feature to a bland smile, he reply in

the same conversational manner:



"well--much well--he'll be all right soon--less excitable--but the

body be wear out."



"and the brain?" ask the artist.



"oh, that's all right--he's get a wonderful brain."



"slightly crack," interpose Blake, nod to Nestley.



"just slightly," reply Nestley, coolly. "but his madness have a good

deal of method in it. he's get queer idea about the re-incarnation of

the soul--but we've all queer idea more or less."



"particularly more," observe Beaumont, indolently. "be you come

back, Nestley? I'll be glad of a companion."



Nestley hesitate. he do not like Beaumont, and mistrust he.

still, there be a wonderful fascination about the man which few could

resist, and in spite of his dislike Nestley rapidly find himself

fall once more under the old spell of that suave, cynical manner.



"I don't mind," he say, carelessly, "particularly as I want to give

you a message from the squire."



"to I?" say Beaumont in surprise. "what about?"



"a picture. the squire want his portrait take, and----"



"you think of I," say Beaumont, with a cold smile; "how charming

you be, my dear Nestley. I'll be delighted to paint the squire, he's

a rembrandtian study, full of light and shade and wrinkle."



"where be you go to, Mr. Blake?" ask Nestley, abruptly turn

to the young man and eye he keenly.



"to the Grange," reply Blake carelessly, "to see the squire. good

morning, gentleman," and with a cool nod, the young man stroll away

in the direction of Garsworth Grange.



Nestley stand look after he oddly.



"to see the squire," he repeat. "yes and Una Challoner."



"ah," say Beaumont cynically. "you've see that, my dear fellow."



"yes. do you know Una Challoner love he?"



"not exactly. I know he love Una Challoner."



"she return it," say Nestley gloomily. "I find that out from her

manner this morning."



Beaumont smile and look strangely at the downcast face of the

doctor.



"I understand," he say, light a fresh cigarette.



"understand what?" ask Nestley angrily.



"that you also love Una Challoner."



"Absurd, I've only see she twice."



"Nevertheless----"



"what?"



"oh nothing, nothing," reply Beaumont airily. "I'll tell you all

about it in a week."



Nestley do not reply, but stand silently look at the ground, on

see which, Beaumont draw his arm within his own, with a gay laugh.



"come along," he say cheerfully, "we'll walk back to Garsworth, and

you can tell I all about the squire and his picture."









CHAPTER VII.

the housekeeper.





     like a lone mountain white with virgin snow,

       which hold within its breast eternal fire

     this woman cold and pale with face of woe

       yet feel at heart an unappeased desire.





Reginald Blake walk briskly up the avenue. it have an excessively

dreary appearance, for the black look tree with their angular

branch seem starve and attenuate while the leave underfoot be

sodden with rain. the marble statue which be stand here and

there, wear a disconsolate look, as if they long for the sunny sky

of their native Italy, and mutely protest against this misty climate

which discolour and mar their beauty.



when he arrive at the terrace, the long white façade of the house

seem grim and uninviting. no smoke ascend from the slender

chimney, no face appear at the bare stare window, and the

terrace, which should have be throng with gay company, be silent

and desert, chill the very soul with its mute sense of

desolation.



the young man ring the bell in the monstrous porch, and before the

harsh jangling have cease to echo through the dreary house, the door

be open by Jellicks. on recognise Blake, she wriggle a welcome

and admit he into the vault-like hall which still retain the

musty smell observe by Nestley. outside the grey sky, inside the grey

twilight, it seem as though the sun have not warm this dismal place

with his cheerful beam for century.



"I want to see Miss Challoner," say Reginald, when the heavy door be

once more closed, "be she at home?"



Jellicks reply that she be, in a serpent-like hiss, and then, still

more like a serpent, she wriggle along the dark corridor on the

atmosphere of decay.



at length she stop midway in the passage and on knock at a door

be bid by a thin voice, seemingly that of Miss Cassy, to enter.



Reginald do so, and jellick having twist herself apologetically
