dreary landscape.



Una, flush with life and beauty, seem somewhat out of place in

this charnel house though, truth to tell, the room have a more homelike

appearance than the rest of the Grange. not very large, panel

with carve oak, dark and solemn-look, it be hang round with

picture in tarnish gilt frame, the floor be cover with a

comfortable-look carpet of reddish tint. in the huge fireplace

burn a goodly fire, which somewhat warm the chill atmosphere. the

furniture be quaint and old-fashioned, of all date, range from

heavy oak table of Tudor day to spindle-legged chippendale chair

and curiously inlay cabinet of more modern construction. there be

the Garsworth family. a quaint room of ancient and incongruous

appearance, yet have withal a quiet beauty of its own, a tone of

intense restfulness, which be not without charm.



"good morning, Miss Challoner," say Reginald politely, mindful of the

presence of Miss Cassy. "I have call by the desire of Dr. Larcher to

see how the squire be."



"oh, well, much well," interpose Miss Cassy before Una could

speak. "I say it be nerve all along--so very odd--quite excitable

he be, but the dear doctor's medicine you know--so soothing, really

very soothing--I don't know what the dear squire will do without the

"he's not go to do without he, aunt," say Una with a smile; "my

cousin be afraid of get ill again, so have ask Dr. Nestley to

"what about his own practice?" ask Reginald.



"oh, he say that will be all right, as he have leave it in charge of

his partner. have you meet Dr. Nestley?"



"yes, at the gate; he have go back to Garsworth with Mr. Beaumont."



"Beaumont," say Miss Cassy with vivacity, "that be the painter, very

odd, ben't it? he's go to paint the dear squire's picture--how

"why do the squire want his picture paint?" inquire Blake.



Una laugh.



"not for his beauty, at all event," she say mischievously, "but, you

know, there be only one picture of he in the gallery--as a young man.

I presume this will be for the sake of contrast. do you know Mr.

"slightly. he's a stranger here," reply Blake, a little coldly. "I

"he look wicked," say Miss Cassy, nod her head sagely; "worn,

you know--oh, shocking!--but very handsome--just the kind of man I

"oh, aunt!" say Una, slightly shocked.



"well, I would, Una. you know I should like to have be marry--I'm

sure I don't know why I haven't be marry," say the poor lady,

pathetically. "I'm sure anyone can see I'm not make for a

spinster--it's so odd, ben't it?"



Blake, be directly appeal to, suppress a smile, and, and

assent politely; whereupon Miss Cassy resume:



"it's so hard for an unmarried girl to know when to leave off be a

girl--I'm sure I don't know--ivy, you know, I feel like it; I'm make

to cling to a manly oak--no, I mean an oakly man--no! not that--mix,

you see! I mean a man like an oak--yes, that's it, and then I might

have have twelve stalwart son--all oak! Odd, ben't it?--most

peculiar."



"my dear aunt, what curious thing you say!" say Una, look

reproachfully at Reggy, who be try to smother his laughter.



"yes, I know, dear," reply Miss Cassy, complacently, "we're all

odd--nerve--quite chronic; anyone can see that it come of be an

ivy--I mean a woman--so very nice--yes, I always say so--don't you,

Mr. Blake?"



Reginald could not exactly say he do, as he be in doubt as to what

Miss Cassy mean, but make some confused answer, and then ask to see

Patience Allerby.



"she's in the housekeeper's room, I think," say Una. "auntie will

take you there, and when you be do with she I'll go to Garsworth

with you."



"will you, really? I'm so glad!" say Reginald, eagerly.



"I want to see Cecilia in the church," reply Miss Challoner, "about

the concert."



"what concert?"



"don't you know? oh, we're go to have a concert in the school-room

shortly. you be to be ask to sing."



"delighted."



"Cecilia will play a piece--she don't like the piano as much as the

organ, but we can hardly get that out of the church."



"I'm go to sing also," say Miss Cassy, shake her curl, "so

nice--quite operatic. I'll sing a duet with you, Mr. Blake, if you

like."



Blake hastily excuse himself, as he have great dread of Miss Cassy's

vocal performance, which be, to say the least, somewhat screechy.

the lady accept his apology graciously, and then lead he out of the

room to find the housekeeper, leave Una to get ready for the walk.



Miss Cassy, be delighted to have a charming young man for audience,

chatter all the way in a disconnected fashion.



"so damp, ben't it--quite chilly. I never do like the weather. very

watery--rheumatic, you know. I mean the weather, of course--not

myself! I think Patience be in her room--so kind of you to see

your old nurse--quite delightful! light of what's his name, you

know--Moore--exactly; irish melody--so pretty! this be the door. oh,

Patience--you be in--so glad--here be Mr. Blake to see you! the

squire's easy--yes, nerve, of course--I know it. I'll go back to

Una, Mr. Blake, and see you later on--very pleased, indeed--quite a

treat to see a male. sound like the post--very odd, ben't it?--yes!"



and Miss Cassy, close the door after she, depart leave Reginald

alone with his old nurse.



the tall, placid woman, stand near the fireplace, make a step

forward, as if to embrace Reginald, but restrain herself, as though

doubtful how to proceed. Blake, however, cross over to she and

kiss she affectionately, which seem to awaken a feeling of emotion

know what it be to love or be love; but every now and then a flash

hide beneath that calm exterior. the young man's kiss seem to warm

disappear under the magic of Reginald Blake's presence. he, dark and

handsome, lean against the mantelpiece, look down at she

"I be so glad to see you, Master Reginald," she say, the hard voice

in which she habitually speak become soft and tender. "I have not

see you for a long time."



"a whole week, Patience, that's all," he reply, carelessly. "you see

I'm busy with my study."



"that's right, dear!" she say, eagerly. "work--work hard, and make a

name in the world.



"for whose sake?" he ask, a little bitterly.



"for mine!"



there be a world of tenderness in the way in which she speak the

word, and her eye seem to devour he as she gaze. he move

of his visit.



"why not for my parent' sake?"



the woman's face lose its look of tenderness, and become hard and

"who have be speak to you about they," she ask fiercely.



"Doctor Larcher."



"and the reason?"



"simply this: I be two-and-twenty year of age, so it's time I have

some aim in life. before I do this I want to know all about my

parentage. be my parent alive or dead?--who be they?--why be I

place in your charge?--can they, or their relation, assist I to get

on in the world? I cannot move until I know who and what I be."



he speak vehemently, and as he do so the woman seem to shrink back

into her chair with a nameless dread in her eye. there be no sound

"your parent be my master and mistress," she say at length, in a

low, harsh voice, "marry against their parent' wish."



"they be marry, then?"



"who say they be not?" she demand, fiercely.



"no one. but the mystery of my birth lead I to think there might

be----"



"Shame!" she interrupt, vehemently. "you be wrong. there be no

shame--they keep the marriage secret, for if know they would have

lose their property. when you be bear, they go over to France for

the sake of your father's health, leave you in my charge. I be to

keep you till they could acknowledge you as their son; but before they

could do so they die."



"die!"



"yes. your father die of phthisis at canne six month after he leave

England, and your mother very soon follow he to the grave."



"she die of what?"



"a broken heart," reply Patience, in a low voice, "a broken heart,

poor soul."



"Good God!"



"I hear of it shortly afterwards," she go on, rapidly, "and as your

birth have never be acknowledge I determine to bring you up without

let anyone know the truth. after stay some time in London, I

bring you to Doctor Larcher, and he have have charge of you since."



"why do you come here?"



"because it be my native place. I only intend to stay for a time,

and then return to service in London, but Squire Garsworth want a

housekeeper, so I take the situation in order to remain near you."



"why do you not tell I this before."



"there be no need to," she answer, coldly, "and even now it be

useless. your parent be dead, and the property have go to distant

heir."



"but I be the heir."



she shake her head.



"no, the property be not entail--it be leave by will, and you have

no claim on the present holder."



"who be my father?"



"he have be in the army, but sell out when he marry, and become a

writer."



"what be his name?"



"Reginald Blake--the same as your own."



"it's my real name, then?"



she look at he in surprise.



"of course! why should you not take your father's name? there be no

reason."



"so I be alone in the world?"



"yes, except for I."



he step over to she, and place his arm caressingly on her

shoulder.



"ah, you have be a mother to I," he say quickly, "and I shall

never forget it. no one could have act with more kindness and

fidelity."



Patience wince and shrink away from his caress while he walk up and

down the room, talk cheerfully.



"now my mind be at rest," he say, with a sigh of relief. "I think

the mystery of my birth involve some stain, but since I have the

right to bear my father's name, why! I feel quite happy. I can make my

way in the world by myself, can ask the girl I love to be my wife."



"the girl you love," she repeat jealously.



"yes, I will tell you her name, though no one else know it--Una."



"Miss Challoner," say the woman, start up; "impossible!"



"why impossible?" he retort gaily. "you think I be not rich enough.

never mind; I carry a fortune in my throat, and will soon be able to

"I hope so," she say fervently. "may God's blessing rest on your

effort. yes, marry Una Challoner if she love you, and make your own

way without troubling about the dead."



"I never know my parent," say Reginald, sighing, "so I can hardly

forward to the future. I have nothing to offer she now but a stainless

I must get back to Garsworth."



"good-bye," she say, kiss he eagerly. "come again soon, my dear

boy; and although Una Challoner love you, do not forget your old

nurse."



"of course not," he reply gaily, and walk away hum an air.

Patience Allerby wait till the door be close and the sound of his

voice have die away, then fall on her knee, beat her breast with

"God! God!" she cry, amid convulsive sob, "pardon my sin. it be

for his sake, for his dear sake, not for my own. let the dead past be

forget. let he never know anything but what I have tell he, and

bless he, oh God, in his future life."



there be a crucifix of black ebony against the wall, and from it,

with pity eye, look down the face of the Lord at the stricken

woman kneel before he. the ineffable sorrow of the sacred face

seem to calm her spirit, for she cease to weep and her lip move

in a prayer which seem to come from her heart.



"_forgive we our trespass, as we forgive they that trespass against

us_."









CHAPTER VIII.

the BLIND organist.





   "naught have I see of the earth, for mine eye have be darken

    since I be bear to this life, with its toil and vexation,

    yet hath the Maker, in mercy, bestow compensation,

    Music, and love of sweet singing to lighten the burden.

    here, at the loud-swell organ, my soul be responsive

    to passion and grandeur of music, and sighing melodious,

    it burst from its prison of gloom, soar upward rejoicing,

    Borne on the stormy, majestical breath of the organ."





as a rule, the conversation of lover be hardly worth recording,

consist, as they mostly do, of incoherent rhapsody of love and

devotion, with very little of that useful quality call common sense.

but Reginald and Una be the most sedate of sweetheart, and talk

of other thing besides the ardour of their passion. in this instance

they be discuss their future and the chance of their marriage.



it would have be difficult to find a handsomer pair as they walk

along; she fair and slender, with a charming smile on her face; he

tall and dark, with a touch of haughtiness in his manly dignity. they

look like two lover who have stray from the enchant garden of

Boccaccio, with nothing to talk about but the pain and passion of

Eros, but, alas, such thought be impossible, save under the magic

influence of twilight; and this youthful pair, who seem the

incarnation of romance, be talk in a most prosaic fashion.



"you see, dear," say Reginald, after he have explain everything to

Una, "it be not the slight use my depending on my relation, even

if I be to find they out."



"I don't think it's much use in any case," reply Una decisively.

"it's far well for you to depend upon yourself. but how do you

intend to proceed?"



"it's rather difficult to say. I have no money and no chance of

obtain any. Patience have a certain sum which she pay to Doctor

Larcher for my education. I believe," say the young man, somewhat

charity."



"Doctor Larcher have never say so."



"no, he be too kind-hearted and generous for that, but I feel sure

that such be the case. never mind; should it ever lie in my power I

will repay his charity a thousand fold."



"do you think he will like you become a singer?" ask Una

apprehensively.



"I don't fancy he'll approve of it--at first," say Reginald bluntly,

"but what else can I do? the law, the church and medicine all require

money to make a start, and even then it be a difficult game to play. I

know a good deal about music, and, accord to Beaumont, who be

certainly no flatterer, I have an excellent voice. so it be my only

chance."



"if the vicar approve, what will you do?"



"I'll ask he to lend I some money. I shall then go to London and

place myself under a good master, and if my voice be good, with hard

work I'll soon be able to do something."



"it seem very risky," say Una, with a sigh. "many fail."



"and many succeed. if a man be sober, industrious and observant, he

can hardly help succeed. beggar must not be chooser, and if I

don't use the only talent I've get, what else be there for I to do? I

cannot remain here all my life on the bounty of Dr. Larcher. if I do,

there would be small chance of our marriage."



"I have a little money," she begin timidly.



"yes, I know," he answer hastily, "but I'm not the man to live on my

wife. it be your dear self I want, not your money; though, as the

squire's heiress, you be far above I."



Una laugh.



"I'm very doubtful about my be an heiress," she say gaily. "it be

true I be the squire's next-of-kin, and should inherit, but you know

how eccentric he be. the property be not entail, so he can do as he

like."



"you mean he be go to leave it to his other self. nonsense! that be

the phantasy of a madman's brain. no court of law would uphold such a

will. how he be go to leave it to himself when his _alter ego_ be

not in existence, I don't know."



"nor I," reply Una frankly. "I know, of course, he be mad, quite

mad, and that any will make on the principle of his hallucination

"a son? why he be never marry."



"no; but he say he have a son who be somewhere about, and he intend

to leave the property to he."



"indeed. then what become of his great scheme of enjoy the money

in his re-incarnated body?"



"it's a mystery," say Miss Challoner, laugh.



"I should think it be, and whatever will he make now, leave the

property away from you, would not hold good, for he certainly be not

in his right sense. you could claim as next-of-kin."



"and I certainly should do so," reply Una, with decision. "but it be

my opinion he'll live for a good long time yet."



"Humph! he's very ill."



"creak door hang long. but do not let we speculate on his

death. I would rather we make our own fortune."



the use of the plural member have a delicious sound for Reginald, and

he felt strongly tempt to there and then kiss his lady-love, but as

they be now cross the bridge and several people be about, he

restrain himself until a more convenient season.



"never mind the squire and his money, dear," he say fondly, "for your

"I'm sure you will," reply Una with the trustfulness of love, "you

know I live a long time in Germany and hear a number of good

singer--your voice be quite as good as any, if not well."



"Flatterer!"



"well, we'll see, Signor Reginald Mario," she say gaily, as they

enter the churchyard, "when you be enchant London audience you

with silver."



"cann't, mum," he retort laugh; "I'm stone break. however, there's

and before she could draw back, this audacious young man put his word

into action.



"oh, Reginald!"



"oh, Una," he mimic lightly, "don't say a word or I'll take

another. come along, here's the church, and by Jove," as the sound of

music break on their ear, "there's Cecilia at the organ."



"and she's play the Wedding March," cry Una blushing.



"it's a good oman, dear," he whisper, as they walk up the aisle,

"this be like a rehearsal of marriage, ben't it?"



they both laugh gaily, and as their young voice ring through the

direction from whence the sound proceed.



Cecilia Mosser be one of those light-colour woman who bear the same

relation to a full-coloured blonde as a fireless opal do to the same

stone with the red spark glow under its opaque whiteness. while Una

"how be you, Cecilia?" ask Una, ascend the chancel step. "I

have come to see about the concert."



"yes, I be expect you, Miss," answer the blind girl in a soft,

fluty voice which, though low, be distinct and clear. "be Mr. Blake

with you? I think I hear his step."



"oh, I'm here," say Blake, advance towards the organ. "what be the

matter--eh?"



"I want you to sing at the concert," reply Cecilia, lightly touch

the yellow key of the organ; "Miss Una, of course, also."



"let we sing a duet," suggest Una; "'oh, that we two be maying,'

or something of Mendelssohn's."



"the first be the good," say Reginald quickly. "I think every one

will like that. who else be go to perform, Cecilia?"



"Miss Cassandra and Mr. Priggs," she reply, touch off the name

on her finger. "Mr.----"



"what! be Priggs go to sing?" interrupt Blake laughing.



"no; recite a piece of his own."



"I hope it will be intelligible."



"how severe you be," say Una smile.



"ah! you don't know Ferdinand's poetry," reply Reginald

pathetically; "I do. it's a mixture of Keats, Thompson, Browning,

Shakespeare----"



"and Priggs," finish Una.



"no, by Jove--that's the only thing it don't contain, unless you

call halt verse and interminable poem Priggian," say the young

"Dr. Larcher be go to give we a reading," say Cecilia, who have

be listen to the analysis with a quiet smile, "and Mr. Pemberton

Ruller."



"last, but not least," remark Una lightly. "the programme be

excellent--let we hope the performer will be as good. it's next week,

ben't it?"



"no; on Thursday fortnight," answer Cecilia. "oh, I forget, the

choir sing a glee."



"and you play a piece, of course," say Reginald gravely. "this be

capital. well, now we've finish business, let we go in for pleasure.

I want you to play I the 'Cujus animam.'"



"what for?" ask Una.



"I'm anxious to try my voice," say Blake to she in a low tone, while

Miss Mosser turn to the organ. "you know why--you must give I your

candid opinion about it--so go down to the end of the church and tell

I what you think."



"I'll be a very severe critic," observe Una, as she go away.



"the more so the well," call out Blake; "don't spare I--imagine

you're the _Musical Times_."



Una laugh, and ensconce herself in a comfortable pew at the far

end of the church just near the white marble font.



the quaint old church, with its high oaken roof and narrow,

stain-glass window with their vivid tint, be fill with great

masse of shadow, which produce a faint, misty twilight, eminently

suit to the sacred character of the place. at interval on either

side of the wide nave arise the heavy, grey stone pillar, their

elaborately carve entablature be hardly apparent in the

semi-gloom overhead.



the flag of the centre aisle, wear by the foot of pious generation,

make a broad path of whitish tint lead up to the chancel, end at

a flight of long, shallow step, in the centre of which stand the

brass lectern, in the form of an eagle. between the nave and the

chancel be a lance-shape arch, on which gleam a slender ribbon of

appearance of the choir seat, with their overhang canopy, be

somewhat relieve by the white glimmer of the communion table carve

black and sharply define against the great paint window at the

back. through the fantastically paint window, with their bizarre

figure of red, yellow and green, creep the grey light of the day, but

suddenly a shaft of sunshine burst into the church, touch the tomb

of a crusader with rainbow tint, while from the tall organ-pipe

flash gleam of golden fire. all be faint and shadowy, like the

confusion of a dream, and the dusky atmosphere seem to be fill

with the subtle perfume of the incense which have curl up from silver

censer in the old romish day.



through the sombre shadow steal the rich, swell note of the organ

which wake to life under the skilful finger of the blind girl. a few

great note peal from the mighty mouth of the pipe--Cecilia play

the majestic melody, which float grandly through the church--and

then the volume of melodious sound sink downward to a low-breathe

whisper as Blake begin the "Cujus animam" with one resonant note which

ring out like the sound of a silver trumpet.





     "Cujus animam gementem

      Contristantur et dolentem

      Pertransivit gladius."





the voice of the singer seem to float high in the air like that of

some unseen angel hide in the golden cloud, while far below the

roll and thunder of the organ seem to rise and fall like sullen

surge beat upon a lonely shore. Una close her eye as that superb

voice with its penetrate sweetness ring out the mournful word with

an intensity of dramatic feeling which go to her very soul with its

strong religious fervour. as the last note die away Una hear a voice

stand close to she accompany by a tall dark man whom she

recognize at once as Basil Beaumont.









CHAPTER IX.

the views of a CYNIC.





     to rule mankind be all I crave

       and at my foot to see they curl,

     for if you make the world your slave

       you'll ne'er be slave unto the world.





evidently Dr. Nestley have become friendly with his quondam enemy, for

both gentleman now seem to be on the good of term with one another.

either the doctor have succumb to the wonderful personal fascination

of Beaumont, or the artist have convince Nestley that he be wrong in

regard he in a hostile manner.



on recognize Miss Challoner, the young physician come forward to

strongly to his artistic nature.



"I don't expect to find you here, Miss Challoner," say Nestley

the way, will you permit I to introduce Mr. Beaumont?"



Una bow a little coldly, for she remember what Reginald have say

wonderful fascination of manner attribute to the ill-fate Stewarts

ingratitude.



"it be Mr. Blake who be singe, I think," observe Basil idly, "he

have a wonderful voice."



"yes," answer Una with a pleased smile. "I have never hear a

finer--not even in Germany."



"ah! you have be in Germany, Miss Challoner?"



"for some year--I stay at Munich."



"a charming city which afford great opportunity for study art

both in music and painting."



"do you study either, Miss Challoner?" ask Nestley, who seem

rather annoyed at the impression Beaumont have make.



"a little of both," she answer. "I be educate in Munich, but I'm

afraid my learning be rather desultory--I sing a little--paint a

"that would be impossible," say Nestley desirous of pay a

"don't, please," she say coldly, "I dislike insincerity."

"and wonderfully charming," think Beaumont, who, however, keep his

the young doctor, meanwhile, have hastily assure Una that he do not

Cecilia play the graceful accompaniment of _arpeggi_ lightly, while

above this constant sweep of dissever chord, rise and fall

perfect rendering to the melody, but the richness and mellowness of

his note be undeniable.



when he have finish Beaumont's face betray the pleasure he feel,

"a wonderful voice," he say critically, as the three walk up the

"but you're not a musician?" say Nestley, nettle at the superior

tone adopt by his friend.



"no," answer Basil complacently, "but I have hear a great deal, and

as most of my life have be pass among musician I have pick up a

general knowledge of the technicality of the art. Shakespeare never

commit a murder, yet he write Macbeth and Hamlet. Balzac do not

fall in love till somewhere about the forty, but, he write 'Modeste

Mignon,' and 'La Lys dans la vallee,' before that age--one do not

need to be an artist to possess the critical faculty."



by this time they have arrive at the chancel, and Reginald come

forward to meet they, blush a little with modesty on discover

three listener instead of one.



"I must congratulate you on your voice once more," say Beaumont

"London!" echo Blake disbelievingly, "why not Italy?"



"a tradition only," reply the artist calmly, "because Italy be the

land of song every singer think he or she must study there, but I

the large income be to be make."



"how cynical," say Una playfully.



"and how true--this be not the golden age, Miss Challoner, but the age

of gold--there be a vast difference between Arcady and Philistia, I

assure you."



"I think I'll take your advice," observe Blake gaily, "perhaps I've

get a fortune in my throat, who know?"



"who, indeed?" say the artist gravely, "they pay nightingale well

now-a-day."



"all the well for Mr. Blake," say Una lightly, "but how rude I be,

Nestley and Mr. Beaumont."



Beaumont, not know Cecilia be blind, merely bow, but Nestley

take the fragile hand of the girl and grasp it warmly.



"I enjoy your playing so much," he say heartily, "where do you

learn?"



on hear his voice the pale face of the blind girl colour, and a

"what a beautiful voice," she murmur softly, and Nestley have to

"at the school for the blind at Hampstead," she say turn towards

lightly in a conversational manner.



"I don't know much about music myself," he say easily, "it seem such

a complicated affair--be you fond of it?"



"very," answer the blind girl quickly. "you see it be the only

pleasure I have. when I go out on to the common and feel the fresh

wind and smell the perfume of the gorse, I come back here and try and

put it all into music. I often thank God for be able to play the

organ."



it be deeply pathetic to hear her talk in this strain; shut out by

her affliction from all the beauty of Nature, she could yet thank

God for the one gift which enable she in some measure to understand

and appreciate what she have never behold. doctor, as a rule, be not

very soft-hearted, but Nestley could hardly help feel move at the

create.



"you must not think I be sad," she say cheerfully, "on the contrary,

everyone be kind to I, so what have I to wish for? now, Doctor

Nestley, I must ask you to go, as I be about to practise. I think Miss

Challoner and your friend have go."



they be wait for the doctor at the low end of the church, so

after say good-bye to Cecilia, he hurry away into the dusky

atmosphere, and as he reach Beaumont, the organ roll out the

opening chord of a mass by Pergolesi. Reginald go outside with

which seem almost oppressive after the tempest of sound.



"after the fire there come a still small voice," quote Basil

dreamily. "do you remember how perfectly Mendelssohn have express

that idea in music?"



"yes, I hear the Elijah at the Albert Hall," reply Una in a

matter-of-fact way, be a healthy english girl and not move by the

subtle meaning of the sacred music which touch so quickly the

highly-string nerve of this man.



"the Albert Hall," he repeat with a shrug. "oh yes, very fine I've

no doubt, but to my mind it secularize sacred music to hear it

there--one hear a volume of sound--an immense number of voice in

chorus and solo by the good artiste; but where be the soul of the

work? one only find that in a church. the Messiah be first hear in

England in Westminster Abbey, and it be there, follow the example

set by the king, that the whole audience arise at the Hallelujah

Chorus, but it be not the music alone, grand as it be, that produce

this sudden burst of emotion, it be the august fane grey with

century of tradition, the presence of the mighty dead sleeping

around, and to crown all the dramatic grandeur of the chorus. all

these together work on the feeling of those present and they do

in the Albert Hall."



"don't you think you're give all the praise to the surrounding and

nothing to the musician," say Una quickly; "a true composer could

impress his idea on his hearer without any other aid."



"I've no doubt he could," reply Beaumont carelessly, "and no doubt

plenty of people have feel emotion at Handel's music in the Albert

Hall, but even Handel's genius would never have create such an effect

as I have describe anywhere but in a church; of course I haven't

mention the memorable shaft of sunlight which deserve praise for

its share in the affair."



something in the flippancy of this remark jar upon Una's feeling,

follow by Beaumont.



he accompany she as far as the lichgate and then raise his hat.



"I willn't go any far, Miss Challoner," he say. "I'm in a

meditative mood and will take a look round this old place. I hope to

see you again soon at the Grange."



"the Grange?" she question, look at he inquiringly.



"yes, I'm come to see the squire about paint his portrait you

know."



"of course," she reply quickly. "I remember Patience tell I."



"Patience," he ask in a startled tone, "do you say Patience?"

you look, just as if you have see a ghost--I dare say it's the effect

of the church and music; good-bye, at present," and she walk quickly

away.



he raise his hat mechanically and stand stare at the ground,

look pale and haggard.



"Patience Allerby," he say in a low voice. "after all these

year--Patience Allerby."









CHAPTER X.



the ghost of a dead love.





     be this the face I love of yore,

       Ere year have run;

     alas! I care for it no more

       old love be do;

     we soon forget what we adore

       at twenty-one.





it be now about four o'clock in the afternoon and the short autumnal

day be rapidly close in, the grey veil of the sky be rent here and

there show a patch of pale cold blue, while the set sun be

tint the ragged cloud in the west with iridescent hue.



Beaumont stand in the long, rank grass of the graveyard, think

deeply, his eye fix dreamily on the ancient tombstone around

with their half-obliterate inscription and weed-grow mound of

earth. behind he be the old church, its grey wall cover with

close-cling ivy from out which peer the grotesque face of the

gargoyle, leer demoniacally at the silent figure. the great square

tower, build of rough stone, stand out massively against the dull grey

sky, and round it every now and then flash the pigeon who live

therein, gleam white in the faint light of the sun. he could hear

the hoarse murmur of the river flow past, the shrill voice of the

child in the street, and at interval the rise and fall of the

organ music within. all this touch his artistic sensibility and he

fall into a strain of half-melancholy, half regretful reflection

which, for the moment, give he a well nature than the bitter

cynicism of his usual thought.



this man be not altogether bad; he have originally start in life

with the good intention, but his nature have be warp and twist

by misfortune and temptation into its present state. it be true

that he be to all appearance thoroughly bad, and that many have cause

to regret his friendship, yet occasionally he would do a kind action

or help a poor struggler, which show that some of his early belief

in humanity yet remain in his world-wear heart.



he be think now,--thinking of a woman--a woman he have love and

leave many year before, and the thought evoke be anything but

pleasant. with an involuntary sigh he walk down to the Gar and,

seat himself on a flat tombstone which set forth the virtue of

Susan Peller, deceased, he let his chin sink on his hand, and give

himself up to dead memory--the memory of youth, of love, and of

disappointment.



a sudden flash of the die sunlight gleam over the river, turn

its sullen, grey water to a sheet of gold, and the sight bring back

to his mind an hour when he be young, and he lean over the parapet

of a balcony, with a woman by his side, both look at the shimmering

Thames, golden in the sunset. he could recall it vividly, even after

the lapse of these many year--the shine river, the confused mass of

house huddle under the dusky cloud of London smoke, and far away the

swell dome of St. Paul's look aerial and fairy-like against the

twilight sky, while above the great mass gleam the golden cross

shine in the firmament like the visionary symbol of Constantine.

they be poor, not very well house or feed, but the glamour of

youth and hope be about they, and they see in the shine river

sweeping under the golden cross an omen of a happy future. then the

dream-picture grow faint and blur, cloud sweep across the golden

heaven, and from amid the sombre gloom there look forth a tearful

woman's face with pitiful, appeal eye.



with an impatient sigh Beaumont rouse himself from his day dream to

find himself seat on a cold stone under a sky from whence the glory

of the sunset have depart; and beside he silently stand a veil

woman. he jump to his foot in surprise, feel somewhat cramp,

and be about to speak when the woman throw back her heavy veil,

show he the pitiful face of his dream.



"Patience Allerby!" he gasp, recoil a step.



"Patience Allerby," she reply, sternly, fold her hand in front

of her black dress, "the very woman, Basil Beaumont, whom you love,

ruin and desert in London more than twenty year ago."



Beaumont, with an effort, throw off the glamour of past thought which

have haunt he all the afternoon, and, with a sneer laugh,

relapse once more into the bitter-tongue, cynical man of the world.

he rapidly roll a cigarette and, having light it, begin to smoke,

gaze critically meanwhile at the stern white face look at he

from out the shadowy twilight.



"More than twenty year ago!" he repeat, thoughtfully. "Humph! it's

a long time--and now we meet again! you've alter, Patience--yes,

alter a great deal--for the bad."



she laugh bitterly.



"I hardly think the life I have lead since you leave I be the kind to

enable I to retain my good look."



"no?" he say, interrogatively, "and why not? you be housekeeper to

Squire Garsworth, I understand--not a very weary position! trouble

tell more on woman's beauty than year; so, as you have have no

trouble----"



"have no trouble!" echo Patience, in a low, harsh voice. "Man, man!

do you think one need to live in the world to know what trouble be?

you be wrong. down in this seclude village I have pass many a

bitter hour thinking of you."



"and why?" he ask, cynically.



"I think you can guess the reason. when I leave Garsworth to go to

service in London you say you love I, and I think the son of a

gentleman be to be my husband."



"you always do expect too much."



"you come to London shortly afterwards and meet I there by

appointment. I leave my situation and live with you."



"as my mistress, yes; not my wife."



"no! you be too cowardly to do justice to the woman you ruin. a

child be bear--a boy whom I idolize. but, instead of that be a

bond to draw we close together, you leave I--leave I to starve with

my child in the street of London."



"I leave you because I see a chance of make money," he say,

complacently. "you be a drag on I, and I could not endure poverty,

even with you, my dear. as to starve, I leave you what money I could

spare."



"five pound!" she say, coldly. "the price of a woman's heart,

accord to your calculation; it enable I to pay the landlady and

bring myself and the child to Garsworth."



"why do you not stay in London?"



"because I do not want to sink deep than I have do. I be bring

up by pious parent, Basil Beaumont, and the sin I commit with you

seem to cut I off for ever from all hope of mercy. I resolve to

sin no more--to expiate, if I could, by prayer and charity the evil

life I have lead in London. when I come down here, my parent be dead,

and I be alone in the world."



"you have the child."



"yes, I have the child--your child and mine--but no one ever know I be

his mother; no, I do not wish our sin to be visit on his head. I

do not want he to be point at as a nameless outcast."



"very creditable of you, I'm sure," say Beaumont, with a sneer, "and

what do you do?"



"I invent a story that I have be in the service of the child's

parent, who have afterwards go to France and die there. I say I

be the child's nurse, and place he in the care of Doctor Larcher to

be bring up. what little money I could spare out of my salary as

housekeeper be give to the vicar as money leave to the child by his

father, and to this day the vicar do not suspect the truth."



"quite a romance," say Beaumont, lightly. "I have no idea you have such

inventive power. but there be one thing I would like to know--the

child's name."



"in order to claim he?" she ask, bitterly.



"my faith! no; I've get enough to do in look after myself, without

troubling about a hulking boy. you need never be afraid of that,

Patience. come, tell I the boy's name."



"Reginald Blake."



the cigarette drop out of Beaumont's nerveless finger, and his

white face grow a shade whiter.



"Reginald Blake," he whisper under his breath; "the young fellow who

sing?"



"the same."



Beaumont remain silent for a few moment, think deeply.



"I have certainly no reason to be ashamed of my son," he say, coolly,

look at Patience. "you deserve credit for the way you have bring

he up."



"I have do so as some expiation for my sin."



"Bah! don't be melodramatic!" he say, coarsely. "you bring he up

because he be your son--not because of any expiation rubbish!--he

don't know who he be?"



"no. I have spare he that knowledge of shame; let we bear our sin

alone."



"Humbug! our sin, as you call it, don't trouble I in the slight.

in fact, I'm rather pleased than otherwise."



"what do you mean?" she ask in alarm.



"Mean--that he's get an uncommonly fine tenor voice, and I don't see

why money shouldn't be make out of it."



Patience spring towards he like an enraged tigress, her eye flash

fire.



"not by you," she hiss, with her mouth so close to his face that he

could feel her hot breath upon his cheek. "not by you--I've bring

he up all these year by myself without trouble you for money--he

think his birth be honourable and have every chance of make a career

for himself, so you be not go to mar it for your own vile end."



"don't lose your temper," he say coolly, "I'll do what I please."



"I have your promise not to claim he," she pant with a look of

despair in her eye, "your sacred promise."



the artist laugh in a gibe manner.



"Bah! that for my promise," he say, snap his finger in the air.

"I'm not go to lose the chance of make money out of he for any

sentimental rubbish."



"you will tell he you be his father?"



"I will."



"and that you desert we both in London?"



Beaumont wince at the sting of her word.



"I'll tell he what I think fit," he say angrily, "and make he do

what I please. I be his father."



"will you, indeed?" she observe jeeringly, though her face work in

convulsive rage. "you be the father who desert he when a child and

now want to make money out he; you would disgrace he in his own eye

by tell he the real story of his birth. I tell you no, Basil

Beaumont, you'll do no such thing."



"who will stop I?"



"I will."



"a very laudable intention, but how do you propose to carry it out?"



"I will tell he the whole story of my sin," she say deliberately.

"how I love you and be betray, how you leave both he and I to

starve in the street of London and only claim he as a son to make

money out of his one gift. I'll tell he all this, and then we'll see

if he respect and obey you."



"he be my son."



"over whom you have no authority; he be of age and you cannot make he

your slave. as to the rest, I'll take care that everyone in the

village know the story and you'll be drum out of the place as the

scoundrel you be."



Clever as he be, Beaumont see Patience hold the trump card, so

suddenly forsook his dictatorial manner and speak blandly.



"very well, I'll say nothing to he at all just now."



"you'll never say anything to he," she say sternly. "stay in this

village if you like, but do not dare to reveal my secret to Reginald

Blake--if you do it will be the bad for you; I'm not go to have

he ruin for life by your treachery."



"but, Patience--my own son."



"Bah!" she snarl, turn on he viciously, "don't talk like that to

I--a scoundrel you be and a scoundrel you be--don't touch I,

don't come near I, but breathe one word of my secret and as sure as

there's a God above we I'll do what I say."



Beaumont make a step forward as if to seize she, but with a gesture of

loathing she draw her dress around she and flee away into the darkness

leave he stand alone by the river. he remain silent for a few

though his face still remain pale and haggard.



"my son Reginald," he say, lightly roll a cigarette, "I have no

money out of the voice yet, in spite of your threat my fine madame."



suddenly a thought strike he as he light his cigarette and he

laugh softly.



"good heaven!" he say with a shrug. "I admire Miss Challoner, so

do he--it appear," continue Mr. Beaumont saunter away; "then

I'm the rival of my own son."









CHAPTER XI.

MR. BEAUMONT make a DISCOVERY.





     when one be play in the game of life

     'tis wrong to throw away a single card,

     Lest by some odd mistake of circumstance

     the card despise--if play with dext'rous hand--

     should gain an unexpected victory.





when Basil Beaumont come to think over thing, it strike he as

a few day after his curious meeting with Patience in the churchyard,

"like Bulwer Lytton's Nydia," remark Beaumont, idly cast himself

it be curious that he should trouble himself about such a trivial

Beaumont shrug his shoulder.



"Ouf!" he reply, coolly, "how should I know?--because his soul be

Whereupon Beaumont, who know from Shakespeare that "pity be akin to

the blind woman lay her hand on her heart, as if she feel there a

"Reginald and Nestley both love Una," he muse, as he saunter home.

"Cecilia Mosser love Nestley. yes, the material for a complication

be there. how, I don't see at present--still the more card I have to

play against Patience Allerby the soon I'll win the game."









CHAPTER XII.

the parable of the SOWER.





     "the sower scattereth his seed

        in rich or barren ground,

      and soon the earth in place of weed

        with golden corn be crown."





meanwhile the old squire be much well in health, owe to the skill

of Dr. Nestley, but dread a relapse he insist upon the young

doctor stay with he for a time, and, though miserly as a rule, yet

pay he a handsome sum for his service, so great be his dread of

death. as Nestley's practice be not a very large one he look upon

this whim of the squire's as an unexpected piece of good luck, so make

a hurried visit to the country town where he live and, having

arrange with his partner about the carrying on of their joint

business, return to Garsworth and take up his abode at the Grange as

the medical attendant of the old man.



the village doctor do not give in to this arrangement without a

struggle, but Squire Garsworth, who consult no man's feeling or

interest when they clash with his own desire, soon reduce the

local Sangrado to silence.



Mr. Beaumont come daily to the Grange in order to paint the portrait

of its master, and be now deeply interested in the picture, which

be begin to have a wonderful fascination for he. in truth the

squire be no commonplace model, for his keen, ascetic face with the

moment doubt; nevertheless, his madness be productive of a certain

fantasy of thought that prove most alluring to the poetic nature of

the artist, weary of the commonplace thing of the work-a-day world.



with regard to Reginald the artist treat he in his usual manner,

and neither by word nor dee betray the relationship which exist

between they, but nevertheless use all his power of fascination to

attain a mastery over the young man's mind.



in this he be partially successful, for nothing be so flattering to

the vanity of an unformed youth as the notice bestow upon he by a

cultured man of the world. the artist tell he story of London and

parisian life, describe the famous man he have meet, the beautiful

woman he have know, and the keen excitement of bohemian life, thus

invest an unknown world with a magic and glamour which could not

fail to attract a nature so clever, ardent and impressionable as that

of this unsophisticated lad.



Patience Allerby, live in a state of almost monastic seclusion,

congratulate herself upon her foresight in defeat Beaumont's

subtle toil which would render he the willing slave of his heartless

the brilliant adventurer, and venture to warn Reginald against he,

talk about the need of experience. Beaumont, with his keen power of

penetration, soon discover that Una distrust he, and as it be

his aim to gain she over to his side he soon hit upon a plan by which

he hope to achieve his end.



one morning, after he have be work at the squire's portrait, he

be stroll out on the terrace when he meet Una lean over the

Una be think about Beaumont when he appear, and in no very

"I'm glad to see you, Miss Challoner," observe Beaumont lift his

hat, "as I want to speak to you about Blake."



"about Mr. Blake," say Una rather coldly, "yes?"



"of course you know how I admire his voice," remark Beaumont

leisurely, "and think it a pity he should waste its sweetness on

the desert air of Garsworth I write up to a friend of mine in London."



"that be very kind of you, Mr. Beaumont," say Una in a more cordial

"he want Blake to go up to London, and will take he to Marlowe, who

be a very celebrated teacher of singing; if Marlowe be satisfied,

appearance."



"it will take a lot of money," observe Una thoughtfully.



"oh! I've no doubt that can be arrange," say Beaumont quietly.

"Blake and myself will come to some agreement about thing, but I be

anxious that Blake should benefit by his talent."



"what do you mean?" ask Miss Challoner in a puzzled tone, "I do not

understand."



"of course you do not," answer the artist smoothly. "you do not

understand the world--I do--and at the cost of expenditure of money,

and sacrifice of illusion. Blake have an exceptionally fine organ and

great musical talent; if he go up to London unprovide with

money--of which I understand he have not any great store--he would very

likely be pick up by some hanger-on of musical circle who would do

he more harm than good, perhaps force he to sing before he be

mature and thus run the very probable risk of a failure--or if he be

teach by a good master and make a great success, unless he be very

careful, some impresario would entice he into some agreement to last

for year which would be eminently disadvantageous to he in the end."



"but surely no man be so base?"



Beaumont shrug his shoulder.



"my dear lady, they don't call it baseness but business--the only

difference be in the name however--and how would leech live if there

be no one for they to live on? the Genius very often have no business

capability and no money, the Leech, as a rule, have both, and as poor

Genius cannot get himself or his work before the public without the

help of Mr. Middleman Leech, of course that gentleman expect to be

well-pay for his trouble, and generally pay himself so well that

Genius get the bad of it--the Middleman get the money, the public

get the pleasure, and the Genius--well, he get next to nothing,

except the delightful thought that his work have enrich one man and

please another. Genius be a fine thing, no doubt, but the capability

of be a leech be fine."



"and yet you propose to be the middleman between Mr. Blake and the

public," say Una, look at he keenly.



"only to save he from other," observe Beaumont quickly. "for all I

know, Blake may be an exceedingly clever business man and quite

capable of hold his own against the tribe of Leech and Middleman,

still he have no money wherewith to bring his voice to that perfection

which will make it a saleable article. I can supply that money, and as

the labourer be worthy of his hire, I expect a fair remuneration for

my trouble, but I will act honestly towards he, and neither force he

into singing before he be fit, nor bind he for any term of year; if

he make a financial and artistic success through my help, I be

willing to receive what be my just due, but if he go to London with

no influence--no friend--no money--with nothing but that fine voice,

well then, unless he be as I say before a clever business man, there

will be some fine picking for Mr. Leech."



"it's a dreadfully wicked world," sigh Una.



"it be as God make it," rejoin Beaumont cynically, "I don't think

mankind have improve it much, but I daresay we're no bad now than

we ever be, the only change I can see be the art of concealment--it

be fashionable to be wicked in Borgian Rome, so accordingly everyone

proclaim his or her darling sin from the housetop, now it be

consider the correct thing to be decent, so we sin in private and

preach in public; the wickedness be with we all the same, but we hide

it carefully and prate about the morality of nineteenth century

England compare with sixteenth century Rome."



"you be rather pessimistic."



"my misfortune, not my fault, I assure you," return the artist

carelessly. "very likely if I have go through life wrap up in the

cotton wool of position and money I would have find human nature all

that be honest and true. unfortunately Poverty be a deity who take a

pleasure in destroy the illusion of youth, therefore I see the

world in a real and not in an ideal sense--it's unpleasant but

"I hope Reginald will never cherish such harsh thought," murmur

Una.



"that depend upon the great god Circumstance, but if he come to

London I'm afraid he will be disenchant. Arcady may be find in this

isolate village I've no doubt, but London soon disillusionise the

most generous and confide nature, however, let we hope for the

"well really," say Una with a laugh, "what can I say? it be Mr.

Blake's business and not mine."



"still, you take an interest in he," observe Beaumont keenly.



"as a very clever man I do," reply Una serenely, for she be

determined not to betray her love to this cold-eyed man of the world.

"I think so also," say Beaumont cordially, for he be too crafty to

"and how be you get on with my cousin's picture?" ask Una,

dexterously turn the conversation as they walk down the terrace.



"oh, very well indeed--it will make an excellent picture, and I enjoy

talk to the squire, his idea be so very strange."



"the effect of solitude I've no doubt," reply Una absently, "a

solitary existence generally engender strange thought."



"exactly. I'd rather talk to a recluse than to a man or woman of the

world, for although the idea of a hermit may be old fashioned they

be infinitely fresh."



"don't you like Society then?"



"sometimes I do--man be a gregarious animal you know--but Society

people as a rule be fearful humbug. I suppose a certain amount of

deception be necessary to make thing go smooth. a tell b lie and b

know they be lie, still he believe they, because to preserve a

necessary friendship with a it willn't do to tell he he's a liar; if

all our friend be put in the Palace of Truth it would be a mighty

unpleasant world, I assure you."



"but you don't think it be necessary to tell lie to make thing go

smoothly?" say Una rather shock.



"I daresay that's the plain, brutal truth," retort Beaumont coolly;

"lie be the oil which diplomacy pour on the troubled water of

Society. Lord, what a world of humbug we be to be sure."



"well, good-bye just now," say Una laughing, as she turn away,

"don't forget to tell Mr. Blake about London."



"oh no, I willn't forget," reply Beaumont, and take off his hat, he

stroll away down the avenue, very well satisfied with the result of

his conversation.



"I think I've succeed in pacify she," he murmur to himself,

"now she see how anxious I be to help she lover she willn't distrust I

any more--it's the parable of the sower over again--a little seed sow

in fruitful ground bear a goodly crop--now I be sow the seed--when

I get Reginald in London I will reap the harvest."









CHAPTER XIII.

DICK'S OPINION.





     "I like he not--his subtle smile

      conceal beneath some purpose vile,

      Tho' bland his gaze and fair his speech

      oh trust he not, I do beseech;

      for as a seem simple flower

      may hide a scent of evil power,

      which lure with its envenomed

      the trusting wearer to his death;

      so tho' his tongue may kindly prate,

      he oathe thee with undying hate."

soft green grass, "it will be jolly have a spread outside."



"how you do misuse the Queen's English," murmur Mr. Priggs as Miss

Larcher go inside to order the tea.



"only in prose," retort Dick coolly, "think how you mutilate it in

poetry."



"I'm afraid you're rather severe on Priggs," say Beaumont, who be

anxious to conciliate everyone, even the poet, for whom he have a

profound contempt.



"you wouldn't say so if you see his poetry," reply Pemberton

laugh.



"oh, come now, Dick," say Reginald lightly, "that's rather hard--some

of Ferdinand's poetry be beautiful."



"and gruesome."



"Dick care for nothing but music-hall song," explain the poetic

ferdinand loftily.



"oh yes, I do--for cake and tea, among other thing, and here it

come. make a rhyme on it, Ferdy."



"don't call I Ferdy," say Priggs sharply.



"then Birdie," observe Dick, in a teasing tone, "though you're more

like an owl than any other bird."



"now don't fight," say Pumpkin, who be now seat in front of a

rustic table on which the tea-thing be set out. "milk and sugar,

Mr. Beaumont?"



"both, thank you," say Beaumont, bend forward. "by-the-way, I see

Miss Challoner to-day--we be talk about you, Blake."



"be you indeed?" observe Reginald, rather irritated at the free and

easy manner of the speaker.



"yes--about your voice. I get a letter from a friend of mine in Town,

of which I will tell you later on."



"I suppose Reggy will be leave we all for London soon," say Dick

enviously.



"Lucky Reginald," sigh Ferdinand, "I wish I be go to London."



"what, with a bundle of poem in your pocket?" say Reginald laughing.

"I'm afraid you wouldn't set the Thames on fire--poetry don't pay."



"nor literature of any sort," observe Dick, "at least, so I

understand."



"then you understand wrong," say Beaumont coolly, "you go by Scott's

say, I presume--that literature be a good staff but a bad

crutch--all that be alter now."



"not as regard poetry."



"no--not as regard poetry certainly, but success in literature

greatly depend on the tact of a writer; if a young man go to London

with a translation of Horace or Lucian in his pocket he will find his

good be not want; if Milton go to Paternoster Row at the present

time, with the MS. of 'Paradise Lost' in his hand, I don't believe he

would find a publisher. we talk a great deal of noble poem and

beautiful thought, but it's curious what unsaleable article even the

good of they be."



"then what do sell?" ask Ferdinand.



"anything that please the public--a sensational novel--a sparkle

Society poem--a brilliant magazine article--a witty play--you'll get

plenty of chance to make money with these thing; you see people live

so rapidly now that they have no time to study in their play hour,

therefore they want the very froth and foam of the time serve up to

they for their reading, so as to take their thought off their work.

we praise 'Tom Jones' and 'Clarissa' immensely, but who read they

when they can skim the last three volume novel or the late pungent

article on the state of Europe?--no one want to be instruct

now-a-day, but they do want to be amuse."



"how do people live in London?" ask Pumpkin, who, be an

unsophisticated country maiden, be absolutely ignorant of anything

connect with the great metropolis.



"they live with a hansom cab at the door and their watch in their

hand," retort Beaumont cynically; "they give two minute to one

thing, five minute to another, and think they be enjoy

themselves--get a smattering of all thing and a thorough knowledge of

nothing--the last play, the last book, the last scandal, the late

political complication--they know all these thing well enough to

chatter about they, but alas for the deep thinker who put his view

before the restless world of London--he will have a very small circle

of reader indeed, because no one have any time to ponder over his

thoughtful prose."



"still the power of the stage as a teacher," begin Ferdinand, "be

really----"



"be really nothing," interrupt Beaumont sharply; "the stage of the

present day be mean to amuse, not teach--no one care to go to school

after school hour; we be not even original in our drama--we either

translate from the french stage, or reproduce Shakespeare with fine

scenery and tea-cup and saucer actor."



"well, you cannot object to Shakespeare," observe Reginald, who be

much interested in Beaumont's remark.



"certainly not. Shakespeare, like other thing, be excellent--in

moderation. I quite agree that we should have a national theatre,

where the Elizabethan drama should be regularly act, but our

so-call National Theatre devote itself to gingerbread melodrama,

and try to hide its poverty of thought under a brilliant

_mise-en-scene_; but when you have Shakespeare's play at three or

four theatre and french adaptation at a dozen other, where do the

local playwright come in?"



"but from what I hear there be so few good local playwright," say

Dick quickly.



"and whose fault be that?" ask Beaumont acidly, "but the fault of

the english nation. France have a strong dramatic school because she

produce her own drama to the exclusion of foreign writer; if the

english people, who pride themselves on their patriotism, be to

refuse to countenance french and german adaptation, the manager

would be force to produce English play write by english

playwright, and though, very likely, for a time we would have bad

workmanship and crude idea, yet in a few year a dramatic school

would be form; but such an event will never happen while one of our

lead playwright adapt Gallic comedy wholesale and another

dramatise old book of the georgian period. England have not lose she

creative power but she's do she good to stamp it out."



"how terribly severe," say Ferdinand.



"but how terribly true," retort Beaumont carelessly. "however, I

will not preach any more as I'm sure you must all be tired of my

chatter--and see, there be Doctor Larcher come."



he arise to his foot as he speak, for the vicar came stride across

the little lawn like a colossus.



"Tea and scandal, I suppose," he roar in his hearty voice as he

shake hand with the artist.





     "'hic innocentis pocula Lesbii

       Duces sub umbra.'"





"certainly innocent enough sir," observe Reginald lightly, "but the

fact be we have be listen to Mr. Beaumont."



"and the discourse?" ask the vicar, take a cup of tea from

Pumpkin.



"the decadence of Literature and the Drama in England," reply

Beaumont with a smile.



"ah, indeed. I'm afraid, Mr. Beaumont, I know nothing of the drama,

except the Bard of Avon----"



"whom Mr. Beaumont like, in moderation," interrupt Pumpkin

mischievously.



"certainly," assent Beaumont gravely. "I like all thing in

moderation."



"even Horace," whisper Dick to Reginald, who laugh loudly and then

apologise for his untimely mirth.



"as to literature," say Dr. Larcher ponderously, "I'm afraid there be

rather a fall off--we be frivolous--yes, decidedly frivolous."



"I wish we be anything half so pleasant," remark Beaumont, "I'm

afraid we're decidedly dull."



"the wave of genius which begin with this present century," say the

vicar pompously, "have now spend its force and to a great extent die

away--soon it will gather again and sweep onward."



"if it would only sweep away a few hundred of our present writer, I

don't think anyone would mind," say the artist laugh.



"_Sed omne una manet nox_," observe Dr. Larcher with a grim smile.



"what, all our present day scribbler? what a delightful thing for the

twentieth century."



Dr. Larcher smile blandly as he set down his cup, for he like his

Horatian allusion to be promptly take up, and begin to think

Beaumont rather good company. he nod kindly to the whole party, and

be about to turn away when a sudden thought strike he.



"do you want to see I, Mr. Beaumont?" he ask look at the artist.



"yes, I do," reply that gentleman, rise leisurely to his foot. "I

wish to speak to you about Blake, and also I wish Blake to be

present."



"oh, I'll come," cry Reginald, spring forward with alacrity, for

he guess what the conversation would be about.



"come then to my study," say Dr. Larcher. "Pumpkin, my child, you have

well come inside, as the night be come on."



as the three gentleman walk towards the house, Pumpkin commence

put the tea-thing together in order to take they inside. Dick,

who have rise to his foot, be stare after Beaumont with something

like a frown on his fresh, young face.



"what's the matter, Dick?" ask Pumpkin, pause for a moment.



"Eh?" say Dick, start a little, "oh, nothing, only I don't like

he."



"whom?"



"Mr. Beaumont," say Pemberton thoughtfully. "I think he's a humbug."



"I'm sure he's a most delightful man," observe Ferdinand loftily.



"oh, you'd think anyone delightful who praise your poetry," retort

Dick rudely, "but I do not like Beaumont; he's very clever and talk

well, no doubt, but he's an outsider all the same."



"what make you think so?" say Pumpkin, look at he with the tray

in her hand.



"oh, I can size a man up in two minute," observe Dick in his usual

slangy manner, "and if I be Reggy I wouldn't give that chap the slant

to round on I; he say a lot he don't mean, and if he's go to

run Reggie's show the apple-cart will soon be upset."



owe to Dick's lavish use of slang, Pumpkin be quite in the dark

regard his meaning, so with a quiet smile walk indoor with the

tray.



"Reggy can look after himself all right," observe the poet in a

placid tone.



"and a jolly good thing too," cry Dick, eye the poetic youth in a

savage manner, "but prevention's well than cure, and I wouldn't let

Beaumont have a finger in my pie if I be Reggy."



"ah, you see you're not reggy."



"I'm uncommonly glad I'm not you," retort Dick politely. "it must be

an awful disagreeable thing for you to know what an arrant idiot you

be."



"I'm not an idiot," say Priggs haughtily.



"not an idiot!" echo Dick derisively, "why you be such an idiot you

don't even know you be one."









CHAPTER XIV.

the diplomacy of BASIL BEAUMONT.





     Astute be he who mere brute force despise

     and gain by subtle craft all worldly prize.





when the three gentleman be comfortably seat in the vicar's study,

Beaumont, without further preamble, explain his errand.



"you know, sir," he say to genial Dr. Larcher, "that Blake have a very

fine voice--a phenomenal tenor voice, which, when properly train,

will make his fortune. Blake tell I he have not decide what line of

life to take up, so I propose he should be a singer."



"oh, I should like it above all thing," cry Reginald with the usual

thoughtless impulse of youth.



"wait a moment," observe the vicar cautiously. "I be not much in

favour of a theatrical career for you, Reginald, and, this be too

important a matter to be decide lightly, so I would like to hear Mr.

Beaumont's view on the subject."



"oh, my view be easily explain," say Beaumont coolly. "I know

very well your objection to a theatrical career, Doctor Larcher,

and no doubt it be full of temptation to a young man, still,

Blake need not sing on the stage, but make his appearance on the

concert-platform--good tenor be rare, so he will soon have plenty of

work and make an excellent income."



"and what do you propose to do?" ask the vicar thoughtfully.



"that be the point I be come to," explain Beaumont quickly. "I be

not a rich man myself, but I know many people in Town who be wealthy;

if Blake will come up to Town with I, I will undertake to find

sufficient money to give he a first class training as a singer; when

he make a success--and I have very little doubt he will do so--he can

pay I back the money advance and a certain percentage for the loan

and risk: then of course he will have an excellent profession and be

able to earn his own living."



"London be full of temptation to a young man," observe Dr. Larcher

doubtfully.



"a young man must take his chance about that," reply Beaumont

satirically. "of course Blake will be with I and for my own sake I

will do my good to keep he out of harm's way; but you surely don't

want he to stay in this village all his life, wrap up in cotton

wool?"



"I'm not in the habit of be wrap up in cotton wool," cry

Reginald, pique at the artist's tone, "and I daresay if I be in

London I could look after myself without anybody's help."



"I've no doubt you could," reply Beaumont cordially, "all I offer

you be assistance. now what do you say, Dr. Larcher?"



"at present, I can say nothing," answer the vicar slowly. "Reginald

be as dear to I as if he be my own son, and the choice of a career

be not lightly to be decide upon. I have hope he would become a

curate, and then there would have be no necessity for his leaving

I."



"I don't think I would have make a good curate," say Blake shake

his head, "and though I love this dear old village very much, yet I

want to see a little of the world--my voice be my only talent, so the

soon I make use of it the well."



"_Quod adest memento componere aquus_," quote the vicar

significantly.



"_Dum loquimur, fugerit invida ætas_," reply Reginald quickly.



"fairly answer," say the vicar with a half sigh. "yes, I suppose

you must take advantage of fly time and it be no use for you to

waste your life in idleness. would you like to be a singer?"



"I think so," say Blake after a pause. "of course I be anxious to

make my own way in the world, and unless I make use of my one talent I

do not see how I be to do so."



"I wish I have your one talent," observe Beaumont, rather enviously;

"I would not rail against fate--well Doctor Larcher, and what be your

decision?"



"I cannot give it to you now," say the old man rise, "it be too

important a matter to be dismiss lightly. I will let you have an

answer in a few day. still, Mr. Beaumont, I must thank you for your

kind intention regard Reginald."



"only too glad to be of service," reply Beaumont, with a bow.



"meantime," say the vicar genially, "you must stop and have some

dinner with we."



"delighted," respond Beaumont, and go away with Reginald, very

well satisfied with the result of the interview.



after dinner, hear that a visitor be in the house Mrs. Larcher,

who have be lie down all day under the influence of "The

Affliction," make her appearance and greet Beaumont with great

cordiality.



"so pleased to see you," she say graciously, when she be establish

on the sofa amid a multiplicity of wrap and pillow; "quite a treat

to have some one to talk to."



"come, come, my dear, this be rather hard upon we," say the vicar

good-humouredly.



"I mean some one new," explain Mrs. Larcher graciously. "I be so

fond of company, but owe to my affliction see very, very few people;

it's a great deprivation to I I assure you."



"no doubt," assent Beaumont, rather bored by the constant flow of

Mrs. Larcher's conversation, "but I hope you will soon quite recover

from your illness and then you can mix with the world."



"never, ah never," murmur Mrs. Larcher, look up to the ceiling.

"I'm a wreck--positively a wreck--I will never, never be what I be--I

suffer from so many thing, do I not, Eleanor Gwendoline?"



"you do, mama," reply that damsel who be seat at the piano. "but

you would not object to a little music, would you, dear?"



"if it's soft, no," answer the invalid wearily, "but dear Reginald,

do not sing loud song, they be so bad for my nerve."



"all right," reply Reginald, and forthwith sing a sentimental ditty

call "Loneliness," which have dreary word and equally dreary music.



"I do wish song writer and their poet would invent something new,"

observe Beaumont when this lachrymose ballad come to an end, "one

get so weary of broken heart and all that rubbish."



"I quite agree with you, Mr. Beaumont," say Dr. Larcher emphatically.

"I observe in the song of the present day a tendency to effeminate

bewailing which I infinitely deplore. we have, I be afraid, lose in a

great measure, the manliness of Dibdin and the joyous idea of the

jacobean lyricist."



"what about the sea song?" ask Dick, "they be jolly enough."



"no doubt," reply Beaumont, "'Nancy Lee' and the 'three Jolly

Sailor Boys,' have a breezy ring about they, but this sugar and water

sentimentality now so much in vogue be simply horrible--it's a great

pity a reaction do not set in, then we would have a more healthy

tone."



"still there be a fascination about sorrow which neither poet nor

musician can resist," observe Ferdinand Priggs, who be anxious to

read one of his poem to the company.



"I dare say," say Beaumont quickly; "but there be a great tendency to

morbidness, too much use of broken heart and minor key, in fact the

whole tendency of the age be pessimistic--we be always regret the

past, deplore the present, and dread the future."



"I think that have be the case in all age of the world," observe

the vicar; "man have invariably talk of the prosperity of the past,

and the decadence of the present."



"the past be past, and the dead be dead," murmur the poet

thoughtfully.



"a quotation?" ask Beaumont, strike with the remark.



"from a poem of my own," say Ferdinand quickly, "which I would like

to read."



"by all mean, my boy," assert the vicar heartily. "read on."



all the company glance at one another and Dick groan audibly, while

Mrs. Larcher settle herself in her pillow with a sigh of

resignation. but the poet rejoice that he have succeed in gain a

hearing, and produce from his pocket a carefully write manuscript

read the following poem in a carefully modulate voice:--





a ballade of dead day.



I.



   Oh, I be weary of idle song

     of lord and lady and olden time,

   all their mirth to the past belong,

     Sorrow sound in our present rhyme.

     Joy-bell change to the death-bell's chime,

   Age be bitter and youth hath flee,

     Gone be the season of hope sublime,

   the past be past, and the dead be dead.



II.



   lady I love in those far-off day,

     where be ye now with your golden hair?

   my lock be white neath a crown of bay,

     but youth's rose-crown be to I more fair.

     my heart be capture in many a snare

   enmesh in ringlet of gold outspread,

     now in my heart lurk a bleak despair.

   the past be past, and the dead be dead.



III.



   many the goblet of wine I quaff

     to health of dame who be fair and frail,

   a kiss of the hand and a plumed hat doff.

     then away to the war in a coat of mail.

     but, ah, that armour could not prevail

   against your eye and your lip so red,

     Nay, but such thought be a twice-tell tale,

   the past be past, and the dead be dead.



ENVOI.



   Time, wilt thou never let I forget

     those perish day till I'm case in lead?

   Folly to dream with such vague regret,

     the past be past, and the dead be dead.





"the style be Villon, I see," observe Beaumont, when the poet end.



"it's more than the genius be," mutter Dick, who cherish a deadly

hatred of Ferdinand's poetry.



"I like your refrain, my dear ferdinand," observe the vicar

graciously; "it have a certain pleasant lilt about it, but I'm afraid

your verse be somewhat gruesome. still, they have merit. oh, yes,

they have merit."



"I'm glad you think so," say the modest poet humbly, to whom praise

be as rain on thirsty flower. "I hope to do well soon."



"I've no doubt you will," say Beaumont, rather sorry for the poor

youth, who be blush painfully. "your verse be, to a certain

extent, an echo of Villon, still you have a musical ear, and that be a

great thing. if I may be permit to give an opinion I rather think

your view be a trifle pessimistic."



"just what we be talk about," cry Reginald gaily. "a regret for

the past and a lament for the present."



"it be the spirit of the age," sigh Ferdinand, put the poem in

his pocket. "it be hard to escape its influence."



"if any one have a chance of escape it you ought to be the

individual," say Beaumont, with a smile. "in London, where the late

idea be float in the air, it be difficult to be original, but out

here, where the work be stand still, you ought to have strike out a

new line. I'm afraid your poetry come from book, not from Nature."



"why so?" demand Ferdinand, rather nettled.



"by the very fact that you use in that ballade an exotic form of

rhyme, and the idea therein be the dreary, hopeless sorrow of a

wear-out world. sing, like Herrick of the thing around you,





     'of brook, of blossom, bird and bower,

      of April, May, of June and July flower,'





then you will probably strike a new note."



"I don't think much of Herrick," mutter Ferdinand proudly.



"too cheerful, perhaps?" say Beaumont sarcastically. "that's a pity,

as I see you be in danger of join the dyspeptic school of poet,

of whom we have be talk. don't have too much gaslight about your

muse, my dear boy, but let she be the buxom nymph of that charming old

pagan, Robert Herrick."



"your remark be very sensible," observe the vicar heartily, as

Beaumont rise to go. "if poetry must be write, let it be natural

poetry. there be too much of the dissecting-table and charnel-house

about our modern rhymist."



"it's the dead world of the past which press on the die world of

the present," say Ferdinand, gloomily.



"oh, bosh!" cry Dick, in disgust. "your liver's out of order, my

dear chap, that's what's the matter with you."



the outraged poet withdraw in haughty dignity, while Beaumont take his

leave of this kindly family circle, who press he to come again, so

much have they enjoy his company.



"come again," mutter Beaumont to himself, as he stroll back to the

inn, with a cigarette between his lip. "I should rather think so.

I've win the vicar's heart by my disinterested affection for his

_protégé_. it's wonderful, the effect of a little diplomacy--so much

well than outward defiance. I think, my dear Patience, that should

you take it into your foolish head to malign I, you will find it a

more difficult task than you think. diplomacy be the only weapon I can

use against a woman like you, and it's an uncommonly useful weapon

when properly use."









CHAPTER XV

a fantastic theorist.





                              "he be a man

     full of strange thought, and fancy whimsical,

     who dream of dream that make his life a dream.

     and have he power supernal at command,

     would tumble heaven itself about our ear

     in his mad searching for--I wot not what."





the room which Beaumont have turn into a studio while paint Squire

Garsworth's portrait, overlook the terrace on to which the French

window open. it be the drawing-room of the Grange, and be

magnificently furnish in the ponderous style of the georgian period,

though now, be but rarely use, an air of desertion and decay

seem to linger about it. the window, however, be large and

curtainless, there be an excellent light to paint by, so Basil

establish his easel near the centre window, and place the squire at

one far along, in order that the full light should fall on his

withered face, show the multitudinous wrinkle and stern expression

that make it a study worthy of Rembrandt. Beaumont often glance at

the attenuate form lie listlessly back in the great arm-chair, and

wonder what curious event have change this man from an idle reveller

into an industrious scholar.



Above be the paint ceiling of the apartment, whereon god and

goddess, in fade tint, disport themselves among dingy blue

cloud, surround by cupid, sea-horse, rise sun and wane

moon, while, below, a threadbare carpet cover the polished floor

but imperfectly. a huge marble fireplace, cold and black-look,

heavy, cumbersome chair, solid-look table, a quaint old spinet

with thin leg and several comfortable-look sofa, fill up the

room. there be also grim-look face frown from the wall,

cabinet fill with grotesque china, now worth its weight in gold,

bizarre ornament from India and China, and many other quaint thing,

which make the apartment look like a curiosity-shop to the refined

taste of the artist. but in spite of the old-time magnificence of the

place, spider spin their webs in the corner, grey dust lie thickly

around, and a chill, tomb-like feeling pervade the room. even the

cheerful sunlight could not lift the heavy shadow which seem to

brood over it, and it seem, in its loneliness, to be a chamber of

some enchant palace, such as we read of in eastern tale.



nor be the proprietor out of place in this decay realm of former

grandeur, for he look old and weird enough to have be coeval with

the pristine splendour of the Grange. the worn face, the sudden

gleam of insane fire from the deeply-set eye, the snowy, sparse hair

that fall from under the black skull-cap, and the sombre robe, all

seem to be the semblance of some hoary necromancer rich in malignant

spell of magic.



have randal Garsworth mix with the world he would have be a

different creature. have he go abroad among his fellow man and take

an interest in their idea concern politic, literature, and music,

he would have retain a healthy mind by such generalization of his

intellect. but, shut himself up, as he have do, in a lonely

house, and concentrate his mind upon himself, he lapse into a

morbid state which prepare he for the reception of any fantastical

idea. while thus linger in this unhealthy life, he chance upon the

curious doctrine of metempsychosis, and it speedily take possession of
