his diseased mind, already strongly inclined towards strange

searching. the weirdness of the pythagorean theory appeal to his

love of the whimsical, and he become a monomaniac on the subject.

under the influence of a lonely life, ardent study of the

philosopher who support the theory of transmigration, and his

selfish application of these wild doctrine to his own soul, the

monomania under which he labour deepen into madness.



to all appearance he conduct himself in a rational manner, though

slightly eccentric, but with his firm belief in metempsychosis, and

his preparation for his future incarnation he could hardly be call

sane. yet he conduct all business matter with admirable skill, and

in spite of the dilapidated state of the Grange, his farm be well

manage, and his tenant find no cause to complain of neglect on the

part of their landlord. like all madman, he be a profound egotist,

and absorb in his belief of a re-incarnation on this earth, he pay

no heed to the claim of relative or friend, neglect all social

duty in order to devote himself entirely to his favourite delusion.

such be the man who sit before Basil Beaumont, by whose skilful brush

and genuine talent the strange face of the recluse be rapidly be

transfer to the canvas in the most life-like manner.



"I hope this portrait will please you," say Beaumont, break the

silence which have last some minute, "it's the good thing I have

ever do."



"be it?" reply Garsworth, vaguely, his mind be far away, occupy

with some abstruse think. "yes, of course. what do you say?"



"I hope you'll like the picture," repeat Beaumont, slowly.



"of course I will," say the squire, quickly. "I want to see myself in

the future as I be now. some people look back on their portrait take

in youth, and see a faint semblance of their old age in the unwrinkled

face, but I will see this picture when in a new body which will have

no resemblance in its form to the wither shape I now bear."



"a strange doctrine."



"as you say--a strange doctrine," say Garsworth, warm with

his subject, "but a very true one. my body be old and wear out.

physically, I be an irreparable wreck, but my soul be as lusty, fresh

and eager as it be in the day of my youth. why, then, should not my

true entity shed this wear-out, fleshly envelope as a snake do its

skin, and enter into a new one replete with the vigour of youth?"



"a difficult question to answer," reply Beaumont, calmly, "very,

very difficult. we have no proof that such a thing can happen."



"you be a materialist?"



"Pardon I, no. a materialist, as I understand the word, deny the

independent existence of spirit; I do not. I believe our spirit or

soul to be immortal: but, as to this re-incarnation theory--it be a

dream of Pythagoras."



"it be a dream of many before Pythagoras, and have be the dream of

many since," rejoin Garsworth, coldly. "the Egyptians, the Hindoos

and the Buddhists all accept the doctrine, although each treat it

accord to their different religion. in our modern day lesse

believe in it; and if you have read the writing of Kardec you will

find that re-incarnation be the very soul of the spiritist belief."



Beaumont sneer.



"I cann't say I have much faith in the maundering of spiritualist.

table-turning and spirit-rapping may be very pleasant as an amusement;

but as a religion--bah!"



"you talk like that because you don't understand the subject. the

thing you mention be only the outward manifestation of spiritualism.

if you read Kardec's book you would find that the true theory of

spiritualism be transmigration. spirit be incarnate in human body

in order to work out their own advancement. if they resist temptation

while in the flesh, they enter into a high sphere, in order to

advance another step. if they fail to lead a pure life, they again

become re-incarnate in the flesh to make another effort; but they

never retrograde."



"and you believe in this doctrine?" ask Beaumont, incredulously.



"with certain reservation--yes."



"and those reservation?"



"I need not mention all, but I will tell you one as an example. the

spiritist deny that we remember former existence--I believe we do."



"oh! and you think in your next body you will remember your

incarnation as Squire Garsworth?"



"I do."



"do you remember your former existence?"



"some of they."



"why not all?"



"because some of the life I then live be base in the extreme, and

not worthy of remembrance, so I forget they--in the same way as you

forget disagreeable thing and only have thought of agreeable

event."



"will you tell I some of your former existence?"



"it would be hardly worth while," reply the squire, irritably, "as

you would only look upon my narration as a fairy-tale. but I can tell

you what I be--an egyptian prince, a roman soldier, a Spanish Moor,

and an english pauper in the reign of Elizabeth."



Beaumont look in astonishment at the old man, glibly run off

this fantastic list.



"and since the pauper stage?" he ask, smother a smile.



"I have be re-incarnate in this present form," respond the

squire, gravely; "it be because I experience poverty in my last

existence that I be save money now."



"I don't understand."



"to keep myself during my next incarnation."



the artist be become quite bewildered at hear this farrago of

nonsense utter in such a serious tone. however the conversation be

so extraordinary that he could not forbear humour the madman.



"a very laudable intention," he say, quietly, "but as you will be

someone else in your next incarnation, how be you go to claim

Squire Garsworth's money?"



"ah!" respond the squire, with a cunning smile, "that be my secret;

I have arrange all that in a most admirable way. I can claim my own

money without any trouble."



"but suppose you be bear a savage?"



"I will not be bear a savage--that would be retrogression, and spirit

never retrograde."



"well," say Beaumont, rise to his foot, and put his brush

away, "your conversation be get too deep for I, Mr. Garsworth. I

understand your metempsychosis theory all right, though I don't agree

with it; but I fail to see how you be go to arrange about get

your own money."



"no, no!" reply Garsworth, raise his form, tall and gaunt, against

the bright light outside, "of course not; that be my secret. no one

will know--not one! be your sitting finish?"



"yes, for to-day."



"come to-morrow--come to-morrow!" say the old man, come round to

look at the picture, "no time to be lose, I may die before it's do,

and then I willn't be able to see myself as I be: but Nestley will keep

I alive--good doctor--very good doctor--pay he handsomely--yes,

handsomely! good-bye for to-day, Mr. Beaumont. don't forget to-morrow;

I may die--no time to lose--good-bye!"



the old man shuffle tremulously out of the room, and Beaumont stood

look after he with a puzzled smile on his lip. he begin to put

his paraphernalia away slowly and talk softly to himself meanwhile.



"I wonder if there's any sense in the old fool's raving--I don't

believe in this incarnation rubbish--but he's get some scheme in his

head about that money--I'd like to find it out--there might be

something in it by which I could benefit--he's a madman sure enough

but still there be method in his madness--however, I'll try to

discover his secret somehow."



he light a cigarette and saunter out on to the terrace, think

over the chance of find out the squire's secret with a view to

turn it to his own account. apparently his cogitation lead to some

result, for after stand for a few minute at the end of the terrace

in a brown study, he remove his cigarette from his mouth and utter

one word:



"Hypnotism."









CHAPTER XVI.

the village concert.





     the viol sound in festal hall

     where come the merry mummer all,

     the minstrel sing their roundelay

     of doughty knight and lady gay,

     and as the carol music swell

     the jester shake his cap and bell,

     while lord and dame of high degree

     Approve the Christ-tide revelry

     and happy in the pleasant din

     Amazed the foolish rustic grin.





the school-room be a long, old-fashioned apartment, with plain oak

wall and a high roof. the wide window be set low down, and when

seat at their desk the scholar could look out and see the old

stone cross of the market-place and the heavily foliage elm that

wave their green leave in front of the queer red-tile house. the

wall be hang round with map of the five division of the world,

and above the teacher's desk, which be set on a raise daïs, appear

a map of the world itself. on this occasion the ink-splash desk of

the teacher be remove and in its place stand a small cottage piano.

dark red curtain hang down from brass rod on either side so that the

dais be transform into a very fair stage, while at the back

decorative effect be obtain by a Union Jack be gracefully

festoon over the royal arm, paint by the village artist.



the desk of the scholar be immovable be leave in their place,

and the audience--which comprise nearly the whole population of the

village--sit like row of elderly pupil ready to be instruct. form

and desk be range in the centre of the room and there be a narrow

walk on either side lead down to the wide door at the end of the

building which be continually open and shut to admit late

arrival and exclude a view of the festive preparation from the

penniless crowd outside who could not afford the necessary copper for

entrance fee. illumination be provide by six oil lamp, three on

each side, set in metal bracket, and from the centre of the roof over

the stage hang a large lamp, while the piano be far adorn with

two weakly-look tallow candle for the convenience of the musician.



the school-mistress, Miss Busky, a dry-up prim-looking little woman,

who resemble a cork fairy more than anything else, have far

ornament the bare room by wreathe round the map and lamp string

of coloured paper flower manufacture by artistically incline

pupil, and even the leg of the piano be swathe in these tissue

paper decoration. over the stage there be also a large placard

bear the word "welcome" wreathe with artificial flower, so that

Miss Busky on survey her handiwork feel quite content with the

general effect of luxury produce by herself and her satellite. the

programme be neatly write out by the good writer in the school,

and hand only to favour visitor as these effort of penmanship

be few in number. the visitor themselves, red, lusty country folk,

have come from far and near to the concert, and the little school-room

be uncomfortably full, but owe to the fierce effort of Miss Busky,

who bounce about like an india-rubber ball, everyone be at last

comfortably settle.



Mrs. Larcher and Pumpkin take no part in the performance be

accommodate with front seat, together with many of the country

gentry, who always patronise these entertainment at the urgent

request of the vicar, who greatly believe in good feeling and

friendliness exist between the lord of the soil and their tenant.



and now amid a great clapping of hand and stamp of heavily shod

foot the popular vicar himself appear on the stage as chairman, and

take his seat beside a small table adorn with a jug of water, a

glass and a programme.



Dr. Larcher make a short speech, end with a quotation from his

favourite poet:





     "et thure et fidibus juvat

      Placare,"





which hardly anyone understand, and then the serious business of the

evening commence.



the concert be open by the indefatigable Miss Busky and Cecilia,

who play a duet by a popular composer on popular air, in which say

air be almost smother in variation, and blend one with the

other in a most surprising manner, for just as the audience recognize

"Rule Britannia" and have settle themselves down for an intellectual

treat the player break off into "the last Rose of Summer," and thence

burst into "Auld Lang Syne," melt, amid a perfect fire-work of

run, into "the british Grenadiers," which latter be play with

full force by four hand, the loud pedal press down, bring the

overture to an end in a noisy manner which delight the audience.



Reginald then sing "come into the garden, Maud," but this number

evidently do not please they very much as they could not make out

what it be all about and, prefer noise to delicacy, do not

appreciate the beauty of the singer's voice. Beaumont, however, who

be present, admire the item greatly, and say as much to Mrs.

Larcher who, arm with a fan and a smell bottle, sit next to he

fight with "the affliction."



"oh yes," sigh Mrs. Larcher when she have get "The affliction" well

under and do not feel inclined to faint, scream, or kick, or give way

to any other eccentricity which "the affliction" be fond of do

at unseasonable hour, "his voice be beautiful, no doubt, but so loud,

it go through my head and rattle my nerve. I love soft song that

soothe I--something cradle-like--a Berceuse, you understand. I'm

afraid you find I rather hard to please, but it's my affliction and

not myself. I assure you, Mr. Beaumont, that a loud voice often

prostrate I for day and leave I a perfect object, do it not,

Eleanora Gwendoline?"



Eleanora Gwendoline, alias Pumpkin, assent with alacrity to this

remark, upon which Beaumont observe that he never should have think

it to look at she, thereby incite Mrs. Larcher to a weakly spasm of

coquetry for she tap Basil feebly with her fan and say he be a

naughty man, then settle herself to listen to a glee by the choir.



the choirmaster, Simon Ruller, a long, thin individual, in a frantic

state of excitement, having reduce his chorus to a state of abject

nervousness start they off in the glee "Glorious Apollo," and after

two or three false start they manage to begin. having begin, their

great aim be to get over the ground as rapidly as possible, and they

rush it through at lightning speed, Mr. Ruller implore they in

fierce whisper to observe the rallentando, which advice, however,

they do not take. on disappear from the stage, chase off by the

excited Ruller, they be succeed by Miss Cassy, attire in a

startling costume of blue and yellow.



this lady's contribution to the proceeding be a milk and water

ballad of a semi-jocular kind, call "almost a case," and the way in

which she leer and smirk at the audience from behind her music in

order to point the meaning of the verse, be quite alarming. she pay

no attention to time, and poor Cecilia be oblige to stop one minute

and play furiously the next in order to follow Miss Cassy's spasmodic

idea of render the song.



"so flippant," comment Mrs. Larcher when the fair songstress have

retire, "a great want of decorum--she make my nerve jump."



"it's the style of song, mama," say Pumpkin generously.



"then why don't she choose less hoppy music?" retort the matron

fan herself vigorously, "it make I twitch to hear she. ah, if

she only have my affliction she wouldn't sing at all."



Beaumont privately think this would be an excellent thing for

everyone, but do not say so, know Mrs. Larcher to be a great

friend of Miss Cassy's.



Dick Pemberton give a sea song with great vigour, and receive genuine

applause, then Una and Reginald sing "oh, that we two be maying,"

which the audience do not care about. the vicar then read Poe's poem

of "the Bells" in a ponderous manner, which crush the airy line,

and after another song from Reginald, Mr. Ferdinand Priggs appear to

recite an original poem "my Ladye Fayre."



Mr. Priggs be usher in by a melancholy strain from the piano, and

place one hand in his breast and toss back his long hair with the

other he burst into a series of question about the fayre lady.





     "be it a dream of sadness

      that reel my brain to madness,

      or how

      do I see her brow

      with its crown of golden gladness?"





after ask these question Mr. Priggs prove conclusively that it

be no dream, but





     "a wild, weird, wandering, warn dame

      who set the ear of all aflame

      with loud acclaim."





the poet treat his audience to about twenty verse of this gruesome

production, and having end with a long sigh stand on the stage for

fully a minute. everyone wait to hear what he be go to say next,

but the poetic Ferdinand double up his limp body into what he call

a bow, and slowly drift out of sight, his leg apparently take he

wherever they choose to go.



on the conclusion of this dismal poem the full company sing "God save

the queen," and the concert end amid the congratulation of all

concern, as they decide it be a great success.



the vicar heartily congratulate the performer on the receipt, as

after pay all expense there remain fully five pound for the

almshouse fund, to aid which the concert have be get up.



"where be Doctor Nestley, to-night?" ask Beaumont as they go out.



"he have to stay with the squire," reply Una, who be lean on

Reginald's arm, "he's not at all well."



"nerve?" ask Mrs. Larcher anxiously, take a medical interest in

the case.



"oh, dear no," say Miss Cassy lightly, "though he have get nerve--so

very odd, ben't it? but this time the dear doctor say it's

lung--something go wrong--a kind of what's-his-name thing, you

know--if he don't take care he'll get that disease--so

odd--something about a moan."



"oh, pneumonia," observe Beaumont gravely. "I hope not, it's very

dangerous, and to an old man like the squire, doubly so."



"I have have it," say Mrs. Larcher, who by her own showing possess

every disease under the sun. "acute inflammation of the lung, it leave

I a wreck--a prostrate wreck--do it not, Eleanora Gwendoline?"



"it do, mama," reply the dutiful Pumpkin.



"it might come on again," say Mrs. Larcher, open she

smell-bottle. "I'll have a cup of hot tea when I go home, and a hot

bottle to my foot."



"I wonder she don't have a mustard plaster and a fly blister,"

whisper Dick to Una, "might draw some of the bosh out of she."



Una laugh, and the great lumber barouche of the Grange having

arrive, drive by the stony Munks, she prefer to enter it,

follow by the chatter Cassy.



"so cold, ben't it?" say that lady, "quite like the North Pole.

Captain what's-his-name, you know, Parry, put I in mind of

Paris--french style--so odd. I'll see you to-morrow, Mr. Beaumont, and

oh, Mrs. Larcher, will you come to tea next week--Thursday--what do

you say, Una? Friday, oh yes--Friday."



"if my affliction permit I," say Mrs. Larcher in a stately tone, "I

will try."



"so glad," reply the volatile Cassy, "and you come also Mr. Blake,

and of course Mr. Pemberton, not forget Mr. Beaumont; so

very nice to see one's friend. oh, yes, Munks, we're quite ready,

good-night--so pleased--delightful concert--odd--very odd."



far talk on the part of Miss Cassandra be check by the sudden

start of the barouche, and what with the uneven road and the wear-out

spring of the coach, Miss Cassy have enough to do to look after

herself without talk.



Mrs. Larcher, lean on the vicar's arm, walk home, follow by

Pumpkin and the three pupil, Dick chaff Ferdinand over his poem

till that poetic soul be nearly out of his mind with anger.



Beaumont, leave alone at the school-room door, light a cigarette, and be

about to go when he hear a faint sigh behind he, and on turn see

Cecilia and the lively Busky.



"I enjoy the concert very much, Miss Mosser," he say gracefully as

they pass he.



"I'm glad of that, sir," say Cecilia, who look tired, "it go off

very well. be--be Doctor Nestley here?"



"no, he have to stay with Squire Garsworth."



the blind girl sigh again, and after say good-night, go away

follow by Miss Busky, who bound along in the moonlight like a

marionette.



"poor girl," say Beaumont thoughtfully, "she love Nestley, and willn't

have the slight chance with he, he's too much in love with Una

Challoner. by-the-way, I must see Nestley; if I want to find out the

squire's secret, I'll have to arrange matter with he--I hate

watch-dog."









CHAPTER XVII.

ANTEROS.





     strong god thou art the enemy of god,

        a hater of blind Eros and his joy,

     Thy rule be bitter as the sting rod

        that scourge at Dian's feast the spartan boy;

     evil his soul who ask thine evil aid,

        and in revenge such evil aid employ,

     in sunder the heart of youth and maid.





the Garsworth family be never a very prolific one, but the estate

have always descend in a direct line from father to son. many a time

the race seem to be on the point of extinction owe to the

representative be an only child, yet though the line dwindle down

to depend on one life alone for its continuity it never absolutely

die out. in the event of such a thing take place it would have be

difficult to say who would have succeed to the estate, as the

Garsworth family seem to be averse to matrimony and their connection

with the county family be, to say the least, doubtful. besides, as

there be no entail, the estate be completely at the disposal of

the head of the family for the time being, and he could will they to

whomsoever he please. as hitherto son have always succeed father,

there have be no necessity for the exercise of such a power, but now

the sole representative of the race be unmarried he be at liberty

to use his own judgment in dispose of the estate.



in the opinion of right-minded people there could be very little doubt

as to who should succeed the Squire, for Una be the next of kin. she

be the only live representative of the young branch of the

family, be the grand-daughter of the Squire's aunt, and therefore

his second cousin. Miss Cassandra, although she constantly allude to

Randal Garsworth as "my cousin," be as a matter of fact only a

relation by marriage, be Una's paternal aunt.



Una's parent have die while she be a child and she have be bring

up by the kind-hearted though eccentric Miss Cassy, who send she to

Germany in order to complete her education. Miss Cassandra, have an

income of three hundred a year, dwelt in London, where she be know

among a select society of well-bear fossil who look upon she as a

mere child. Una, having finish her education, come back to England

and take up her abode with Miss Cassy, and have an income of some

two hundred a year join it to that of her aunt, and thus the two

woman manage to live very comfortably in a small way.



on see Una's beauty, however, Miss Cassandra have no intention that

she should live a dismal life in a smoky London suburb, without at

least one chance of see the gay world and marry as befit she

birth and loveliness, so she write to Squire Garsworth on the subject.

the old man send in reply a gracious message that Una could come down

and stay at the Grange, and that he would not forget she in his will.

Miss Cassy, not know the idiosyncrasy of the recluse, see in her

mind's eye a hospitable country house full of joyous company, so

persuade Una to accept the invitation, say she herself would go

also. after some demur Squire Garsworth agree to Miss Cassy come,

and in due time, having break up their London home, the two lady

arrive at the Grange.



their dismay be great at find the sordid way in which the squire

live, and Miss Cassy would have promptly return to London, only

Una, be touch by the loneliness of her kinsman, determine to

remain, persuade Miss Cassy to do likewise. so they live quietly at

the Grange on the somewhat begrudge hospitality of the old man, their

own income obtain for they any luxury they might require, as

they certainly receive nothing but the bare necessity of life from

their host.



in the mad pursuit of his delusion, Garsworth, in contrast to the

lavishness of his youth, have become absolutely penurious in his mode

of life. the large staff of servant necessary for such an immense

house as the Grange have be long ago dispense with, and Patience

Allerby, assist by jellick look after the household, while the

stony Munks exercise a grim sovereignty over the exterior

arrangement. the squire mostly live in his own study, and Una, aid

by Miss Cassy, manage to make one room habitable for themselves, but

the rest of the house be give over to the rat and spider, become

at last so lonely and eerie that Miss Cassy frequently declare it be

haunted.



Una having fall in love with Reginald, be quite content in her

dreary exile, but Miss Cassy, use to the lively entertainment of the

fossilized society in London, long to get away from the place, and

look forward to the Squire die with a certain ghastly eagerness,

as she think Una would then come in for all the estate and they

could once more live London.



on the morning after the concert Miss Cassy and Una seat at a late

breakfast, be talk seriously about the unsettled health of the

Squire, who be now obviously break up.



"he's about seventy-three now," say Miss Cassy thoughtfully, "I'm

sure he cann't live long.



"my dear Aunty!" reply Una in a shocked tone, "how can you talk so?"



"why not?" retort Miss Cassy indignantly. "he's not much use alive.

I'm sure he'd be more use dead."



"why?"



"because you'd get his money and we could go back to dear London."



"I don't want his money," say Una with great spirit, "and certainly

don't care about speculate on cousin Garsworth's death to gain it. I

wonder at your do so, Aunt."



"well, I'm sure, Una," whimper Miss Cassy, produce her

handkerchief, "you be so odd--I only mean to say I'm tired of this

place--it be dull--now ben't it? I need excitement, you know I need

excitement--and after I bring you up. I always dress you

beautifully--real lace--and keep you so clean. I always have your

nerve attend to--you blame I now--I want to see you rich--it ben't

odd--wish to see you rich, and I'm so dull here; really Una, you

be unkind--quite crush--I'm only an ivy--oh, why ben't I marry?

there's nothing for one to cling to--you don't want I to cling."



"my dear Auntie," say Una with a smile, "you be so sensitive."



"Ivy," sob Miss Cassy, "nerve--mother's side--you've get none--so

very odd."



"I don't want you to think of the Squire die, it willn't benefit I at

all."



Miss Cassy remove her handkerchief and gasp:



"quite ten thousand a year--he cann't take it away--you're his only

relative--no one could be so odd as to leave it to a what's-it's-name

asylum or a cats'-home."



"I don't know whom he'll leave the money to," say Una deliberately.

"I certainly ought to get it, but you know the Squire's delusion about

re-incarnation--you may depend his will be mix up with the idea, how

I don't know--but there will be some trouble at his death."



"such an idiot he be," groan Miss Cassy, "quite

eccentric--hereditary--I've see it in you--bad blood you know--it's

in all old family--our family be always sane."



to prove which sanity Miss Cassy arise from the table to go to her

room, and place the tea cosy on her head to protect she from cold.

the eccentric lady walk to the door talk in a broken fashion all

the time.



"I'm sure I don't want his money--small income but sure--yes--but it's

so dull--I love London--I cann't blossom here--I'm like a cabbage--in

Town I expand--such nice amusement--Madame Tussaud's and the Crystal

Palace--so exciting--it's food--food--oh, dear I, Dr. Nestley be this

you? how be my cousin? better?--so glad--it's very odd, ben't it? I

mean it's not odd I'm glad--no--quite so--oh, you want to see Miss

Challoner--yes--good-bye just now," and Miss Cassy, with the tea cosy

perch on her head, disappear, leave Nestley alone with Una.



the young man be not look well, as his ruddy colour have give

place to an unhealthy paleness, his skin have a flaccid appearance and

his countenance wear an anxious, haggard expression. his eye glance

restlessly round the room look at everything except Una, and he

move his hand nervously. even in his voice there be a change, for

in place of his former bold confident tone he now speak in a low

hesitate manner.



"I just come to tell you the squire be well, Miss Challoner," he

say in an agitated voice, keep his eye on the ground.



"it's very good of you, doctor," she reply courteously. "I hope he

will become quite strong again."



"I'm afraid not, his body be wear out and have not strength enough to

resist disease--of course, now he have only a slight cold, but any

chance exposure may affect his lung seriously and if pneumonia set

in I'm afraid he will have no chance."



"what be to be do?" she ask anxiously.



"I cannot do more than I have do, he must be keep quiet and warm.

I've persuade he to take some strong soup which will do he good--in

fact I think his ascetic manner of living have have as much to do with

his ill-health as anything else."



"I hope he will get well," say Una earnestly, "if he would only

change his mode of life I'm sure he would get well."



"yes," the young man answer absently, "of course, exactly," he

hesitate a moment then burst out in despair, "then I would have to go

away."



Una look at he surprised at his evident emotion.



"of course we would be very sorry to lose you," she say quietly, "but

you, no doubt, would be glad to get back to your home."



"no--I would not," he say passionately, come a step nearer,

"because you would not be there."



"I?"



Una Challoner rise to her foot in amazement at his word.



"I?" she repeat in a puzzled tone. "what have I to do with your

movement?"



"everything," say the unhappy young man with a gesture of despair.

"when I come here a short time since I be perfectly happy--I have

conquer all the evil and sorrow of my youth, and my life be a

pleasant one, but since I see you all be change. I can think of

nothing but you--morn, noon, and night, I see you before I--morn,

noon, and night, I only hear your voice."



he look at she defiantly and see her stand silent and indignant

before he.



"cann't you understand?" he burst out again rapidly. "I love you--I

love you! from the first moment I see you I love you--I want you to

be my wife, will you be my wife Una."



Miss Challoner feel perplexed--this man have only know she a

fortnight, she have speak very little to he, yet here he be ask

she to marry he in a vehement, masterful manner which rouse within

she all the pride of womanhood.



"what you ask be impossible, Doctor Nestley," she say coldly and

deliberately. "I have only know you a fortnight and--beyond this I be

ignorant of your life in every way. I never dream that you would

speak to I in this manner."



"then you don't love I?" he cry in despair, "you cold perfection of

womanhood, you don't love I?"



Una would have reply indignantly, but she begin to see the nervous

excitable temperament of the young man and recognise that, be

under the influence of a strong emotion, he be not answerable for the

way in which he speak.



"no," she reply gently, "I cannot love you, Doctor Nestley--even if

I do, I could hardly respond to your passion after so short an

acquaintance; come, doctor, you have be wear out by your nightly

attendance on my cousin, you be not well and speak without think,

forget the word you have speak and let thing be as they be."



it be a gracious thing of she to say, for, in spite of his evident

earnestness, she feel indignant at the manner in which he have speak

to she.



"thing can never be as they be," he reply dully. "I have see you

and that have change my whole life--be there no chance?"



"there be no chance," she reply coldly, and turn away to intimate

the interview be over. even as she do so, he spring forward with a

fierce light in his eye.



"you love another," he hiss out between his clench tooth.



Una turn on he in a dignified way with her eye blaze with anger.



"how dare you speak to I in this manner?" she say wrathfully. "do

not try my patience too far--I have give you an answer to the mad

word you speak--now go."



she point to the door with a commanding gesture and the young man

droop his head on his breast, move towards it.



"you don't know what you be do," he say in a dreary voice. "you

be destroy my life; whatever evil now drag I down, it will be

your fault."



"a cowardly speech," she say in a clear, scornful voice; "because you

cannot get the toy you long for you speak like a child. I have nothing

to do with your life, if you yield to evil it will be through your own

weak will, not through any fault of mine--not a word," she go on as

he be about to speak; "leave I at once and I will try and forget

what you have say."



he try to look she in the face, but see she stand tall and

straight as a young greek maiden, with nothing but scorn and

condemnation in her eye, he turn away with a sigh, and let his

head fall on his breast walk slowly out of the room, careless of

what happen to he now that he have place all his chance on the

casting of a die--and lose.









CHAPTER XVIII.

the fall of MAN.





     who stand so high that he may never fall,

     who lie so low that he may never rise?

     the lowly may one day win life's prize,

     the high thro' temptation lose his all.





Beaumont be a man who neglect no chance, however small, by which he

could benefit himself; consequently think if he discover Squire

Garsworth's secret it might prove of use to he, he determine to find

out all about it. he know perfectly well that no power of persuasion

would lead the madman to divulge his thought, so the only chance of

discover anything be to reduce he to a mere automaton, perfectly

powerless in his hand. this he hope to do by mean of hypnotism, of

which curious process he know a good deal.



while in Germany, some year before, he have by accident come across

Heidenheim's book on animal magnetism, which interest he so much

that he pursue the subject. after read the opinion of Grützner,

Berger and Baumler on hypnosis, he turn his attention to french

authority, eagerly follow the history of animal magnetism from

Mesmer and Puységur downward, and lead by such study to try his hand

on subject, he become quite an adept in this strange psychological

science. take it up at first merely as an amusement, on go deep

into the subject he soon see that such hypnotic power would be a

terrible weapon in the hand of an unscrupulous man, as, by reduce

the hypnotised person to the condition of a mere instrument, it

enable he to do act through such instrument for which he himself

could not be hold legally responsible.



in a book on the subject by MM. Demarquay and Giraud Teulon, entitle

"Recherches sur L'Hypnotism," he have come across a case in which a

lady in a condition of hypnotic hallucination begin to tell aloud

secret which compromise she exceedingly. take this case as an

illustration of what could be do during hypnosis, Beaumont

determine to throw the squire into a cataleptic trance, and by

question or suggestion lead he to reveal his secret. this being

do, he could restore he to his normal condition, absolutely

ignorant of his revelation, and he think if the secret be worth

anything, he could then do what he please.



having thus definitely settle his plan of action, the next step to

take be to guard against the possibility of Nestley surprise he in

any of his hypnotic experiment, with which, as medical attendant of

the squire, he would have a perfect right to interfere. although

Nestley have become much more friendly with Beaumont, he still regard

he with a certain amount of suspicion, so the artist's aim be now to

reduce he to the state of subjection in which he have be in London

five year before.



he know Nestley be a very clever man, but remarkably weak, and likely

to be lead astray. in London, under the influence of drink, he have be

a slave to Beaumont, and here in Garsworth the artist determine to

reduce he to a similar state of slavery. never for a moment do he

think of the clever brain he would destroy, or the life he would

wreck--all he want be the assistance of the young doctor in certain

plan beneficial to himself, and, at whatever cost, he determine to

carry they out. Beaumont, as a matter of fact, have in he a great deal

of the italian Despot nature as describe by Machiavelli, and with

cold, relentless subtlety, set himself to work to ruin the unhappy

Duncan Nestley body and soul for his own end.



Nestley be doubtless weak to allow himself to be so dominate, but

unhappily it be his nature. if nature endow a man largely in one

way, she generally deprive he of something else in equal proportion,

and while Nestley be a brilliant, clever man, who, if leave to

himself, would have live an honest and creditable life, yet his

morally weak nature place he at the mercy of any unscrupulous

scoundrel who think fit to play upon his feeling.



unhappily, circumstance aid Beaumont's nefarious plan, for after

leave Una the young doctor walk across the common to the village,

hope to pull himself together by a brisk walk.



at the bridge he find Beaumont lean over it, look at the water

swirl below, and on hear footstep, the artist look up with a

gratified smile as he recognise his victim.



"what's the matter, Nestley?" he ask after the first greeting; "you

don't look well."



"I'm not well," retort Nestley abruptly; "I'm nearly wear out by

that old man--morn, noon and night I've get to be beside he--if he's

pay I handsomely he's take his full value out of I."



"yes, I think he be," reply Beaumont deliberately, "you look quite

thin--not the man of three week ago. he must be a kind of mediæval

succubus live on the blood of young man. it would be wise for you to

leave he."



Nestley lean his chin on his fold arm, which be rest on the

parapet of the bridge, and sigh deeply.



"no--I cann't do that."



"oh! I understand," say Beaumont with a sneer, begin to smoke one

of his eternal cigarette.



"what do you understand?"



"why you willn't leave the Grange."



"there's no difficulty in guess that," retort Nestley angrily,

"my medical---- what the deuce be you grin at?"



"you, my friend," say Basil smile, "your

medical--what!--honour--knowledge--interest--what you like."



"don't talk rubbish."



"as you please."



"look here," say Nestley, turn round with a resolute frown on his

haggard face, "what be the reason I don't leave the Grange?"



"not be in your confidence I cann't say, but if I may guess, I

should think Una Challoner."



Nestley make a gesture of assent, and turn once more to gaze moodily

at the grey water of the river.



"if I only have the courage," he mutter harshly, "I would throw

myself into the water and end everything."



"More fool you," remark Beaumont cynically; "man have die from time

to time, and worm have eat they, but not for love. don't give

Rosalind's remark the lie."



"I've no doubt she love someone else," say Nestley bitterly.



"I've no doubt she do," reply Beaumont tranquilly, "but you seem

quite wear out between love and sickness, so come with I to the inn

and have something to eat."



"I don't mind," say Nestley listlessly, "but I cann't eat a thing."



"don't give way so easily, my dear fellow," say Beaumont scornfully,

as they walk along; "be a man, not a baby."



"you're not in love."



"true, oh king; but I've have the disease badly enough--it's all dead

and do with now. I've leave Venus for Plutus, and I think Mercury,

the god of trickster, have some of my worship."



Dr. Nestley make no remark, be occupy with his own sad thought,

so Beaumont say nothing more, and they walk along to the inn

silently. on arrive there they go into the parlour, and Nestley

take his seat near the window, stare idly out at the dusty road,

while Beaumont order a slight luncheon, and a bottle of champagne.



Job Kossiter's idea of wine be a very vague one, as he himself

habitually drank beer, but in deference to Beaumont's wish, he send

over to Duxby and obtain a few dozen case of champagne, whose

excellence satisfy even the fastidious artist. the table be lay

and the luncheon bring in, Beaumont fill two tumbler with

champagne, one for himself, and the other he place by Nestley's

plate. the young doctor, be wrap up in gloomy thought, do not

perceive this, and, when he take his seat at the table, have no idea

that the glass at his elbow contain wine instead of water. he try

to eat two or three mouthful of food, but not succeed, take up the

glass to drink, and so preoccupied be he that it be not until he have

swallow a mouthful that he perceive what it be. replace the

glass on the table immediately, he glare angrily at Beaumont, who,

feign not to observe his annoyance, go on eat his luncheon

with great enjoyment.



"why do you give I champagne?" ask Nestley harshly. "you know I

only drink water."



"I know you're an idiot," retort Beaumont coolly, "and don't know

what's good for you. in your present state of health a glass of

champagne will do you no harm."



"you forget the harm drink have do I already."



"five year ago," say the artist mockingly. "you've be a

teetotaller for five year, so I think you be entitle to a little

indulgence now. go on, drink it up like a man."



"no," reply Nestley resolutely, and he turn his head away. "I will

not drink."



"very well," say Beaumont indifferently. "please yourself."



his unhappy friend look again at the amber-colour wine in the

glass, and feel half inclined to yield. after all, he have not touch

liquor of any sort for five long year, and do not feel as a rule

incline to take it, but now the night of watch by the bedside of

the old squire have wear he out physically, and the disdain of Una have

make he wretched mentally, so he half determine to take this one

glass to cheer he up. his good angel, however, come to his aid at

this critical moment, and turn his head away with a shudder, he

go on make a pretence of eat. Beaumont, who have watch he

narrowly all this time, see the struggle that be go on in the

young man's mind, but with true craftiness, pretend to take no

notice, satisfied that his victim be gradually be lure into the

snare so artfully lay.



"so you love Miss Challoner," he say genially. "well, I can hardly

wonder at that. to tell you the truth, I fall in love with she

myself--merely in an artistic sense, I assure you," add the astute

artist with a laugh as he see the anger in Nestley's face. "she have a

lovely face which seem to wear the calm of those old greek statue. I

should like to paint she as Artemis--the inviolate Artemis before she

love endymion--with the serene light of chastity on her face and the

sweetness of night in her eye. it would be a wonderful picture."



"I wonder you don't ask she to be your model," growl Nestley,

sulkily.



"hardly worth while, for two reason," reply Beaumont lightly, yet

with a suspicion of regret in his tone. "in the first place she would

refuse, and in the second, my hand have lose its cunning. one need to

be young and enthusiastic to paint a classical picture. I be of too

earthy a nature to have such hopeless vision. well, be you go to

play the part of young endymion to this moon goddess?"



"no," answer Nestley bitterly, "she willn't have anything to do with

I."



"poor endymion!"



"don't be a fool, talk such classical stuff! I tell you I'm madly

in love with she, and she willn't have anything to do with I.

everything be against I. I'm poor, unloved and obscure. life ben't

worth live under such condition."



he look again at the sparkle wine, which seem to invite he to

try it as an anodyne for his pain. everything seem to his distort

imagination to be dull and dark. Wine would at least give he a few

hour' respite from these torture thought. he be master of himself

now. he would drink one glass and no more. after all, see that

everything be lose, what do it matter if he do fall once more? he

have nothing to live for now. a wild despair take possession of his

heart, and with a reckless laugh he seize the glass and finish the

wine to the last drop.



"_Evohé Bacchus_," say Beaumont, drain his glass. "there's nothing

like wine to cure a broken heart."



the insidious wine mount rapidly to the excitable brain of the young

man, and he no long feel regret at break the pledge he have make

five year before. the humdrum past of struggle and respectability be

do with. Wine would solace he. drink! who care for such a thing?

Anacreon be the head of a glorious band of poet, and praise the

wine. Wise Anacreon, he know the true virtue of the grape. the past

be dead, the future be uncertain. live--live only in the present, with

wine to make we as gods--_Evohé Bacchus_.



the stimulating wine have perform its work excellently, and the world

hitherto so gloomy now appear of a roseate tint.



"a broken heart!" he repeat, with a gay laugh. "Pish! heart don't

break so easily. a woman's no means yes. I'll ask again."



"nothing like perseverance," say Basil, observe with infinite joy

the flushed face and bright eye of the young man. "have some more

wine?"



"rather!" reply Nestley, hold out his glass, which Beaumont

fill. "I be a fool to give up this for water. I'm sick of total

abstainer--thin-blooded croaker. here's confusion to they!" and he

drink off the second glass.



Beaumont now see that his victim be in that obstinate stage of

recklessness which could not brook contradiction, so know well how to

proceed.



"well, we've finish the bottle," he say brightly. "suppose we go

out for a walk."



"no--no walk," return Nestley, with an imbecile grin. "you've stand

I a bottle. now it's my turn."



"I don't want any more," say Beaumont indifferently, "and I think

you've have enough also."



"I haven't," retort Nestley defiantly. "I'm as straight as a die. I

suppose you willn't drink with I?"



"oh yes, I will, if you insist upon it."



"I do insist," cry the doctor, bring his fist down on the table

with a bang. "you must drink to show there be no ill-will. we be

once friend, Basil."



"and be so still, I trust," say the artist, cordially.



"your hand," say Nestley, with an outburst of maudlin affection.

"give I your hand."



Beaumont suffer his hand to be shake violently by the doctor, and

then that gentleman, now in a hilarious state of excitement, walk to

the bell, ring it with unnecessary violence.



Margery appear in answer, and seem somewhat astonished at

Nestley's state, as he have always be so reserved and quiet in his

demeanour.



"another bottle of champagne," say Nestley in a thick voice, come

close to she. "you be a pretty girl."



he try to embrace she, but Margery, who be use to see the

rustic in a similar state, push he away with a hearty laugh, and

go off to get the wine.



Nestley resume his seat at the table, talk rapidly to Beaumont

about all sort of thing, and then begin to boast about himself.



"I can do anything--anything, I tell you," he say, look at

Beaumont, who be smoke. "my brain's worth a dozen of any other

fellow' don't you believe I?"



"oh, yes, I believe you," reply Beaumont, as Margery return with

another bottle of champagne; "but, if I be you, I'd take no more

wine."



"willn't I!" say Nestley in a defiant manner. "you'll see."



Margery retreat, laugh at the maudlin condition of the young man,

and fill his tumbler up to the brim with wine, he drink it off with

an air of drunken bravado. Beaumont, with a sneer on his thin lip,

sit calmly watch the grotesque antic of the man he have bring so

low, and only take a little of the second bottle. Dr. Nestley sing and

laugh and boast till his leg begin to get shaky, and then he sit

down and finish the rest of the bottle, thereby reduce himself to

a state of hopeless intoxication.



finally he fall asleep with his head on the table, whereupon Beaumont,

not without some difficulty, wake he up and half lead, half drag

he to the sofa. with noisy protest that he be all right, the

unhappy young man lie down, and in a few moment fall into a drunken

slumber, while Beaumont, feel no compunction at having reduce a

human be to the level of the beast, stand over he with a sneer.



"I don't think you'll give I much trouble," he say serenely. "you've

start on the downward path once more, and this time I expect you'll

never get back again."



he go out, calmly smoke his cigarette, and ask Margery to let no

one disturb his friend.



"he's take more than be good for he," he say apologetically.



"oh bless you, sir, that's nothing," return Margery stolidly. "a

sleep will put he all right."



"will it?" say Beaumont to himself when he be stand in the bright

sunshine. "a sleep will never put you all right again in this life,

Duncan Nestley."









CHAPTER XIX.

JAM, JAM EFFICACI DO MANUS SCIENTIAE.





                    I use no charm,

     ephesian letter, philtre, spell or rune,

     nor aught of necromantic devilry

     yet thro' the power of new-find science

     before my searching gaze I bare your soul

     and read the secret longing writ thereon.





owe to Nestley's skilful treatment the squire soon recover from

his illness, but the fact of twice be lay upon a bed of sickness

within a few week, show how susceptible his constitution have become

to the slight ailment, and how rapidly any such ailment might

terminate with fatal result.



to a young and vigorous frame such slight indisposition would be

comparatively unimportant, but the weak body of the old man, with its

wear-out-organization, be able to develop these disorder in a most

alarming tanner. the flame of life be very feeble, and it be only by

the utmost watchfulness that it could be keep alive at all.



in spite of his settle conviction regard incarnation in a new

body, the squire seem remarkably loth to leave his old one, and

obey the doctor's order in a most slavish manner, dread lest by

some chance his soul should slip away into the next world. he have

accumulate a large fortune, which accord to his delusion he hope

to enjoy when his soul have become incarnate in a new body, so he have

no trouble on that score. his great desire be now to get his portrait

finish, and to this end, in spite of his ill-health, he insist

upon leave his bed and sit to Beaumont accord to his regular

custom.



Basil having once more bring Nestley under his dominating will,

determined to proceed at once in his hypnotic experiment, and at this

final sitting judge it an admirable time to carry out his idea. all

he want be an opportunity to introduce the subject without rouse

the squire's suspicion, and the old man, during their conversation,

speedily afford he an opportunity of do so.



they be in the drawing-room as usual, and the squire, look more

wrinkle and wear than ever, be seat in his arm-chair, while the

artist dexterously put a line here and there on the paint face

before he.



"you don't seem well this morning, Mr. Garsworth," say Beaumont, as

the old man move wearily in his chair.



"no, sir, I don't," retort the squire in his harsh voice. "I don't

expect I'll leave my bed again when I once go back to it."



"oh, thing be surely not so bad as that."



"I'm afraid they be," reply Garsworth, shake his head. "I be

anxious to go into a new body and leave this wear-out frame with its

incessant pain."



"be you in pain now?" ask Beaumont, sympathetically.



"yes--I have a bad attack of neuralgia--the east wind always affect

I more or less that way."



"I think I could do you some good."



"nonsense--you're not a doctor?"



"I be not the rose, but I've live near it, my dear sir," say

Beaumont equably, "and I know something of therapeutic."



"a little knowledge be a dangerous thing," reply the old man

sneeringly.



"I can reply with another proverb," say Basil smile. "a drown

man will clutch at a straw--so take I as your straw and see what I

can do--I cannot cure you of your neuralgia, but I can give you some

relief."



"in what way?"



"by hypnosis."



"Bah!--Mesmer and charlatanism."



"not at all--I have study the subject, and I assure you there be

more truth in it than you imagine. Mesmer be not altogether a

charlatan remember--he be wise than Cagliostro."



"well--well--what do you propose to do?"



"hypnotise you."



"and then?"



"well--the neuralgia will go away after you've be in the trance some

time, then I'll wake you and you can retire."



"but the portrait?"



"it willn't affect the portrait in the least--I-can go on painting and

you will be free from pain."



the squire hate pain, and be moreover very curious to test

Beaumont's knowledge, so he consent to the idea.



"go on, sir," he say grimly. "I don't object."



Beaumont nod carelessly, delight thus to have gain his end, and

produce from his pocket a facetted piece of glass, he arise from his

seat and walk over to the old man.



take up his position at one side of the chair he hold the glitter

object just above the squire's forehead.



"look steadily at this," he say in a quiet tone, and on Garsworth

do so he wait silently for the result, which soon take place. the

eye become humid and brilliant, the gaze fix and the pupil

dilate, until the old man fall into a cataleptic state. as the glass

facet be still hold in front of his eye he soon pass into a

lethargic condition and fall backward in his chair with a sigh.



Beaumont take the glass away with a feeling of relief, as he doubted

be able to produce the hypnotic sleep so easily. he have now at his

command a will-less automaton who would do what ever he be tell. but

this be not what Beaumont desire, as he be unable to suggest the

secret to the helpless man before he, and without suggestion the

automaton would not do anything. he want to change this lethargic

sleep into a somnambulistic state, so that he could have the memory,

the intelligence, and the imagination of the squire at his command.

this he achieve by slightly rub his hand to and fro for a few

minute across the top of the head, and in obedience to the feeling

produce by this Garsworth rapidly pass into a state of active

somnambulism.



he arise from his chair, look quickly from right to left, while

Beaumont speak to he, and during the conversation that follow, be

in a state of perpetual movement. all that Beaumont have now to do be

to suggest thing to the somnambulist which would engender train of

thought, and these train of thought would be speedily act upon by

volition.



the tall figure in black sway rapidly to and fro while Beaumont

speak in a clear, deliberate manner, suggest the question he

want to be answer.



"you have a secret?"



"I have a secret," assent the somnambulist, in the same slow manner.



"you have arrange a certain affair so that you will be able to enjoy

your present fortune during your next incarnation?"



"yes."



"you think you have arrange everything necessary to carry out this

idea?"



"I think so."



"state to yourself the whole scheme so that you can see you have

forget nothing."



Garsworth remain silent for a moment, then begin to talk rapidly.



"I have arrange everything in a proper manner. I be sure I have

forget nothing. my will have be make some year, and in it I have

leave all my property to my natural son. such natural son do not

exist--at present he be a fictitious person. when I be re-incarnate

he will become a reality. I will be my own natural son, and the property

will pass to myself in the new body by the action of my will in this

present body. it will be necessary for I in my new form to prove

myself the person mention in the will. I do this in such new body by

produce a certain paper and my seal ring, which I have safely hide

away. retain my memory during my next incarnation I go to the

hiding-place, find the paper and the ring, produce they to the lawyer

who hold my will, and having prove my identity as natural son, can

become possessed of the property. yes, everything be all right."



he cease speak and Beaumont, having listen attentively, be much

strike with the ingenuity of the idea express in the delusion. this,

then, be the way in which he hope to carry out his scheme. be ever

madman so whimsical? the artist do not see much chance of benefit

by the discovery so far, still if he see the paper mention by the

squire, there might be something in they which would prove useful.

yes; he would get the squire to show he the hiding-place of the

paper.



"your scheme be perfect," he say slowly, "but some one may find the

hiding-place and steal the paper?"



"no, no," reply the somnambulist, in an exult tone. "no chance of

that. I've hide it too well."



"go and see if it be safe."



"safe! safe! be that paper safe?" mutter the old man, with a frown.

"I must see. I must see. but how can I go? I be too weak."



Beaumont instantly exert his power by suggestion.



"you be very strong. go at once and examine the paper."



ordinarily the squire use a crutch to walk with, but on hear the

remark about his strength from his hypnotiser, he at once became

imbue with the hallucination that he be physically a vigorous man,

and walk towards the door of the drawing-room with rapid, springy

step, follow by Beaumont.



the somnambulist lead the way up the stair, pause for a moment on

the first landing, then, turn round, walk towards the front of

the house on the first floor. at this moment Patience Allerby come out

of one of the room, and see the squire walk in such a rapid

manner, and Beaumont follow, look at they both in alarm.



"where be go sir?" she cry, as Garsworth brush past she, and,

put out her hand, try to grasp he. the slight touch she give

he appear to cause the somnambulist suffering and break the

hypnotic spell, for he pause at once. alarm lest the old man should

awake, Beaumont grip Patience by the wrist and drag she back

quickly.



"you be go for your paper?" he say to Garsworth.



"I be go for my paper," repeat the squire slowly, and then, in

obedience to the impulse engender, go on again. Patience would

have speak, but a devilish look on Beaumont's face seem to freeze

her blood.



"be silent," he say in a harsh whisper, shake her wrist. "I will

tell you all soon, but now be silent for your son's sake."



she wrench herself free and shrink back into the shadow with a cry,

while Beaumont, take no further notice, quickly follow the squire

who be now some distance ahead.



Garsworth open a large folding-door that stand a short distance away

from the stair and which lead into the ball-room of the Grange.

follow by the artist he go into the long, bare room, which

stretch nearly the whole length of the front wing of the house,

be light by eight large window, look out on to the park.



the room be chill and bleak, every footfall awake a responsive echo

and leave a mark on the grey dust that have accumulate on the floor

for many year. the wall opposite the door be adorn with

delicately-paint panel, represent the nine muse, each female

figure be twice life-size and rise from the floor to the arched

roof, between each of the eight window. at one end of the room the

panel represent the three Graces, at the other the three Fates,

while the remain wall display nine goddess of heathen

mythology. the arched roof be paint a deep blue, silver with

star, but nowhere appear any male form--nothing but the gracious

female figure of Hellas be to be see around.



the squire go straight to the extreme corner of the room, on the

leave hand of the door, and kneel down where there be a panel

represent Clotho spin the thread of life. he evidently touch

a spring conceal in the gold-emboss frame of the panel, for it

silently slide back, display a wall of rough stone. the upper block

of stone appear heavy and cumbersome, but the low one be much

small, and as Beaumont look he see Garsworth drag from its place a

smallish stone in the low centre of the wall, display only the

rough place where it lay, but no cavity where anything could be hide.

the squire, however, soon show how ingenious be the hiding-place he

have choose, for on turn round the stone which he have take out,

there appear a small hole hollow out and from this the old man

take a paper and a ring. he lay they down for a moment to lift the

stone off his lap, but at this moment Beaumont, exert his hypnotic

power, say abruptly:



"you be look at the paper."



under the influence of the hallucination produce, the squire look

earnestly at the stone on his lap, while Beaumont, pick up the real

paper, glance over it rapidly, examine the ring, then lay they both

down again by the somnambulist.



"you should put they back," he suggest distinctly. Garsworth pick

up the paper, and replace it in the stone, put it once more in its

former position, and then drag the panel along till it click on

the spring, thus resume its former appearance. no one, to look at

it, would think that such a large picture could be move in any way,

and even if the secret of the panel be discover, Beaumont feel

sure no one would think of examine the interior of the stone in the

wall. having now ascertain all he want to know, Beaumont's next

care be to get the squire back to his former position and wake he,

so that he would be unconscious of what he have do during his

hypnotic sleep. to this end he bend forward to the kneeling figure on

the floor.



"Mr. Beaumont be wait to finish your picture."



"yes, yes. I must have the picture do," say Garsworth, and, rise

to his foot, he leave the room, follow by Beaumont, who see the white

face of Patience peer from the shadow and frown at he in a

menacing manner.



place his finger on his lip to enforce silence, he glide past she

down the wide stair, across the hall and into the drawing-room, where

he find the squire have once more re-establish himself in his chair.



"well," say Beaumont to himself, "there seem to be some chance of

make use of this secret, but I cann't do it without the help of

Patience, so I must see she. meanwhile, I'll wake the squire."



he cross over to the squire and touch his face with his own cold

hand, upon which the old man start violently.



he then speak loudly into his ear:



"Mr. Garsworth!"



the somnambulist open his eye, and a confused expression appear

on his face as he look at Beaumont.



"do you feel well?" ask the artist, gently.



"yes," answer the squire, slowly pass his hand over his forehead.

"the pain be go, but I feel very tired."



"it's always the case in hypnotism."



"how long have I be asleep?"



"about a quarter-of-an-hour," reply Beaumont, glance at his watch.

"be you dream at all? hypnotism usually produce dream."



"Aha!" say Garsworth, cunningly, "I be dream of my secret. I do

not speak in my sleep, do I?" he ask, in sudden terror.



"no, you be perfectly quiet," answer the artist, go back to his

seat.



"I feel too tired to sit any more," observe Garsworth, rise with a

great effort. "I must lie down. hypnotism seem to exhaust the body

very much."



"it do, of course; it act physically."



the squire, with the aid of his stick, move painfully to the door,

leave Beaumont smile at the picture before he.









CHAPTER XX.

when in DOUBT, play TRUMPS.





     Life be a game

     the keen win.

     Repute or shame.

     Life be a game;

     we give or claim

     for virtue, sin;

     life be a game

     the keen win





Beaumont be perfectly satisfied with the result of his experiment, as

he have discover the squire's secret, and have yet succeed in

keep he in ignorance of his having do so. with the keen

intellect of a man accustom to live by his wit, he have, during his

rapid survey of the paper, see the chance of turn the secret to

his own advantage. but to do so he require the co-operation of

Patience, and this he be doubtful of obtain.



she hold studiously aloof from he, and since the interview in the

churchyard have give no sign that she be aware of his existence. many

man would have be discourage by this contemptuous silence; but not

so Beaumont, who never see discourtesy in anyone of whom he want to

now, since his discovery of the existence of her son, and since he have

his end--end calculate to benefit himself alone.



the only way by which he could hope to gain her ear be through her

put away his painting utensil, he light a cigarette, and

appeal to her motherhood, which appeal, he well know, would not be

neglect by this woman, whose whole life be devoted to her son. Mr.

very much. so, in this case, be somewhat doubtful of Patience, yet

hold a strong hand, he take an illustration from his favourite

"when in doubt, play trump."



"it will be a charming game," he murmur, as he knock at the door

of the housekeeper's room, "she be no mean adversary, and hate I

statuesque by the window, stare out at the rapidly darken

"I hope you don't mind my cigarette," he say, languidly, "but I cann't

exist without smoking."



"you cann't exist without all sort of luxury," reply Patience,

"I have to deny myself a good many thing when we be starve in

you about London."



"and I want to speak to you about the squire," she retort, quickly.

"what be you do follow he upstairs?"



"don't distress yourself, my good soul," say the artist, in a coolly

talk of Chelsea."



"no."



"Pardon I--yes. do you remember how we live there, you and I, and

the vision we use to indulge in? I haven't forget it, I assure

the boy her surname."



"and what if I do?" she flash out fiercely, with a deep frown on

could I give he mine--the mother to whom his birth be a disgrace?"



"a disgrace! I think you love he?"



"so I do--I love he more than my life; but his birth be a disgrace,

and I wish to keep the knowledge from he, please God."



"be the boy you call Reginald Blake ever christen?"



"no."



"why not?"



"because I could not tell the truth about his birth, and I refuse to

tell a lie. he be neither christen, nor be his birth register."



"then he have no right to the name he bear."



"I know that. whose fault be it, Basil Beaumont--your or mine? why

don't you make an honest woman of I?"



"because I do not choose to," he reply, coolly; "by the way, have

our son be confirm?"



"no."



"oh," he say, sneer, "I'm sorry he's not get some religious

flavour about he. I wonder, Patience, when you call he Blake, you

do not pass he off as Fanny's son."



she arise from her seat in a fury.



"do you think I be go to place my sin on Fanny's shoulder?"



"I don't see why not--Fanny and yourself both come up to London at the

"there be no reason."



"not then; but there be now, and a very excellent reason--ten thousand

a year."



"what do you mean?"



"simply this, that Reginald Blake, from this time forward, be the son

of Fanny Blake and Randal Garsworth."



Patience look at he in surprise, and involuntarily draw back a

step, think he mad. Beaumont see this, and laugh mockingly.



"don't be afraid--there's method in my madness."



"there's some villainy in it," she say, with a hard smile, sit

touch a hair of my son's head I'll punish you."



"I intend to give he ten thousand a year, if you willn't be a fool."



she smile coldly, and fold her hand upon her lap.



"I'm no fool, but I know you--go on, Ananias."



Beaumont fling the burn-out cigarette into the fire with an irritable

gesture, and turn his face towards the frigid woman seat before

"listen to what I've get to say," he say slowly, "and then you can do

as you please--if you assist I it mean money and happiness for our

son; if you don't, I'll tell he everything, and then leave the

village for ever."



Patience shiver slightly under the steely glitter of his eye, and

then resume her cold impassive manner.



"brag's a good dog," she say mockingly, "but he do not bite--go on,

I'm all attention."



the artist glance at the door to make sure that it be closed, then

draw his chair close to that of Patience Allerby, begin to talk

rapidly, in a low tone of voice.



"of course you know the squire be mad--quite mad--he have an idea that

his soul will be re-incarnate in another body, and as he be afraid he

may be bear poor, he have invent a silly scheme by which to become

about it--his idea be to pass himself off as his own son."



"but he have no son."



"of course not, you fool," say Beaumont impatiently, "he couldn't

"I understand, so far; but how do the squire, in a new body, expect

"oh! he think he'll remember about the affair when he be bear again,

"what a foolish idea."



"what a foolish remark, you mean," say Beaumont; "of course it's

foolish, the man be mad. when he die the paper will remain

"what do you mean?"



"simply this--as he do not know when or where he'll be

re-incarnated, he have leave a number of blank in the letter."



"have you see the letter?"



"of course I have. I know where the paper be hide--didn't I tell you

I'd discover his secret. well, all I've get to do be to fill up

birth, and all the rest of it."



"I see. but what have I to do with this?"



Beaumont arise to his foot and walk angrily to and fro.



"what an idiot you be, Patience," he say irritably. "cann't you see?

I'm go to fill up the mother's name as Fanny Blake, and the son's

as Reginald."



"our son?"



"precisely. now do you see why I want your help?"



"I do, but you shalln't have it."



"indeed; why not?"



"I'm not go to have such a sin on my conscience."



"there's no sin, you Puritan," he say quickly, "the re-incarnation

"it would dispossess Miss Una."



"it would do nothing of the sort--under the will Miss Una cannot

"Miss Una could dispute the will on the ground of madness."



"I dare say she could, but she won't--if Reginald become master of

Patience think for a moment or two and then look up.



"how do you know Reginald will marry Una?"



"because I've get eye in my head. the boy be madly in love with she.

I'm sure you must see that your help I to place Reginald in

"I see that, but I fail to see what benefit you obtain from it, and I

don't think you're the man to work for nothing."



"you're perfectly right," he reply calmly, "but I'm go to make

"and ruin he."



"I willn't ruin he. why should I want to ruin my own son?"



"Bah! don't talk like that to I."



"oh well, if you disbelieve in interest, I'll put it another way. why

should I kill the goose with the golden egg?"



"yes, that's more like it," she say with a sneer, "I think your plan

be an admirable one, but there's one obstacle."



"what be it?"



"Reginald be an honourable man, and willn't accept any property gain

Beaumont sigh in a resign manner, apparently hopeless of

"still, he think he's be bear in lawful wedlock, and to undeceive
