"give he ten thousand a year," interrupt Beaumont coolly. "well,

what do you say, will you help I?"



"I'll tell you to-morrow."



"why not to-day?"



"because I don't trust you, I want to go over the affair in my own

mind."



Beaumont shrug his shoulder, put on his hat and light another

cigarette.



"just as you please," he say, pause a moment at the door. "I'll

call and see you to-morrow; but if you don't help I in this, I'll do

what I say and tell Reginald everything."



when he be go Patience sit for a long time look into the fire,

evidently ponder deeply. at length she sigh and mutter:



"I don't know what to do, I must ask counsel of the Lord."



she arise, and having light a candle open the Bible.









CHAPTER XXI.

the good SAMARITAN.





Man be but a whimsical animal at good, for there be no life but what

may some day act in discord with its theory.





Patience Allerby occupy a very peculiar position, and know that she

do so, much to her perplexity. ever since her lapse from virtue she

have live a self-deny existence as an expiation for her sin, and

she have cause to be satisfied with the past twenty year of her life,

see that she have do nothing wrong all the time. true, live in

almost monastic existence, she have no temptation to fight against,

and the absence of temptation render it comparatively easy to lead a

virtuous life. not be tempt she live an ascetic life, be

absolutely certain that she be strong enough to withstand any

temptation, however powerful. vain hope, for now the devil in the

person of her old betrayer assault she on her weak side. have he

try to make she rob her master or return to her old life of sin, he

would have fail dismally, but an appeal through her motherhood be

perilous to her strength, and Beaumont know this when he use her love

for Reginald as a weapon against she. in spite of her prayer, her

tear, her comfort text, she know that if Beaumont wish she to

commit any crime to benefit her son she would do so in defiance of her

religious belief, however strong.



God alone know the night of anguish this woman pass, wrestle with

the subtle temptation place before she in such an attractive shape.

those old saint, who, accord to pious legend, fight with the

visible power of evil, have no such terrible enemy to cope with, in

contrast to a soul rack with doubt fight against spiritual

prompting.



in vain this poor soul, who wish to do right, close her ear to the

infernal whispering of evil spirit, in vain she read with frenzied

ardour the terrible prophecy of Isaiah or the comfort promise of

the Gospels, in vain she kneel weep bitterly before the crucifix,

pray to be guard from fall into sin. it be all useless.

either spiritual weapon have lose their efficacy, or her intense

maternal passion blunt her sense of religious duty, and after a

terrible struggle with her invisible enemy, which leave she

completely prostrate, she begin to calmly consider Beaumont's scheme.

from that moment she be lose; for, on review the whole matter she

begin to pacify her conscience with argument concern the rectitude

of the affair.



she would be do no wrong to anyone--nay, she would be confer a

benefit on Una, see that by her marriage with Reginald she would be

put in possession of the property at once, whereas should the will be

carry out strictly she would have to wait everlastingly for the

appearance of a non-existing person. suppose she agree to Beaumont's

plan, and say Fanny Blake and the squire be the parent of her son,

he would become rich and honour, bear a renowned name and no

long be an unknown waif, heavily handicapped in the battle of life.



on the other hand he would learn the shame of his birth, and that

would cast an everlasting shadow on his young spirit. what

wealth--what position could compensate in his own eye for the moral

stigma thus cast upon he. he might succeed to the property, marry

Una, and thus do no harm to anyone, still, if he become a father, how

deeply would he feel for the sin of his parent be visit on his

offspring. no, she could not place he in such a position; well for

he to remain unknown and obscure, with a full belief in his

honourable birth, than go through life haunt by the spectre of an

intolerable disgrace.



while thus hesitate between these two view of the case, a sudden

idea come to she, which incline she to refuse to help Beaumont and

let the boy make his own life, ignorant of the stain on his name. the

squire, in spite of his miserly habit, have a kind heart. she would

ask he to give Reginald fifty or a hundred pound to help he, then

the lad could go to London and make a position by his vocal talent.

thus, he would benefit in no way through money unjustly obtain, and

Una, be in possession of the property, he could marry she and enjoy

it just the same as if the scheme be carry out. yes, it would be

the good way; he would at least never know who or what he be, and she

would thus assist he in life without commit a crime. the more she

think of the plan the well she like it, and fall on her knee

in the dark she thank God long and fervently for the solution he have

show she of the difficulty.



next morning she proceed to carry her idea into effect, for after

Miss Cassy and Una have pay their usual morning visit, she find

herself alone with the squire and in a position to make her request.



Garsworth be lie in bed, prop up by pillow, and look very

feeble indeed, so that Patience see the end could not be far off, in

spite of Nestley's care and attention.



after his recovery from his debauch, Nestley have feel bitter shame at

his fall, but having lose his self-respect by thus revert to his

old way, he try to drown remorse by drinking, and alcohol be

rapidly regain all its old influence over he. still he do not let

it interfere with his attendance on the squire, and if the old man see

that Nestley's hand be shaky, and his eye becoming blear, he say

nothing, and the unhappy young man perform his duty in a

mechanical way, drink deeply whenever an opportunity offer.



Nestley, look haggard and unsteady after his drinking of the

previous night, have just leave the room, leave Patience alone with

the Squire, when the old man speak sharply:



"Patience, what be the matter with the doctor?"



"drink!" she answer laconically.



"drink!" repeat the squire, raise himself on his elbow. "nonsense,

woman, you must be mistaken, he drink neither wine nor spirit."



"he never do until a week ago," answer Patience coolly, "he use to

be a total abstainer, but now--well, you can see for yourself."



the long connection that have exist between this strange couple as

master and servant, have develop between they a certain amount of

familiarity.



"I remember," say Garsworth musingly, "that in my last incarnation, I

drink ale very much--it be in the reign of Elizabeth, and we drank

confusion to the King of Spain--it result in confusion to myself. if

I have not be a drunkard, I would not have be a pauper; it's a pity

this young man should follow the same downward path."



"it's his own fault," reply Patience in a stony manner, "he ought to

stop when he find it do he harm."



"no doubt," return the old man acidly, "but do you ever know a man

deny himself anything if it do he harm?"



"you do."



"yes, because I have an object to gain. the life I lead in Town be very

pleasant, but it would have leave I a pauper for my next incarnation."



it be no use try to argue the old man out of his delusion, so

Patience say nothing, but stand beside he in grim silence with

fold arm.



"I'll enjoy myself when I'm bear again," pursue Garsworth exultingly.

"I will have plenty of money and a new body. I will have youth once

again. oh, youth! youth! how short be your golden hour. young man

never know the treasure they possess in youth, and waste it in

idleness and folly; there's that child you bring up, Reginald

Blake----"



"I do not bring he up."



"well, well," rejoin Garsworth testily. "you know what I mean, you

be his nurse--but he have youth, good look, health and talent--why

don't he go to London with such advantage, instead of waste his

life in a dull village?"



"he's get no money," retort Patience icily; "all you mention go for

nothing without money."



"no doubt, no doubt," mutter Garsworth, his eye gleam; "money be

a necessity--still he have talent, I hear."



"what can talent do?"



"everything; a clever brain command the world."



"I dare say," retort Patience ironically, "if it get money to give

it a start. Master Reginald have it in he to make a great name by his

voice, but he need help--the help of money--who will give he that

help?"



she eye the old man keenly as she speak.



"ah, who indeed?" he reply carelessly, "who indeed?"



"why not yourself?" say the housekeeper eagerly.



"I?" he ejaculate in surprise.



"yes, you," she retort vehemently. "I be as you say the nurse of

that boy. I have love he far more than his dead parent ever do;

they leave he to I, and I stand in his mother's place: it be my

dearest wish that he should succeed--with money he can do so. I have

serve you long and faithfully and ask no favour, but now that you

have mention his name, I ask this first and last favour of you, give

he money and help he to succeed."



"do you think I'm mad?" cry the old man shrilly. "why should I help

he? what be he to I? I have gather all my wealth by year of

self-denial. I want to enjoy it in my next existence, not squander it

in this by help a pauper."



"and yet you talk of the golden hour of youth," she reply bitterly.

"it's easy saying, but hard doing. what be a hundred pound to you?--a

drop in the ocean. what be it to him?--everything."



"I cann't part with my money," he say doggedly, turn his face away.



her voice take a tender tone as she plead for her son.



"he have no claim upon you, I know, but think of his youth, his

talent, waste in this dull village. you say you will remember in

your next body what you have do in this; for year you have never

do a kind action to a human being, do one now by help this lad,

and your next existence will be none the bad for having help an

unknown man."



the old man make no reply, but be clearly move by her argument.



"and again," say Patience, still in the same anxious voice, "with

your help he will make a position in the world. what position will

you occupy? with all your money, you may be bear a prince or a

ploughboy--you do not know--but in whatever station you be bear, his

influence, his friendship, may be a help to you, and it will be all

the more precious when you know it be your work."



the woman's voice die away in a soft manner, and she anxiously

watch the old man's wrinkle face to see if he would do what she

ask. evidently her word appeal either to his selfishness or good

nature, for, turn towards she, a smile spread over his crabbed

face.



"I'll do it, Patience," he say quickly. "I'll do it--perhaps he will

be of help to I in my next life--get I my cheque book, and I'll

write a cheque for fifty pound--no more--no more. I cann't afford it."



"fifty be no use--say one hundred," she urge eagerly.



"well, well! one hundred," he say peevishly, "it's a large sum, still

it may do good to I. I'll write a letter with it, and tell he he

must do what I ask in my next life. will he do that?"



"yes! yes!" she reply impatiently, in nowise affront by his

selfish motive. "he be not the man to forget a kind action."



"you don't thank I," he say angrily, as she go over to the

escritoire and get his cheque book. "grasp! ungrateful!"



"I'm not ungrateful," she retort, bring the pen and ink to he

with the cheque book, and a block of blot paper to write on, "but

I do thank you. I be never one for lip service."



"Bah! woman be all alike," he say viciously, sit up in bed, and

seize the pen. "go and bring I some letter paper and an envelope."



she do so, and return to his bedside by the time he have write the

cheque.



"I've post-date this cheque," he say cunningly, "because I willn't

send it to he till just before I die."



"what do you mean by post-date?"



"this be the twelfth," he reply, smooth out the letter paper, "I

have date it the thirtieth."



"how do you know you'll die then?"



"I don't know if I will, you fool," he retort angrily, "but I think

so--if I don't I'll write another cheque."



"yes, and change your mind."



"no--no--a promise be a promise--if he help I in the future I'll

help he now--be quiet, you cat, I want to write."



she remain silent, and very slowly and painfully the old man write a

letter, then he direct the envelope to Reginald Blake at the

Vicarage and place the letter and cheque therein. after do this he

close the letter and tell she to bring sealing-wax and his seal.



"what for?" she ask, go over to his desk.



"because I'm not go to let anyone but himself see what I have

write--you needn't be afraid--I will do what I say, look at the

cheque, you fool."



she have bring a candle to the bedside so that he could melt the wax

for the seal, and as he hold the cheque out to she she read it in the

dim light.



"it's all right," she say with a sigh of relief, "I thank you very

much."



"you needn't," he retort cynically, seal the letter with the

Garsworth arm. "I do it for my own sake not his; now put this letter

in the desk and let I see you do it."



he hand she his key, so take they and the letter over to the

desk, she deposit it in the place indicate by his lean,

outstretched finger, and having lock it safely up, blow out the

candle and bring the key back to he.



the Squire place they under his pillow, then lie down again with a

sigh of exhaustion.



"there, I've do what you ask," he say in a dull voice, "now go

away. I'll sleep a little."



Patience carefully tuck all the clothe round he and then leave the

room with a look of triumph on her face.



"now, Basil Beaumont," she say when she be outside the door. "I

think I can laugh at you and your threat about my son."









CHAPTER XXII.

PHANTASMAGORIA.





     shadow of what be shadow--live once

     now naught but ghost among a world of ghost.

     who know--we may but shadow be on earth

     and act the other life's reality.





Miss Cassy be greatly excited over the afternoon tea to which she have

bid Mrs. Larcher and the rest of the vicarage inmate. it be a

long time since she have take part in a little social festivity such

as she have be accustom to in London, so both herself and Una

determine it should be a success. in the dreary dismal life they lead

this be a little mild excitement, consequently, it be to they as

great an event as the ball of the season to a Town belle.



Reginald and Pumpkin walk over to the Grange, but Mrs. Larcher be

drive over in state by Dick Pemberton, who drive at such a speed that

he nearly rattle the vicar's wife into hysteric. consequently on

arrive at her destination, Mrs. Larcher be severely under the sway

of "The affliction" and have to be at once comfort with strong tea.

Cecilia have also be invite, and arrive at the Grange under the

guardianship of Miss Busky, who bounce the blind girl so rapidly

along the road that she enter the Park in a state of exhaustion.



the party all assemble in Una's private room, where they be shortly

afterwards join by bluff Dr. Larcher and Beaumont. jellick, having

wriggle in with the tea-cake and muffin, be dismiss altogether,

as Mrs. Larcher, under the influence of "the affliction," declare the

old woman make she feel creepy.



"she's so twisty, my dear," she observe to Una, "like a sea-serpent

you know--even the vicar have notice she."



"_Qui siccis oculis monstra natantia_," roar the vicar, quote from

his favourite poet, "though to be sure, I speak of she in the

singular."



"of course," say Dick slily, "she's singular in any case:"



"so very odd," giggle Miss Cassy, who be make the tea, "I don't

mean Jellicks, but what you say--pun you know--like what's his name,

Byron, have in his burlesque--not the Don Juan one you know, but the

other--so odd, ben't he?"



"not half so odd as Miss Cassy," whisper Dick to Reginald, but the

"I don't know if I ought to eat muffin," say Mrs. Larcher darkly, as

Gwendoline?"



"yes, often, Mama," assent the obedient Pumpkin.



"I hope you're well now?" observe Beaumont politely, see the

lady's eye fix upon he.



"ah, yes, now," sigh Mrs. Larcher, stir her tea, "but will

it last? the question be will it endure? my affliction be so

capricious--I'm very weak--quite a Hindoo."



"why a Hindoo, my dear?" ask the vicar, rather puzzle.



"because they be weak--die if you look at they," explain Mrs.

Larcher, "rice of course--they live on it and there's no nourishment

in it."



"by the way, Miss Challoner, how be the squire?" ask Beaumont, who

be lean up against the mantelpiece look rather bored.



"he's not at all strong," reply Miss Cassy, take the remark to

moment--but Dr. Nestley be do he good; but I don't think the dear

doctor be well himself."



Beaumont smile slightly at this, guess the cause of the doctor's

illness, and glance at Cecilia, see the blind girl be tremble

violently.



"I hope he be not very ill," she say in her low, clear voice.



"oh no--he'll be all right soon--I think it's overwork," say Una

hastily, anxious to avoid any discussion of the doctor's complaint,

the cause of which she, with her feminine shrewdness, half guess.

"Cecilia, will you play something?"



the blind girl assent, and be lead by Una to the quaint old spinet

which stand in the corner. with the true feeling of an artist Cecilia

do not play anything noisy on the delicate instrument, but a dainty

old gavotte which sound faint and clear like the sound of a silver

"I hope you like mine," observe Miss Cassy, look at the gown she

wear, which be of white muslin dot with pink bow. "I be afraid

I'd make it dabby--I'm afraid I have make it dabby--do you think so?"



Mrs. Larcher eye the production of Miss Cassy's artistic nature with

a critical eye, and pronounce her opinion that it be dabby, thus

reduce poor Miss Cassy to the verge of tear. when Cecilia finish

the gavotte all present urge she to play something else.



"it's like fairy music," say Beaumont. "I love to hear those old air

of Purcell and Arne play upon such an instrument. it's so thoroughly

in keep with the idea. the lyric in 'a Midsummer Night's Dream'

set to the old-fashioned music and play on a spinet, give one a

charming idea of the court of Oberon and Titania."



"and Miss Mosser play so charmingly," say Reginald, gaily.





     "'o testudinis aureæ

       Dulcem quæ strepitum Pieri tempera,'"





quote the vicar, in his rolling bass.



"I prefer the sweet harmony of the spinet to the lyre," say Beaumont,

smile.



"dear I, vicar," observe Mrs. Larcher angrily. "I wish you wouldn't

be always talk Latin. no one understand it."



"that's hardly a compliment to the gentleman present, my dear," say

Dr. Larcher in his most stately manner, "but, as Horace say, 'oh,

mater pulchra'----I beg your pardon, I will refrain from the bard."



"now, Mr. Blake, I want you to sing something," say Una, crossing

over to Pumpkin.



"certainly--some old English melody, I suppose, to match the spinet.

'Phyllida flout I,' or 'mistress mine where be you roam?'"



"let we have they both," say Beaumont, lazily. "very likely the

ghost of the old Elizabethan lyrist will come and listen."



"you'll see a real ghost shortly," say Una mysteriously, as she and

Pumpkin, after a whisper consultation, move to the door.



"the ghost of whom?" ask Reginald, who be stand by the spinet.



"Lady Betty Modish or Sophia Western--which ever you like--town or

country," reply Una, laughing, and thereupon vanish with Miss

Larcher.



"what do she mean?" demand the vicar in astonishment.



"something very odd," say Miss Cassy, shake her girlish head. "yes,

quite like a play. the School for what's-it's-name. Sheridan, you

know--quite lovely."



and now Reginald begin to sing the quaint old song "Phyllida flout

I," while Cecilia, who know the music off by heart, play the

accompaniment. the night be begin to close in, and the room be

full of shadow, light in a fantastic manner by the red glare of the

the sombre face look from the wall. Beaumont, lean his elbow

on the mantelpiece, listen quietly, while opposite to he the vicar,

ensconce in a great arm-chair, cross his leg and keep time to the

music with his spectacle.



so gay and charming the old song sound. nothing of the sickly

sentimentality of the modern drawing-room ballad--nothing of the

florid passion of the italian school--but all fresh and wholesome,

like a gentle wind blow freely over an english meadow, white with

daisy. Reginald sing the complaint of the unhappy lover charmingly,

and end amid a subdue murmur of satisfaction, even Mrs. Larcher

be pleased.



"so simple," she say, nod her head. "quite soothing, like a

cradle. ah, there be no song now-a-day like the old one."



"my dear, we be past the age of Corydon and Chloe," reply the

vicar. "Virgil and Horace would find no Arcady to sing about now."



"well, I don't suppose that Imperial Rome be more arcadian than

London," say Beaumont, lazily, "but I'm afraid we've lose the charm

of simplicity."



"ah, you've never hear 'Lady Bell,'" say Dick wisely.



"no. I must confess my ignorance," reply the artist. "who or what be

Lady Bell?"



"it's a song--simplicity, if you like. Reggy find it among some old

music at the vicarage."



"do he indeed?" observe the vicar placidly. "no doubt it belong to

my grandfather. I think that music be all burn. _Damnosa quid non

imminuit die?_"



"he spare this, sir, at all event," say Reginald gaily. "Miss

Mosser, you can play 'Lady Bell'?"



"yes, I think so," reply Cecilia, strike a chord. "it haunt I

when I first hear it. sing it now, Mr. Blake."



Whereupon she play a prelude of silvery-sound chord, and

Reginald sing the old ballad of "Lady Bell." how, despise all the

beaux, she give her heart to a plain young country squire, and leave

the delight of Ranelagh for the quiet of a village. so dainty and

crisp ring the music to the simple story with its arcadian end.





     "my Lady Bell in gold brocade,

      look not so fair or trim a maid

      as when in linsey woollen gown,

      she leave for love the noisy town."





and then the door open as Reginald end the delightful old song,

and surely on the threshold stand my Lady Bell as she appear at

Ranelagh, in powdered hair, in shimmer of gold brocade, with wide

hoop and patch on her arch-look face, with dainty red-heel

shoe and skilfully manipulate fan. it be surely Lady Bell that

step so stately into the room in the red glare of the fire to the

melodious clearness of the gavotte play by Cecilia, who, be

whisper to by Reginald, at once seize the spirit of the jest. or

perchance one of the old Garsworth dame have step down from her

gilt frame, and, attract by the familiar tinkle of the spinet, come

to look at what gay company be assemble in the oak parlour; but no,

it be to their eye Lady Bell, fair and dainty as of old, who sweep

into the firelight with tap of high heel and sweep of stiff

brocade.



"we must have light to see this," cry Dick, jump up from his

chair.



"no, no, I protest!" say Beaumont, lift up his hand. "it will

spoil all. this be not Miss Challoner, but Lady Bell--a ghost from the

day of powder and patch come to visit we. she move in mysterious

shadow--a light will cause she to melt away."



"I'm too substantial for that, I'm afraid," laugh Una, wave she

fan. "but ben't this a charming dress? I find it the other day, and

think I would give you all a fright."



"I don't think you could give any one a fright," whisper Reginald,

whereupon she flash a saucy look at he out of the shadow. the

sweet, clear music be still steal through the room, and Beaumont,

in his low, languid voice, talk idly.



"Lady Bell, I admire you vastly. how have you leave London and the

modish company at Soho? surely no highwayman stay you on the way

hither in your coach and six? and what of my Lord Mohun? be there any

news at Will's coffee-house, and do the belle admire the new opera of

Mr. Handel? come, tell we the news."'



"I would need to be a gazette to do so."



"and you be not--only a fair dead woman from the perish past, come

to show we what wit and beauty go out with powder and patch. ah,

my dear Lady Bell----"



at this moment he be interrupt, for a wild shriek ring through the

house, and all present spring to their foot, look at one another in

wild surmise.









CHAPTER XXIII.



the end of all thing.





     we may have die in be bear to earth

     perchance our dying be another birth.





the shriek be utter by Patience Allerby, and when the whole

party, recover from their surprise, go upstairs they find she

lean against the door of the squire's room, with pale face and

terrify-look eye. beyond, half see in the dim candlelight

which illuminate the room, lie a dark shapeless object on the floor.



there be no need to say what have happen, for in the air there be

that indescribable feeling which tell of the presence of the great

destroyer. leave Patience to the care of Beaumont, to whom she clung

with convulsive terror, Dr. Larcher reverently enter as he think

the chamber of death. he bend down to the form lie so still on the

floor, and turn the face to the light with tender hand. it be

ghastly pale, and from the thin lip there flow a thin stream of

blood; still the vicar see at a glance that life yet remain, so

call softly to Reginald and Dick, the three man lift the body up

gently and place it on the bed.



Beaumont have succeed in somewhat pacify Patience, and induce the

woman to go downstairs while he send for the doctor to examine the

sick man. they all re-assembled in the oak parlour, and terrify

face and subdue whisper take the place of merry look and jocund

laughter.



attract by the housekeeper's shriek, Dr. Nestley now enter the

room, and proceed to see what he could do towards revive the

squire. Beaumont glance keenly at he as he pass, but though his

face be pale and heavy-look, still he be perfectly sober. he

catch the artist scrutinise he, and draw himself up with an

angry frown, pass he by without a word.



"what be the matter, doctor?" ask the vicar anxiously, when the

young man have conclude his examination.



"Aneurism," he reply briefly. "the body be thoroughly

debilitate--he have burst a main artery."



"be it his heart?" ask Reginald.



"if he have burst any artery in the vicinity of the heart, he would

have die at once--even now he cannot live very long--I expect

this?"



"what produce the rupture?"



"some sudden emotion, I presume, or violent exercise--here come the

housekeeper; she will tell we all about it."



Patience, look pale but compose, and in answer to the

interrogatory of the doctor, tell the follow story:



"the squire be quietly sleep in bed," she exclaim calmly, "and I

fall asleep in the chair by the side of the bed--he must have arise

and go to his desk, for I be awaken by a fall, and see he lie

on the floor. I be so startled that I cry out and you come up--I

know nothing more."



owe to the remedy which Dr. Nestley be apply, the sick man now

revive, and moan feebly. shortly afterwards, open his eye he

stare wildly at the figure surround his bed, and try to speak,

but seem unable to make any sound beyond an indistinct murmur.



Dr. Larcher come close to the bed, and bend down speak distinctly

and slowly to the die man.



"you be very ill," he say in a pitying voice. "I hope you have make

your peace with heaven."



with a superhuman effort Garsworth raise himself on his elbow, and

stretch out his hand point to the desk.



"in there," he gasp. "Blake--there."



the effort be too much for he, for with a choking cry he fall back

on the bed a corpse.



Nestley, start to his foot, bent over the bed, and tear open the

squire's shirt, put his hand on his heart--it have cease to beat.



"he be dead," he say, in a coldly professional manner, "that last

effort kill he."



"dead!" echo Patience, who be lean against the curtain with

stare eye and a white terrify face.



"yes--dead," repeat Dr. Larcher gravely. "we can do no good now,"

and follow by Reginald and Dick he leave the room, wonder in his

own heart what the old man have mean by point at the desk while

pronounce Blake's name.



the melancholy news be convey to the terrified woman downstairs,

and shortly afterwards everyone depart, leave the inmate of the

Grange alone with its dead master. Una and Miss Cassy, stun by the

suddenness of the event, retire early to bed, and jellick, with the

help of Patience, lay the corpse out on the bed ready for the

undertaker. Nestley go to his own room and solace himself with

brandy; Patience remain by the side of the corpse to watch it during

the night, and over all the house there hang a shadow of fear and

dread which invest the place with awesome terror.



and that which once hold the soul of Randal Garsworth lie on the bed

under the heavily-drape canopy--a still white-faced form with the

dead hand cross on the dead breast, and on the white lip a

terrible smile. candle burn on each side of the body with a sickly

light, and a woman with her face bury in her hand knelt pray for

the dead man's soul.





"_oh God who art the Judge of all have mercy upon the soul of this

wretched man_."





not a breath of air in the vastness of the room, no sound, no blaze of

light--only the pale glimmer of the candle hollow out a gulf of

luminous light in the sombre blackness of the brood night.





"_oh God who art all powerful and just, let not the soul of this man

suffer for the sin of his life, for the mind which should have rule

the soul be a wreck and incapable of so rule_."





be there not a sneer upon the still feature of the dead man at this

prayer for his misspent, useless life--he that despise prayer and

only look upon his soul as useful to inhabit a new body so that he

could make it an instrument by which to enjoy the sensual thing of

this earth.



Midnight, and the wind be rise--with querulous voice it sweep

through the leafless tree and whistle through the chink and

cranny of the old house, make the dim light of the candle flicker

and flare in the dense darkness. no prayer now sound from the thin

lip of the watcher, for a sudden thought have dart through her

brain.





"_the letter for my son--I must get it from the desk_."





she rise softly from her knee, and put her hand under the pillow

whereon rest the head of the corpse, draw forth the key of the dead

man, hold her breath meantime, fearful lest he should arise and lay

cold hand upon she. the key chink musically in the silence, then

with stealthy stride and sound of sweep dress, she cross to the

desk, bent on obtain the letter write by the squire to Reginald

Blake.



the minute slowly pass, and the wind be still rise; now howl

furiously round the house, shake the shutter and flutter the

curtain as though wroth at witness the sacrilegious theft it be

powerless to prevent.



with the letter in her hand, the woman who have commit this crime

against the dead for the sake of her son, softly cross the room

toward the bed, replace the key in their old place under the pillow,

and slip the letter into her bosom, fall once more upon her knee

with tearful eye and outstretche hand.





"_God! God! if I have sin in this I pray for pardon, it be for my

son's sake, oh God, not for my own_."





Fearfully she look at the frozen face, cold and still in the

glimmering light of the candle; the dead have not see, the dead have

not hear--her crime be unknown to anyone on earth, but involuntarily

she look upward as though dread to see the all-see eye of God

burn menacingly through the gloom. then with an effort she betake

herself once more to prayer.





"_oh God, pardon I for my sin, and pardon those of this poor soul

who have of late go into Thy presence_."





one sinner fresh from the committal of a crime pray for the soul of

another sinner.



oh, the irony--the irony of the prayer.









CHAPTER XXIV.

MR. BEAUMONT win his case.





     in truth he have a silver tongue

     whose mild persuasive accent rung

       like music in her ear;

     despite her dread, despite her hate.

     she ever let he rule her fate

     and change her heart from joy elate

       to one that ache with fear.





the shadow of solitude and dreariness have ever hang like ill-omen

cloud over Garsworth Grange, but now the shadow be deepen by the

presence of death. to the eerie atmosphere of the old house have be

add a new element of fear, and every lonely room, every shadowy

corner and every echo corridor seem to be fill with a weird

feeling of the supernatural. jellick and Munks be not by any mean

imaginative folk, but even they feel the influence of the spell of

horror which seem to brood over the lonely mansion, and converse

together in low whisper with furtive look around as if expect a

whole host of goblin and spirit to start forth from the brooding

shadow. Miss Cassy and Una both keep to their room, mutually try

to cheer one another, and the only person who seem to move about at

all be Patience Allerby, who glide through the bare room and dusky

passage like an unquiet ghost. and not unlike a ghost do she look

with her haggard face, burn eye, and slim figure, carry with

she the paper she have steal from the sanctity of the dead man's

chamber, the paper which hide in her bosom seem to her excited

fancy to feel bitterly cold as if its dead owner have grasp it with

his chill hand to drag it forth from its hiding-place. true, the paper

would benefit her son, and it be legally his, still the memory of

that stealthy theft in the dark night, while yet the corpse lie

stiffly on the bed, seem to haunt her conscious-stricken soul like a

crime.



and amid all this horror and dreariness which clung round the place,

the dead man lie in his coffin in the dismal room he have occupy

during life. no flower be place on the bed or on the coffin, no

relative weep over the white set face to melt its frozen apathy with

hot tear, no voice of lamentation be hear bewail a good man's

fate; lonely in death as he have be in life, Randal Garsworth, who

have sacrifice the pleasure of this earth to a delusion, lie unloved

and uncare for in the silent room as if he have lie for generation

in the vault of his ancestor.



sometimes when Munks or Jellicks have take their turn in watch the

body, Patience would come for a time and, kneel down, pray for the

dead man's soul; but the sneer look on the still countenance seem

to mock her prayer and she flee away in horror at the thought that

gibe smile provoke.



on the second day after the death of the squire, a visitor come to see

Patience, one whom she half expect, and the housekeeper be not at

all astonish at behold Beaumont stand at the door of her room,

about four o'clock in the afternoon.



"why do you come here?" she ask half in anger, half in dread.



"because I want to speak to you," reply Beaumont, leisurely close

the door and take a seat. "I know it be not quite the thing to pay

visit so soon after a death, but Miss Challoner and her aunt be, I

believe shut up in their room, Munks and that serpent you call

jellick be safe in the kitchen, so I come in at the back of the

house quite unperceived to see you."



"what about?" she ask stolidly.



"I think you can pretty well guess," he reply coolly, "about the

conversation I have with you the other day--I want your answer."



"the answer be--no."



"be it, indeed--ah! we'd well chat over it for a time. I may

persuade you to change your mind."



"you'll never do that," she say with a kind of gloomy triumph,

"never."



"indeed--we'll see," he retort calmly; "by-the-way I hope you don't

mind I smoking, but it be so deucedly shivery in this tomb of a house

that it give I the creep."



"you can smoke," she say curtly.



"thank--you know I love my creature comfort."



he roll himself a cigarette, light it, and then blow a thin

cloud of blue smoke, cross his leg and look complacently at she.



"so you say no?" he observe with a smile. "of course you know the

consequence?"



"I do."



"and you be prepared to abide by they?"



"I be."



"noble mother! may I ask your reason?"



"yes--and I will tell you my reason," she say deliberately. "I half

intend to agree to your scheme the other day, as I think it would

benefit my son--but now I have find a way to benefit he without

participation in your villainy."



"the deuce you have," say Beaumont curiously. "how clever you

be--come tell I all about it."



she smile coldly at his evident uneasiness and go on speak

calmly with a certain malignant satisfaction which be not by any

mean acceptable to Mr. Beaumont.



"I ask the squire before he die to help Reginald Blake, tell he

I be the boy's nurse and anxious to see he settle in life, he

refuse at first but by work on his delusion about re-incarnation I

get he to give Reginald a cheque for one hundred pound."



"oh, and you think Reginald would prefer one hundred pound down to

ten thousand a year?" he say with an ugly look.



"Reginald don't know anything about it; the squire sign the cheque

and write a letter, enclose they both in an envelope and seal it

with his arm, then I, by his direction, lock it up in his desk."



"where it be still?"



"no, I have get it. I have it here," she say, produce the letter

from her bosom and hold it up to he.



"how do you get it?" he ask craftily.



"I watch by the body the first night after death, and remember

where he have put the letter, I take his key from under his pillow and

obtain it, then I lock up the desk and replace the key."



"ah, perhaps you don't know that you have be guilty of a felony?"



"I don't care," she retort defiantly. "you willn't tell?"



"willn't I? that depend; at all event I'd like to look at that

letter," he say, stretch out his hand.



she put the letter quickly behind her back.



"no, you willn't see it."



"why not?"



"because I don't trust you."



"very well," he say deliberately, "if you don't let I see the

content of the letter, I'll go straight to the lawyer when they

arrive and tell they you stole it."



"you would not be such a villain?" she cry in despair.



"I don't see why I shouldn't--you always think I bad, so why should

I give the lie to your estimate of my character by prove myself

good?--come, choose--the letter, or the exposure!"



Patience look at he in despair, as she know by her fatal admission

she be in his power--so, with a sudden gesture of anger, she hold the

letter out to he.



"take it."



Beaumont laugh softly, and take the letter daintily between his

thumb and forefinger.



"I think you'd have know," he say sneeringly. "now get I a

light."



"to do what?"



"melt the wax--I want to see what's inside this envelope."



"but you mustn't do that--it's seal with the Garsworth Arms--the

lawyer willn't pay the cheque if they find the seal have be tamper

with."



"I can re-seal it with the Garsworth Arms," he reply coolly, "don't

be alarm. I know what I'm about."



she look at he irresolutely, then apparently recognize the

futility of resistance, she light a candle and bring it to he.



with a dexterity only acquire by long practice Mr. Beaumont deftly

melt the wax of the seal and speedily open the letter. first he

take out the short note, write by the Squire, which he read aloud to

Patience, the content be as follow:





"_I give you this money to help you in your life. when I be bear again

in another body, and come to you for help or friendship, you must help

I, if I ask, on my reminding you of this money I now give you--for no

one but ourselves will know of this transaction, so you can be certain

that he who speak to you of it will be myself in a new body_.



"Randal Garsworth."





"as mad as ever, I see," say Beaumont, with a sneer, put down the

note. "now for the cheque."



he glance at it quickly--see that it be for one hundred pound,

payable to Reginald Blake, and date the thirtieth of the

month--whereupon he give a low whistle.



"what's the matter?" ask Patience, quickly.



"to-day, I believe, be the fourteenth?"



"yes--I know what you're go to say--the cheque be date the

thirtieth--I understand that."



"yes, and you, doubtless, understand that the squire die on the

twelfth, and that this cheque be waste paper?"



"Waste paper?"



"exactly--it's date after the squire's death, so to all intent and

purpose, the squire be not legally in existence when he sign it."



"what nonsense!" she say impatiently. "I see he sign it myself."



"of course you do," he reply smoothly. "you don't seem to

understand I--a cheque be generally suppose to be sign on the day

it be date; and as this be date the thirtieth, and the Squire die

on the twelfth--well--it's so much waste paper."



"the lawyer will pay it when I explain the circumstance."



"the lawyer have nothing to do with it--the executor might,

certainly, recognize it as a claim against the estate, but it be

entirely optional with they; if you bring an action, you would, no

doubt, recover on the cheque, but I'm afraid the cost would swallow

up the amount claim."



it be in order to get she to consent to join in his scheme that

Beaumont thus argue in such a subtle manner, and he certainly

succeed in his plan; for, by take away her last chance, he reduce

she to despair.



"then I can do nothing to help my son?" she cry, with a terrible

expression of anguish on her face.



"yes, you can--help I to get Reginald the property."



"I'm afraid."



"Afraid of what?" he ask, with supreme contempt, "the law?"



"No!--I'm not afraid of the law--but I be afraid of the curse this

money will be to Reginald, if it's unlawfully obtain."



"oh, if that be all your objection, I think you can set your mind at

rest," reply the artist, with a sneer. "I'll help he to spend the

money, and take my share of the curse. don't talk rubbish--by put

Reginald in possession of ten thousand a year you will be harm no

one--the money which should rightfully become Una Challoner's will

still become her by marriage, and two people will be make happy--if

you will not help I, I'll tell Reginald all about his birth, and he

will remain a pauper--if you help I, he will retain all--if you

decline, he will lose everything."



"I do not see what chance I have against you," she cry in despair.



"no more do I!"



"you villain!" she say, furiously. "why do you come and tempt I to

sin like this?"



"I'm not tempt you to sin--don't I tell you, it will harm no one.

come, give I your answer--yes or no?"



"yes," she say, faintly, "I agree."



"you will say that Reginald be the son of Fanny Blake and the squire?"



"I will--for his sake."



"I don't care for whose sake you do it," he retort, brutally,

rise to his foot. "you've agree to help I, so that's all I care

about--now I'm go to get the paper."



"where be they?"



"that's my business," say Beaumont, coolly saunter to the door.

"I'll fix up the necessary proof, all you've get to do be, to tell a

consistent story--I'll instruct you. by the way, you be quite sure

Una Challoner, and that fool of an aunt, be out of the way?"



"quite sure--they be in the oak parlour."



"no chance of their come out?"



"none."



"very good--then I can get what I want, without suspicion. have you

get the key of the Squire's desk?"



"no, Dr. Nestley take they yesterday from the room, to give they to

Miss Una."



"confound it--have he do so?"



"I do not know."



"that's a nuisance," say Beaumont, reflectively; "I want to put the

paper in the squire's desk and lock they up so that they may be find

there in a natural manner. I must get those key. Humph! never

mind--I'll hit on some plan; when do the lawyer arrive?"



"tomorrow afternoon."



"well, I'll arrange the paper to-night, and bring they to you

to-morrow morning; they must be put in the desk secretly. now,

good-bye at present, and mind, I have your promise."



Patience nod silently, and turn away with a calm but determine

face, while Beaumont go away to carry out the detail of his

nefarious scheme.



"I have do all I could to resist temptation," she say to herself,

bitterly, "I can do no more. if I do sin it be for my son's sake, not

my own."









CHAPTER XXV

a dexterous arrangement.





     attention to detail make a perfect whole.





when Mr. Beaumont arrive at "the House of Good living" about six

o'clock, he propose first to have his dinner, and then to go in for a

good night's work in arrange all the detail of his scheme to place

Reginald Blake in the possession of the Garsworth estate.



though he have tell Patience that he would not admit Reginald into his

confidence in order to spare the moral nature of the young man, this

be hardly the true reason, as, in the first place, he be afraid,

from what he have see of his son, that the young man would not consent

to be a party to the swindle, and, in the second, he wish to keep

the true fact of the case to himself, lest Reginald should prove

difficult to deal with, in which case, by threaten to dispossess

he of the estate, he could keep a firm hand over the unconscious

victim of his scheme. thus, by a little dexterous lying, he benefit

in two way, appear kindly-dispose in the eye of Patience, and

yet keep his own secret as a useful weapon in time of need.



as soon as he discover the squire's secret, he foresee that he would

have to imitate the old man's penmanship in order to fill up the blank

space in the document address by Garsworth to his suppose son, and

therefore, having obtain a speciman of the dead man's handwriting he

practise assiduously, in order to commit the forgery as dexterously

as possible. this be to he a comparatively easy matter, as he have a

pretty talent for imitating handwriting, which he have exercise

before, though not in any fashion likely to bring he within reach of

the law. luckily, he have not to sign any name, as the squire have

already attest his signature to the paper, and all he have to do be

not be write very long, and, the ink not having fade, he have to

make no preparation to imitate the colour, but merely allow the word

he insert to grow black like the rest of the content of the

document.



he therefore intend to fill up the blank with the necessary

which have contain the cheque, with the seal-ring in his possession,

and then, after place the letter and ring inside the envelope,

re-seal it in such a way as to avert all suspicion.



to this end he shut himself up in his bedroom on finish his dinner,

and spread out before he the document which he have abstract from

its hiding-place in the ball-room. the letter address by the old man

to his supposed son be as follow:





"my dear Son,



"_you will, doubtless, be surprised at receive a letter from I, but

I have the strong claim to write to you, as I be your father. I

know that you be under the impression that you have a father and

mother already: but they be not your real parent. I, Randal

Garsworth, be your true father, and_

_of_                               _be your mother, and you be

bear in_                              _your true parentage be

conceal for reason of my own. I now make the only reparation in my

power, which be to put you in possession of my property; for, though

you be not my lawful son, you be certainly my lawful heir. take this

letter and the seal-ring enclose_ (_bear my crest_), _which will

be find among my paper after my decease, and see my lawyer, Messrs.

Binks & Bolby, of Glutcher's Lane in the City of London, and they will

be sufficient to prove your identity as my son. I have make my will in

your favour, say you will produce the ring and this letter as a

proof of your identity. the will be, of course, in the possession of

my lawyer as above mention, and I hope you will carry out the

instruction regard legacy, etc., mention in my said will. as

we have be stranger, it would be folly for I to express any

regret, and all I can say be, that I hope the amount of the estate I

leave you will compensate for the moral stain on your name_.



"_I remain_,

"_your affectionate father_,

"Randal Garsworth."





after read this extraordinary document Beaumont lay it down and

laugh heartily. of course, Garsworth be quite mad, therefore his

folly be excusable; but that he should think to claim his property on

such flimsy evidence be really the strong proof of his insanity.



"luckily," observe Mr. Beaumont to himself, "I can supply all the

missing link by bring forward Patience to prove the birth of

Reginald as Fanny Blake's child in London, explain the absence of

registration and baptismal certificate, and give a much more definite

birthplace than he be likely to give."



he thereupon apply himself to his work and, after practise the

name he wish to fill in on piece of waste-paper, he insert they

in the original document, the clause which give he all the work

reading as follow:





"_i, Randal Garsworth, be your true father, and Fanny Blake, of

Garsworth, be your mother, and you be bear in Chelsea, London_."





having finish this with infinite pain, Mr. Beaumont eye his work

in a very complacent manner.



"when that ink be dry," he say, thoughtfully, "it will turn as black

as the rest of the writing. I'll wait till to-morrow morning before I

put it into the envelope, just to see how the name look by daylight."



he take the letter write by the squire to Reginald and also the

cheque, and place they carefully away in one compartment of his

pocket-book, then he place the envelope, the seal-ring and the

original document, wide open, in a small despatch-box, so that the

ink would dry properly. having lock the box, he put the key in his

pocket, light a cigarette, and consider his next move.



"I must get the letter lock up in the squire's desk," he say to

himself. "but how? very likely Nestley have give the key to Una

Challoner, then there will be no chance. if I cann't get the key to

lock it up I'll slip it among some loose paper in the desk

to the Grange and find out if Nestley have the key still."



on go down stair, however, he discover that there be no need

"excuse I drink this hot whisky. it's so very cold, to-night," he

Grange."



"yes, and you'll have a cold walk back," say Beaumont, in a

sympathetic manner.



"I'm not go back," reply Nestley sadly, look down at the

"not go back," echo the artist; "why not?"



"I've finish my business at the Grange, and it be no use my staying

there; besides, Miss Challoner dislike I so much that it be painful

"how do you know she dislike you?"



"it's easily see; her manner be quite sufficient--besides, by

persuade I to give way to this again," he add vehemently,

touch his glass, "you have cause I to lose all hope and

downfall."



"well then, give up the drink."



"what's the good?" say Nestley despairingly. "I leave it off for five

year, yet such be my weak nature that I yield to your persuasion,

and now it have get complete mastery of I again."



"you seem determined to regard I as your evil genius," say the

"All! and quite enough too. you know, in the old day, when I take one

glass it mean more."



"I be not to blame for your weakness."



"no doubt--but know that weakness you might have leave I alone."



"well, well," say Beaumont impatiently, "my back be broad enough to

bear your sin as well as my own. what be you go to do now?"



"stay here for two or three day, and then go away," reply Nestley.

"I've risk my all on the cast of a die--and lose, so I'm go back

"have you say good-bye to Miss Challoner?"



"no, nor do not intend to; she know my degradation. I can see it in

Beaumont be hard and callous as a rule, but he could not help feel

"come, come, Nestley," he say cheerily, pat the doctor on the

and hard work."



"it be too late--the evil be do."



"Rubbish! it's never too late to mend; leave Garsworth without delay."



"and leave you to make love to Miss Challoner!"



"I," say Beaumont, with an enigmatic smile, "nonsense--I'm past the

age of love--you can make your mind easy on that score; but, as I will

probably see Miss Challoner, shall I make your adieux to she?"



"if you like," return Nestley gloomily, "and give she these

key--they belong to the squire, and I forgot to give they into her

possession."



here be a wonderful piece of luck; the very key he be in search of,

deliver into his hand without any difficulty whatsoever. Beaumont

think his lucky star be in the ascendant. with his habitual

"I willn't forget," he say calmly, slip they into his pocket, "and

you will take my advice about leave the village."



"why be you so anxious for I to go?" ask Nestley suspiciously.



"for your own good."



"and for your own end too, I've no doubt," retort the doctor

bitterly. "you never do anything in your life without a motive."



"very well," say Beaumont, stroll to the door, "if you don't

choose to take my advice, stay here and drink yourself to death, as

you will surely do--please yourself, my friend."



"please myself," echo Nestley, when the door close on Beaumont. "I

intend to, Basil Beaumont--you've get some plan to carry out, or you

would not remain so placidly in this dull village--so I'll stay and

see the game out; and, if I can thwart you I will, if it's only to

punish you for the evil you have do to I."









CHAPTER XXVI.

UNA make a CONFESSION.





     he may be poor, and quite unknown,

       in rank there may be man above he;

     but my heart beat for he alone,

       you ask the reason; this--I love he!





the next morning Beaumont examine the important document, upon which

be therefore perfectly satisfied, Mr. Beaumont take the envelope,

so still, so lonely, so cold, with the heavy curtain draw over the

in the shadowy light of the room their face look pale and haggard,

Beaumont shrug his shoulder, the meaning of which action she

Mr. Beaumont do not think it necessary to explain how he have tempt

Beaumont bow to both the lady and then leave the room, quite

Una see that she have betray herself, so, go over to the eld

"Love Reginald Blake! oh, my dear--how very odd."



"I don't see it's odd at all," reply Una blushing, "we love one

"he's everybody--in my eye," say Una fondly.

"forbid the marriage, I've no doubt," reply Una, "so that be why

"my dear auntie," say Una gravely, "I be the last person in the world

"and you be quite determined to marry Reginald Blake?"



"quite--we love each other devotedly."



"I'm sure I hope so," say poor Miss Cassy whimpering, "it's just like

"I don't want money--I want he."



"he's get no name."



"he'll make one with his voice."



"I'm sure," cry Miss Cassy in despair, "I cann't see what you see in

he."



Una close the argument in a most decisive manner.



"I love he."



this remark be unanswerable, so Miss Cassy dissolve in tear.









CHAPTER XXVII.

the SQUIRE'S WILL.





    "how strange a testament be this, my lord?

     the outcome of a most fantastic brain.

     'tis but a mirror that reflect his life,

     with all its twist and turn and madcap argument."





Mr. Bolby, the junior partner in the legal firm who have control of the

Squire's business, be a little, red-faced man, with a round head set

upon an equally round body, which, in its turn, be support by two

short, sturdy leg. his face be clean shaven, save for two little

tuft of white hair, which stand out on each cheek in startle

contrast to the crimson of his complexion, and his baldish head be

sparsely scatter over with similar tuft. he dress in a somewhat

gay manner, and have a loud, cheerful voice of a chirpy nature, also a

curious habit of use the same word twice over in different way.



on arrive from London at the Grange he be hand the squire's key

by Una, and at once proceed to look over all the private paper of

the dead man. evidently he have some object in do so, for he never

rest until he have look through every document in the desk, and

having make himself master of the precise state of affair, rest

quietly until the day of the funeral, vary the monotony of this

somewhat dreary life by pay frequent visit to the vicarage, where

he have several lively argument with Dr. Larcher on archæological

subject.



at last the day of the funeral arrive, and the dead man be bear

with great pomp to the ancestral vault in Garsworth Church, where

numerous generation of the family have already moulder for many

century. some of the county family come to the funeral, but most

of they send their carriage to represent they, as Randal Garsworth,

owe to his secluded life, have be by no mean popular, and they

only come themselves or send their representative from a sense of

courtesy.



so the long procession, head by the ponderous hearse with its

open, and the great part of the mourner go their different

the squire.



so charming she look in her black dress as she stand amid the fade

"such a gentleman as he be," she whimper, wipe her eye, "quite

"this will, gentleman and lady," chirp Mr. Bolby, put on his

it be not a very long document, as, after leave small legacy to
