of her presentiment that she would never get the property, Una could

not help feel disappointed, as it seem to be a bar to her

marriage with Reginald. however, she have a thousand a year, and they

could live on that, so after a moment's reflection, she do not grudge

this unknown son his good fortune. Miss Cassy, however, be not so

easily satisfied, and loudly express her anger at the Squire's

duplicity, which sound rather comical consider how she have be

previously praise up his virtue.



"so dreadful!" she say indignantly, "a son we never hear of--how

very odd!--who be his mother?--where be he born?--what be his

name?--is most peculiar."



"it be very peculiar," assent Mr. Bolby drily, "particularly when I

tell you I don't know any of the three thing you have state--that

be, the three thing state by you."



"do you tell I, sir," ask the vicar in his ponderous manner, "that

you don't know the name of this son?"



"no."



"nor the name of his mother?"



"no."



"nor his birthplace?"



"I give you my word of honour," say Mr. Bolby solemnly, "that I be

absolutely ignorant of all these--of all these, my dear sir, I be

ignorant absolutely."



all present look at one another in blank astonishment, and it be

some time before anyone could speak. Una be the first to recover, and

at once address herself to the lawyer.



"if this be the case," she say slowly, "how be this unknown son to

claim the estate?"



"do you not hear the will read, my dear lady?" reply Mr. Bolby

equably. "do you not hear I read the will? the son must produce a

letter write to he by his father, and also the seal ring of the

family."



"but you surely would not give an unknown man the estate on such

slight evidence?"



"what can I or Dr. Larcher do," say the lawyer with a deprecating

shrug, "Dr. Larcher and myself; what can we do? if he have the paper

and the ring, he be undoubtedly the heir if he produce the ring and

the paper."



"it's the will of a lunatic," cry Miss Cassy angrily.



"I assure you he be in his right mind when it be write," chirp

Mr. Bolby placidly, "my dear lady, in his right mind I assure you."



"I will contest this will," say Una firmly.



"well wait, my dear young lady," say the lawyer, "my dear young

lady, well wait--till the heir appear."



"but suppose he never appear?" suggest Dr. Larcher.



"oh, he'll turn up all right," say Bolby calmly, "people don't give

up ten thousand a year so easily--no--ten thousand be not so easily

give up by people."



"but Mr. Bolby," say Una in despair, "be there no note or certificate

among my cousin's paper which can lead to the identification of this

unknown person?"



Mr. Bolby produce a letter from his breast coat pocket. "now we be

come to it," he say with great glee. "I think such a thing might

be possible; so as it be possible such a thing might be, I search

and find this letter--it be seal with the arm of the family, and

be find by I lock up in his private desk, so everything so far be

in order--I'm sure you will agree there be order in everything so far;

it certainly have a ring inside it, for a ring be inside certainly, as

I can feel it. to my mind this envelope contain the letter and ring

mention in the will."



the curiosity of everyone be now rouse to the high point, and Una

ask the next question amid a breathless silence.



"to whom be the letter address?"



a profound silence ensue, during which the proverbial pin might have

be hear to drop, as the lawyer reply solemnly and slowly,



"the letter be address to 'Mr. Reginald Blake, Vicarage,

Garsworth.'"



"address to I?" cry Reginald in an astonished voice, spring to

his foot. "impossible!"



"see for yourself," reply Bolby, hand he the letter.



Reginald take it in silence and stand hold it irresolutely for a

few moment, during which time he glance round at the astonished

face present. at last with an effort he tear open the envelope, but

overcome with emotion seem unable to proceed far, and cross

the room, give the open envelope to the vicar. Dr. Larcher arise

from his seat as he take the letter and look steadily at the young

man.



"do you wish I to read it?" he ask slowly.



Reginald bow silently, and sit down in the vicar's chair.



Whereupon Dr. Larcher take the letter out of the envelope, leave the

ring still inside, and having open it, read the content in a slow,

deliberate manner. everyone listen in amazement to the extraordinary

disclosure, and every eye be fix on Reginald, who sit in his chair

with his face bury in his hand.



"this then," say the vicar fold up the letter, "prove that you

Reginald be the son of Randal Garsworth and Fanny Blake, for here be

the letter, and here be the ring."



he step up to the lawyer and solemnly deliver both to he, then

return to his seat lay his hand kindly on Blake's shoulder.



"you hear what I have read," he observe sonorously. "what do you

say?"



"say?"' cry the young man, spring to his foot with a pale,

haggard-look face, "that it's a lie--you know yourself, sir, that I

be not the squire's son--Patience know all about my birth--it be

honourable--honourable. I--I be not the son of that man," and the poor

young fellow fairly break down.



on hear Reginald be the heir to the property a great joy appear

in Una's face, but it give place to a look of pity and sorrow as she

see how keenly he feel the ignoble circumstance of his birth.



"there be only one thing to be do in order to make sure," she say,

rise. "call Patience Allerby."



Dick Pemberton go out of the room to fetch she, and during the dead

silence which now prevail Una walk across the room to Reginald and

take his hand.



"this make no difference to I," she whisper fondly. "do not think

that your birth will stand in the way of our marriage, I love you too

well for that."



"God bless you," he mutter brokenly, and clasp her hand

convulsively.



the housekeeper enter the room look pale and wear, with a hard,

defiant expression on her face, as if she be determined to face the

affair out to the bitter end, as indeed she be. on hear she

footstep Reginald arise unsteadily to his foot and look at she

anxiously. on see the anguish in his face she seem to falter

for a moment, but soon recover, and veil her agony under stolid

composure.



"Patience," say Reginald in a broken voice, "I have learn by a

letter from Squire Garsworth that I be his son, and that Fanny Blake

be my mother--be it true?"



she bow her head and reply slowly.



"perfectly true."



Reginald fling up his hand with a cry of anguish and fall back in his

chair--it be true--the possession of ten thousand a year could never

cleanse away the stain which rest on his birth.



"why do you deceive the lad?" ask Dr. Larcher sternly.



"by order of his father," she reply doggedly. "if you remember, sir,

I go to London with Fanny Blake over twenty-two year ago; she tell

I the squire have ruin she, and that be why she leave the village;

six month afterwards her child be bear and she die. I bring the

baby down to the village to the squire, he refuse to recognize his

own offspring, but say he would pay for the boy's keep, so to save

the good name of the child, I invent the story of the parent die

in France, and place it in your care, and he have grow up all these

year under the name of Reginald Blake."



"and Reginald Blake be the squire's son?"



"yes. I hope he have do the boy justice at last."



"he have. by his will Reginald Blake be acknowledge as master of

Garsworth Grange."



Patience give a cry of delight, and with a face beam with

tenderness approach the young man. he arise slowly from his chair as

she come near he fix his wild eye in horror on her face. she see

the look and half recoil, but offer her congratulation timidly.



"you be now rich--" she begin, when he interrupt she furiously.



"Rich!--rich! who care for rich? I be dishonour for the rest of

my life. I have no right to the name I bear. you have deceive and

trick I with your lie, lead I to believe that my birth at

least be without dishonour, and now--now, I find my life have be one

long lie. do you think money will ever repay I for the stain on my

birth. I declare to God that I would willingly become the pauper I be

if I could only regain my self-respect with my poverty. look at I all

of you. I be rich! young, and a bastard."



with a cry of passionate anger he rush from the room, and with an

answer cry of anguish Patience Allerby fall faint on the floor.









CHAPTER XXVIII.

the bitterness of DEATH.





     we call death cruel, but death end all strife,

     Dishonour turn to gall the sweetest life.





to say that those who have assemble in the drawing-room of the Grange

to hear the will read be astonish at the extraordinary disclosure

they have hear, would give but a faint idea of the amazement they

feel. that the squire should have leave his large fortune to a son of

whom no one have ever hear be most remarkable, but that the son in

question should turn out to be Reginald Blake be almost beyond

belief.



still, after examine all the evidence of the fact, Mr. Bolby come

to the conclusion that there could be no doubt as to the identity of

the young man.



accord to the story tell by Patience Allerby, who be well known to

be the nurse of the boy, he have be bear at Chelsea, London, six

month after Fanny Blake's arrival there, and have be call by his

mother's name. on bring he down to the village, Randal Garsworth,

no doubt dread the scandal, refuse to recognise his son, but

agree to pay for his keep. patience, therefore, have do the good she

could under the circumstance, and have place the boy with Dr.

Larcher, tell he that his parent be dead, thus give he at

least the fiction of an honourable birth. it have be a lie, no doubt,

still it be a lie the nobility of which there be no denying, and one

which would hardly be set down by the Recording Angel.



as to the strange discovery that have be make, everyone see at once

that the squire have try to make tardy reparation for his sin by

leave his property to his unfortunate son; and the evidence of the

will itself, the evidence of the letter find in the squire's desk,

and the evidence of the seal ring, all show plainly that the young

man be really and truly the mysterious son allude to in the will.

besides, accord to Dr. Larcher, the squire have mention Reginald's

name on his death-bed, and point towards the desk, intimating, no

doubt, that the document which would give the young man his just right

be hide there, as indeed it be. altogether, on review the whole

case through, Mr. Bolby declare it to be the most extraordinary one

that have ever come under his notice. there could be no doubt but that

justice have be do, and Reginald be formally recognise by

everyone as the master of Garsworth Grange.



of course, the absence of registration and baptismal certificate

would doubtless have prove a stumble-block in a court of law, but,

as Beaumont have foresee, there be no hesitation upon Una's part to

surrender the property to one whom she believe to be the rightful

heir, and moreover, when Mr. Bolby discover that the two claimant

be engage to be marry, he declare that it be a very neat

solution of the difficulty, although, as a matter of fact, owe to

the clearness of the case on the one side and the refusal to test its

truth by legal process on the other, no such difficulty have ever

arise.



Beaumont be now extremely satisfied with the way in which his

conspiracy have succeed, as he have place his son in possession of a

fine estate, worth ten thousand a year. now his next object be to

gain control of this large income through the young man himself.

thank to his ingratiating manner, he completely succeed in

fascinating Reginald, who admire he greatly, and Beaumont only

want to have the young man in his company for a few month to become

indispensable to he. he propose to become Reginald's right-hand man,

at a fix salary, and with authority to look after the estate, out of

which he foresee he could make some nice picking. to do this,

however, he would have to get Reginald away from the village, as

Patience jealously watch her son, and if she think for one moment

that Beaumont be try to take advantage of his lack of worldly

experience, be quite capable of expose the whole swindle.



Fate, however, once more play into his hand, for Mr. Bolby, having

recognise Reginald as the heir, insist upon his come up to London

to see his partner, and be put in formal possession of the estate.

Beaumont therefore determine also to go to London first, so as not to

arouse the suspicious nature of Patience Allerby, and then call on

Reginald when he arrive later on. once he have an interview with he

in London he be quite satisfied that he could do what he like with

the plastic nature of the young man.



on his part Blake, or, as he be now call, Garsworth, be anxious to

leave the village for a time till the nine day' wonder be over, for

in spite of the consolatory feeling of have ten thousand a year, he

feel his position bitterly. having be bring up in an English

gentleman's household, he have imbibe rigorous principle all his

life, therefore it seem to he a terrible disgrace to have such a

stigma on his name. he be a nobody--a nameless outcast, unrecognize

by the law of England--and much as he want to marry Una, he shrink

from give she a name to which he have no legal claim. he dread lest

there should be child of such a marriage, in which case they would

have to bear the stigma attach to their father's birth, and he speak

seriously to Dr. Larcher about release Una from her engagement and

restore to she the property to which he feel she be justly

entitle. thus be the fruit of Beaumont's crime place in jeopardy

by the honour and upright feeling of the young man whom such crime have

benefit, but luckily for Mr. Beaumont, Una come to the rescue.



she plainly tell Reginald that she do not care for the circumstance

of his birth, which he could not help in any way, and as to she be

rightfully entitle to the property, if she marry he the property

would be just as much her as if it have be duly leave to she by the

squire. so after a great deal of persuasion from Una and Dr. Larcher,

Reginald come to accept his somewhat improve position with

equanimity.



"I cannot stay here, however," he say bitterly. "everyone stare at

I as if I be a wild beast. I will go up to town with Mr. Bolby, and

return in a few month, when I get more used to the position."



Una fully approve of this, and agree to stay on at the Grange with

Miss Cassy until he return, then they would be marry, and go

abroad for a year, during which time the old house would be

redecorate, and they would then return to live in it, when all the

circumstance of his succession to the property have to some extent

be forget.



Beaumont, having hear this decision, determine to go up to Town in

advance and there await Reginald's arrival. so, after take an

effusive farewell of everyone, he depart, carry with he the good

wish of all with whom he have come in contact. only Patience do not

wish he God speed, but survey he grimly when he come to say

good-bye to she.



"I'm glad to see you go," she say coldly. "our son be now provide

for, and you have at least do something towards repair your

villainy. I hope I'll never set eye upon you again, but if ever I

hear of you meddle with Reginald in any way it will be the bad for

you."



"say the bad for both of we," retort Beaumont airily. "we're in

the same box over this affair, and punishment to I mean the same for

you."



so he take his departure, leave an excellent impression behind he,

and everyone hope he would come back again some day, which he

laughingly promise to do if his engagement would permit he.



"I'll see you in London, Reginald," he say to the young man, "and

anything I can do for you there, of course, you may command I."



Reginald thank he for his kindness, little think how treacherous

that kindness be, and then address himself to the work of prepare

for his own departure.



he have a long interview with Patience, in which she inform he that

the story tell by she to Dr. Larcher have be tell with the good

intention to spare he the truth, and on consideration he see for

himself that she have act for the good, so he forgive she for the

falsehood. patience stay on at the Grange, live her old life, and

feel quite satisfied now that the future of the human being she love

well on earth be secure.



Reginald ask Dr. Larcher to let he take Dick to Town, which request

the worthy vicar grant, only admonish Mr. Bolby to look carefully

after the pair.



"I love they as my own son," say the good man gravely, "and I dread

lest they should be lead into evil way in the great city--they be

young and untried--let they not drink, for what say Horace? '_Non ego

sanius, Bacchabor Edonis_.'"



"they willn't get any bad example from I," say Mr. Bolby, "from I

there's no bad example to be get. I'll take they to the theatre and

several amusement, but that's all."



so the vicar, full of anxiety for his dear boy, allow they to go,

and the last to bid Reginald farewell be Una.



"don't forget I among all the beauty of London," she whisper

archly; "or I'll come to Town to look for you."



"don't be afraid," he reply with an affectation of lightness he be

far from feel. "I will come back to you heart-whole, and then if

you'll have I we'll be marry."



so the poor lad depart, having learn already thus early in life

that wealth alone do not bring happiness.









CHAPTER XXIX.

from DR. NESTLEY'S POINT of VIEW.





     so low--so low--yes I be low indeed

     but he thy lover tho' of high estate

     will fall to this--I tell thee dainty dame

     the devil even now be at his ear

     breathing temptation in most subtle guise

     which soon will lose he all he hold most dear.





the autumn be now nearly over, and it be that bleak, chill season

just before winter when the tree, denude of foliage, seem to wait

for the snow to cover the bare branch which shiver complainingly

in the chill wind. under foot the ground be dark and sodden, overhead

the sky dull and lower, while piercingly cold blast blow across

the lonely marsh and whistle shrilly over the waste moorland.



dreary and desolate as it have look in summer time, Garsworth Grange

appear even more dreary and desolate under the sombre-colour sky.

the damp have discolour the white marble of the statue, which seem

lose amid the surround desert of bare tree and dead leave. it be

everlastingly rain, and Una, look out of the antique window at

the gloomy landscape see through the drive mist of rain, feel dull

and depressed. all day long the wind whistle through the dismal

room, and the rain ceaselessly drip from the eave, so it be

hardly to be wonder that both Una and Miss Cassy feel anything but

cheerful.



it be now about two month since Reginald have go up to town, and

Una have receive frequent letter from he about the way in which

everything be be arrange by the lawyer. of late these letter

have become feverish in tone, as if the writer be try to invest

his correspondence with a kind of fictitious gaiety he be far from

feeling, and this sudden change of style give her serious uneasiness.

she know how sensitive Reginald be, and how deeply he have feel the

discovery of his real birth, so dread lest to banish the spectre

which haunt he he should plunge into dissipation. in one of his

letter also he have mention that he have meet Beaumont in town, and as

Una learn from the vicar that Dick Pemberton have go to folkestone

to see his uncle, she feel doubtful as to the wisdom of an

inexperienced youth like Reginald be leave alone in London with a

reckless, man of the world like Beaumont.



she have mistrust Beaumont when she first meet he, but by his

fascinating manner he have succeed in overcome her repugnance, but

now that he be away the influence of his strong personality die out,

and she begin to dread his power over her lover's honourable,

guileless nature.



"I wish Reginald would come back at once," she say to Miss Cassy,

"and then we could be marry, and he would have some one to look

after he."



"I'm sure I'll be glad when you be marry," whimper Miss Cassy,

whose spirit the lonely life she be lead sadly depressed. "I'll

go melancholy mad if I stay here--I know I shall. I'm sure that ben't

odd, be it? I feel like what's-she-name in the Moated Grange, you

know--the weary, weary dead thing I mean, and the gloomy flat--not

half so nice as the flat we have in town. if we could only go to it

again--I feel so shivery."



and so Miss Cassy ramble on in a disconnected fashion, one think

suggest another, while Una sat stare out of the window, with

Reginald's last letter in her hand, wonder what be good to be

do.



"I don't trust Mr. Beaumont," she say at length. "he be not a good

companion for Reginald."



"oh, my dear," say Miss Cassy, pick up the tea-cosy, which she

keep by she to put on her head when she feel cold, "such a charming

man--quite a Lord what's-his-name in his manner."



"his manner be all right, I've no doubt," return Una drily, "but

what about his moral?"



Miss Cassy give a little girlish scream and extinguish herself with

the tea-cosy.



"what dreadful thing you do say, Una," she observe in a shocked

tone. "so very odd--quite like Zola, so very french."



"my dear aunty, I know you be one of those people who think that

unmarried girl should be absolutely ignorant of such thing. I don't

agree with you. there's no need of they to parade their knowledge of

evil, but they cannot help hear about it, however carefully bring

up. I know London be not a good place for a young man with plenty of

money, especially when he be so inexperienced as Reginald--besides,

Mr. Beaumont be a man of the world, whom I really believe life by his

wit--and if it be a case of his wit against Reginald's, my dear

aunt, I'm afraid poor Reginald will come off worst."



"what's to be do then?" say Miss Cassy blankly. "do you think if I

send dear Reginald some tracts----"



"I don't think that would be much use," interrupt Una laughing. "no,

I'll go over to Garsworth to see the vicar--he will know what be good

to be do. I will show he Reginald's letter, and I'm sure he will

agree with I that it will be wise to withdraw he from Mr. Beaumont's

influence."



"why don't Mr. Bolby look after he?" say Miss Cassy indignantly.



"I daresay Mr. Bolby have get his own business to look after," reply

Una with a faint sigh; "besides, he only regard Reginald from a

monetary point of view, nothing more--will you come to the vicarage

with I, aunt?"



"oh yes, dear," cry Miss Cassy with great alacrity, "the walk will

do I good, and I'm so dull--I'll talk to dear Mrs. Larcher, you know,

she's so odd, but still she's well than one's own company, ben't

she, dear?--let we get ready at once--the rain have go off I see."



"then let we follow the example of the rain," say Una with a laugh,

and the two lady go away to prepare themselves for their walk.



when they sally forth with heavy cloak and thick boot, they find

that for once the sun have show his face and be look through the

watery cloud in a somewhat feeble fashion. the ground under foot be

wet and spongy, still it be well than be immure in the dreary

Grange, and as they walk rapidly along their spirit rise in spite

of the depressing influence of the weather.



when they arrive at the bridge after a sharp walk they see a man

lean over the parapet look at the cold grey water swirling

below.



"dear I, Una, how very odd," exclaim Miss Cassy, "there be Dr.

Nestley."



"Dr. Nestley," echo Una rather startle. "I think he have go away

last week?"



"he be go, but for some reason do not," answer Miss Cassy, who

by some mysterious mean hear all the gossip of the village. "I hear

he be still stay at Kossiter's--drinking, my dear--oh dreadful--so

very odd."



by this time they be directly in the centre of the bridge, and

hear footstep Nestley turn round, show a wan haggard face

with dull bleared eye fill with mute misery. so ill and desolate

do the young man look that Una's heart smite she as she think the

change be bring about through her refusal to marry he, and though

she despise he for his weakness of character in thus be

influence, yet she still feel pity for the helplessness of the poor

fellow. Nestley flush as he recognize the two lady, then raise

his hat and without say a word turn once more to look at the

river. Una feel uneasy as he do so, for a sudden doubt arise in her

heart as to whether he do not intend to put an end to his life, so

take a sudden resolution she whisper to Miss Cassy to walk on by

herself to the vicarage.



"I will join you soon," she say in a low voice, "but first I want to

speak to Dr. Nestley."



"but it's so odd," object Miss Cassy, "really so very--very odd."



nevertheless she make no further objection and trot away through

the village street, leave Una alone on the bridge with Dr. Nestley.

though the unhappy young man know that she be still behind he he do

not turn round but keep stare dully at the foam-streaked water of

the Gar.



"Dr. Nestley," she say, softly touch he on the shoulder, "I want

to speak to you."



he turn sullenly round, though the touch of her gloved hand send a

thrill through his frame, and Una recoil with an exclamation of pity

as she see what a wreck he be. his face, formerly so fresh-colour,

be now grey and thin, his eye blear with dark circle under they,

while his nervous lip and shake hand show how deeply he have be

drink. even in his clothe she see a change, for they be

carelessly put on, his linen be dirty and his tie arrange in a

slovenly manner--altogether he look like a man who have entirely lose

his self-respect and care neither for his health nor appearance.



Nestley see the expression on her face and laugh, a hollow mirthless

laugh, which seem quite in keep with his wretched appearance.



"you be look at your work, Miss Challoner," he say bitterly,

"well, I hope you be satisfied."



Una's pride be up in arm at once.



"you have no right to speak to I in such a manner, sir," she say

haughtily, look at he with a proud cold face. "do not ascribe your

own folly to any fault of mine--that be both weak and unmanly."



the wretched creature before she droop his head before the severe

gaze of her eye.



"you would not marry I," he say weakly, "you would not save I from

myself."



"be I to go through the world save man from their own passion?" she

return scornfully. "shame upon you, Dr. Nestley, to take refuge

behind such a weak defence. surely because a woman refuse to marry a

man he ought not to lower himself as you have do, and then lie the

blame on she instead of himself--you ought to make an end of this

folly."



"just what I be think," he mutter, glance at the river. she

instinctively guess what the glance mean, and look at he,

say:



"would you add suicide to the rest of your follies?--that be a

coward's refuge and one not worthy of a clever man like you. come,

Doctor Nestley," she continue, lay a kind hand on his shoulder,

"be advise by I. give up this mad love of drink which be lower

you to the level of the brute, and go back to your home--then amid

your old companion you will soon forget that I ever exist."



"never! never!" he say in a broken voice.



"oh yes you will," she reply cheerfully. "time be a wonderful

consoler--besides, Doctor Nestley, I could never have marry you, for

though you do not know then you know now--I be go to marry Mr.

Blake."



"and what difference will that make to you?" he ask mockingly,

lift his dull eye to her earnest face.



"I do not understand you," she say coldly, draw back.



"then I can easily explain," reply the young man quickly, "the only

difference will be this--you love he, you do not love I--for the

rest both Reginald Blake--or shall I call he Garsworth?--and myself

will be equal in all else."



"you be talk wildly," say Una in an icy tone, "so I shall leave

you--permit I to pass if you please?"



"not till I have have my say," he retort, his eye grow bright. "I

can wring your proud heart now as you wrung mine then. I see your look

of horror when you look at I and see how low I have fall through

drink--in the same way you will look upon your lover when he return

from the guardianship of Basil Beaumont."



Una give a cry of alarm and reel against the stone parapet of the

bridge for support, while a cold hand seem to clutch at her heart.



"you have hear of those devil of old who tempt mankind," go on

Nestley rapidly. "yes, you have hear such story and think they

pious fiction of Catholicism--but it be true, quite true. there be

devil of like sort in our midst even now, and Basil Beaumont be one.

I know he in London five year ago when I be a young man just

start in life. I have no vice, I have great talent, I be devote

to my profession and all seem to promise a fair life. but Beaumont

come, devil that he be, in the guise of an angel of light, and ruin

I. he beguile I with his wheedle tongue and specious manner into

believe in he. having gain my confidence he lead I to gamble and

drink until I sink so low that even he forsook I--yes, forsook the

man he have ruin. it be when his fatal influence be withdraw that

I begin to recover. I take the pledge, leave London and its

fascination and plunge into hard work. for five year I never

touch alcohol and thing seem go well with I once more--but I

come down here and meet he again. I resist his persuasion for a

long time, but on the night you reject I I be wear out with

watch by the bedside of the squire, and sick with disappointment;

he persuade I to take a glass of wine--it be follow by

another--and then--I need not go on, but next morning I find I have

lose my self-respect. I give way to despair, there seem no hope for

I, and now see what I be, and all through Basil Beaumont--I have lose

my good name--my money--my position--everything--everything in the

world."



Sick with horror Una try to speak, but could only look at he with

white lip and a terrified face. see her alarm he resume his

discourse but in a somewhat milder fashion.



"your lover have go to London, and Beaumont be with he. he be the

possessor of money. Beaumont will want to handle that money; to do so

he will reduce Reginald Blake to a mere cypher. do you know how he

will do it? I will tell you. by fast living--he will reduce your lover

to the abject condition I be in, and through he squander the

Garsworth money. it do not matter how high Reginald Blake's

principle may be, how pure he desire to live, how temperate he may

have be, he be in the power of Basil Beaumont, and, little by

little, will be drag down to the low depth of degradation and

despair."



"no, no!" she cry, wildly, "it cannot be!"



"it will be, I tell you--I know Beaumont, you do not--if you would

save your lover, get he out of the clutch of that devil, or he will

become an object of horror to you as I be."



he turn away with a look of despair, and cross the bridge on to

the common, slouch along the muddy road without cast a glance

back, while Una, with pale face and tightly-clench hand, gaze

after he with mute agony in her eye.



"oh, great Heaven!" she moan, lift up her wan face to the grey

sky, "if this should be true--it must be true--I can see he be

speak the truth! Reginald to sink to that--no, no! I'll go and see

the vicar. I will tell he all--all! we must save he before it be too

late!"



with feverish impatience she begin to walk down the street on her way

to the vicarage, intent only on find some mean of save the man

she love.



and the man who have no woman to save he slouch wearily along the

road--a lonely, desolate figure, with only the grey sky above and the

grey earth below, with no hope, no peace, no love await he, but

only the blank, black shadow of approach sorrow brood over his

life with sombre wing.









CHAPTER XXX.

a mother'S AFFECTION.





   _niobe_. from cruel Ph[oe]bus all my child fly.

   _chorus_. fly then, oh Queen, else will they bring thee harm.

   _niobe_. what evil counsel be upon thy tongue?

   _chorus_. the counsel that would save thee from thyself.

   _niobe_. a mother's love should thus protect her child.

   _chorus_. from such protection cometh death to thee.

   _niobe_. death will be welcome if it cometh thus

            for naught thou know of true motherhood

            think that fear of death will drive I hence

            to leave mine offspring to Ph[oe]bean dart.





the next day be Sunday, and during the night there be a heavy fall

"Mr. Beaumont."

when she have finish all her work for the day she retire to her room

outside the snow have cease to fall, and all the cold tint and

then she cross the bridge, and, glance over the side, see the

she know that Nestley be stop at "the House of Good living," so

"be Miss Una ill?" he ask, think she have come for his

Dr. Nestley look at she in surprise, with a bitter smile on his

"what, you too?" he say derisively, "be you another of his victim?"



"no--I be not his victim--but, as you know, I be the nurse of Mr.

"everything; you tell Miss Una your story yesterday and she say

the woman tighten her thin lip ominously.



"I don't think so if I can help it," she say grimly.

"sure," he repeat bitterly, "I know it only too well to my own cost,

"what do you know about he?"



"More than he care the world to know."

"I don't care if he come to Garsworth as long as he leave my--my boy

"Reginald Blake--I be his nurse--I will get he to return here, and

"he'll do this much," cry Nestley quickly, "he'll try and prevent

"why?" she ask curtly. "for what reason?"



"the good of all reason--he love Una Challoner himself."



Patience arise to her foot with a cry, her face turn to a ghastly

"I know Basil Beaumont--you don't."



"don't I!" she cry viciously, turn round. "I know he only too

"he have get to deal with I as well as with they," she say grandly.

"I will do what I say--if he do not accept the warning I send he,

Nestley stand silent with astonishment, while without another word,

"Ugh!" he say with a shiver. "I believe she will--but no--Beaumont be

he crouch down over the fire, the red light of which glare upon his

"it's Sunday," he whisper. "Sunday night--I ought to go to church.

CHAPTER XXXI.

PSALM CVII. 19.





                            some chance word

     may strike upon an inattentive ear

     and rouse the soul from selfish slumbering,

     to wrestle with a thousand subtle foe

     that would destroy its hope of Paradise.





outside the snow fall fast and thick from the dull impenetrable sky,

but within the church all be warmth and light. owe to the primitive

civilization of the village the holy edifice be only illuminate by a

few oil lamp, which just suffice to fill it with shadow. the great

arched roof above be completely in darkness, and hang low down,

almost on a level with the pew, the lamp burn with a dull yellow

light in the heavy atmosphere. on the communion table four taper

shine like amber-colour star, touch the white limb of the

Christ hang on the ebony cross with fitful light. a lamp enclose

in a red globe swing from the centre of the chancel arch, name

fiercely crimson like a red eye glare out of the semi-darkness, and

on each side of the pulpit two candle throw a doubtful glimmer on the

open bible. amid all this fantasy of shadow and light kneel the simple

villager with bow head, follow, with murmur voice, the

Lord's Prayer, recite by the vicar. the confused sound buzz among

the multitudinous arch, lose itself in faint echo amid the great

oaken beam, and then the thunder of the organ roll out a melodious

amen which die away in a whisper as, with a rustle, the congregation

arise to their foot to make the response.



during the singing of the psalm, the door at the low end of the

church open and, herald by a blast of cold air which make all the

lamp flicker, a man steal stealthily to a dark seat and kneel down.

this be Duncan Nestley, who, torture by madden thought and

overpower mental anguish have come to religion for consolation, now

kneeling, with hot dry eye and clasp hand, amid the shadow.



the evening psalm be that magnificent chant wherein David describe

Jehovah as come forth in all his glory, and the choir, really be

an excellent one, the rolling verse of the hebrew poet be well

render. the thin treble of the boy ring out piercingly shrill

through the mystic twilight.





"_he ride upon the cherubim and do fly: he came fly upon the

wing of the wind_."





then, without pause, the deep voice of the man thunder out the

sublime word:





"_he make darkness his secret place his pavilion round about he with

dark water and thick cloud to cover he_."





no wonder, as the great volume of sound rang through the church, the

heart of the unhappy man be fill with fear.



this terrible deity who come forth in such appalling splendour be his

enemy, this awful Jehovah of the Hebrews, in whose hand flash the

sword of vengeance, be his merciless judge, and kneel there with

tightly clench hand he feel crush to the earth by the fierce

trumpet ring out the proclamation:





"_the Lord liveth, and blessed be my strong helper and praise be the

God of my salvation_."





there be mercy then--this unknown Splendour whose terror have be

shadow forth with such grandeur have pity as well as vengeance; a

dull feeling of exhaustion steal over he as the psalm end with the

promise of mercy, and his dry lip move mutely as though to join in

the final "glory be to the Father."



he do not rise from his knee, but still in a posture of abject

supplication hear, as in a dream, the reading of the lesson and the

sweet kindly music of the hymn. it be only when the vicar, tall and

stately in his white surplice, mount the pulpit and give out the

pew, utterly exhaust by the conflict emotion rouse within he

by the music, but the word of the text give out by the resonant

voice of Dr. Larcher seem to convey some comfort to his despairing

soul.





"_then they cry unto the Lord in their trouble and he saveth they out

of their distress_."





he listen to the sermon idly at first, but soon find, to his

surprise, that he be follow the word of the preacher with close

attention. Dr. Larcher be no golden-mouthed Chrysostom by any manner

of mean, but he preach a plain, homely sermon, eminently adapt to

the simple congregation of which he be pastor. never for a moment do

he lose himself amid abstruse theological argument which they would

not have understand, but tell they practical truth in vigorous Saxon,

the meaning of which no one could fail to grasp.





"_for, my brother, when a man be at the low depth of despair it

be then that he first call upon the name of the Lord. in time of

peace and plenty, when our friend be around we and our coffer be

full, we be ala too apt to forget that all these benefit come from

the Almighty, and thus at time neglect to thank he for his many

mercy. but when the cloud of adversity gather around we, when the

love one sink into the grave, when our worldly wealth disappear

like snow, when our name become a by word of scorn and reproach, it

be then that we turn to God for that help which be deny to we by

man. and do he ever refuse to aid us?--No!--In the word of the

Psalmist, 'cast thy burden upon the Lord and he shall sustain

thee'--to the heart that be truly contrite he give peace and help in

time of need; none so low but what he will not hear and grant their

prayer if make from the heart. it be not to the terrible Jehovah of

the jewish nation, with pomp and pride of sacrifice and blow of

silver trumpet, that we of later generation appeal. no, since the

come of our dear Lord, who form the link between most high heaven

and lowly earth we offer up humble prayer to he in solitude and he,

the mild and merciful Father of we all dry the tear from our eye

and take the sorrow from our heart. if a man be weak and would

commit sin let he call upon the Lord and he will be strengthen--if

the temptation to which he have be expose have be too heavy for

his bearing and he have succumb, let he implore mercy of the

Almighty and he shall surely find it. alas! how often do we find

unforgiveness in man forget the word of Christ, 'forgive we our

trespass as we forgive those who trespass against we,' they turn

their face away and leave we abase in the dust, but Christ lift we

from that position of humiliation with comfort word, 'arise poor

sinner and thy sin be forgive thee, for to this end do I come into

the world.' if there be any one of you present who have sin let he

repent this night and he will find the peace of God which passeth all

understanding. if he be weak, God will give he strength to conquer;

if he be in despair, God will give he hope of pardon. pray--pray

unceasingly, for it be by prayer alone that our weak voice can reach

the ear of the eternal Father_."





Nestley wait to hear no more, but with a stifle cry of anguish flee

from the church into the cold, white world outside. stumble over the

tombstone, through the blind snow--now fall in thick flake--he

soon find himself in the open street, and urge by some mad impulse,

he know not what, he speed wildly onward through the market-place, over

the bridge and on to the trackless common. with clench tooth and

wild, stare eye he make head against the storm that be sweep

along. his foot make no sound on the yield snow and he glide along

like an unquiet ghost, the burn word of the sermon ring in his

ear.



he be in the low depth of despair and all man have turn their

face from he; he would call upon the Lord to help he--but would God

attend?--surely he would--what be the word of the text?







"_then they cry unto the Lord in their trouble and he saveth they out

of their distress_."





he also would cry and the Lord would save he from the terrible agony

he be endure. he would kneel down there and then in the snow and

call upon this unseen God, pavilione in the terrible splendour of

encircle cloud, to aid he.





"_God! help I!_"





no answer save the whistling of the wind and the soft sound of the

snow sweep past, caress his cold face with delicate touch.





"_God! show I how to be save_."





nothing, nothing, only the black sky above, the white earth below, and

himself, between the two, a reckless, despair man hold up his

helpless hand.





"_our Father which art in heaven_----"





how sweet those word sound; he have surely hear they at his

mother's knee--then he be an innocent child, but now! oh God, the

evil life he have live since then!





"_God! God!--pity and save!_"





it be get quite warm now and he feel drowsy; if he sleep for a

while he would then awake and ask God once more to save he; but no,

if he fall asleep in the snow he would never awake again, for this

treacherous snow would slay he with cold embrace. he would die--die.

ah! he could not die, even though lull to sleep by the siren voice,

and soft caressing of the snow queen; life be sweet, so he would

fight to retain it.



a long struggle and he be on his foot; the road! where be the road?

he could not see it. never mind, the snow and wind be at his back,

he would walk on till he come to the bridge, then he would be in

safety. oh, the weary, weary mile--half daze, half mad, he stagger

on, reel like a drunken man. would the road never come to an end?

oh this incessant whirl of snow-flake that he be in, it be the

dance of death and he be the dancer.



Quicker and quick fall the flake on the white common and over to

the dark surface of the Gar but no figure be struggle along now;

no, it be lie upon the bridge, a disorder heap of black clothing,

which the snow be rapidly hide beneath its soft white mantle.



over the bridge come the horse and gig of a sturdy farmer who have to

cross the wild white waste beyond to reach home, and the sturdy farmer

himself with his buxom wife beside he drive the wise old horse.

suddenly the old horse shy at the figure lie in the snow--a start

on the part of the farmer and his wife--then exclamation and call

for help, black figure come glide over the snow like shadow, and

kindly hand raise Duncan Nestley from his deadly resting-place.



take he to the inn, place he before a roar fire, force some hot

brandy between his blue lip, and rub his frozen limb to bring back

the circulation of the chill blood.



dead! no, not dead! he open his eye. in they there be no

intelligence, only a vacant stare--he babble a few word and then

fall back in a faint.



Delirious, yes, and delirious for many a long day, poor soul.









CHAPTER XXXII.

LONDON.



     London be the candle which, ever attract country moth by its

feverish glare destroy they remorselessly in its cruel flame.





Reginald Blake be not enjoy himself very much in Town owe to his

disturb state of mind. for year he have picture to himself the

marvellous city and his life therein; how he would one day find

himself a denizen of the great metropolis, eager to win fame and

fortune by the magic of his voice, how he would delight in lead the

ambitious, half Bohemian, wholly delightful existence of a singer, and

how he would be able to wander about the street and see the brilliant

life of the mighty city with its restless activity and ardent

striving after wealth, fame and novelty. Grey Westminster Abbey,

noble St. Paul's, the enormous pile of the Parliament House, the

golden-top column of the Monument, he would see all these, with

their wealth of historical, religious and artistic association. he

would tread the very street over whose stone wander proud

poverty-stricken Chatterton, courtly Addison and ponderous Dr.

Johnson; he would find the picturesque alley, house and road

describe in the fascinating page of Dickens, and he would stray

about the sacred purlieus of Drury Lane, haunt by the stately shade

of Wilkes, of Siddons, of Bracegirdle, and David Garrick. good

heaven, what innumerable fantastic castle do he not build in Cloud

Cuckoo Land about the unseen glory of London, where every street and

stone be redolent of the glorious history of England from Plantagenet

to Guelph.



oh, beautiful castle of Cloudland, how rapidly do their gorgeousness

disappear from his fancy before the disenchant touch of chill

reality. he be indeed in London, but alas it be not the magic London

of his dream, this enormous assemblage of house through which flow

a melancholy grey river and over which hang a dismal dark cloud of

smoke and fog. the London of romance and the London of reality be

two very different thing, yet the disenchantment of this dreaming

youth be not wholly due to the prosaic appearance of the city itself

but rather to the gloom and depression of his spirit.



the recollection of how his wealth have come to he weigh heavily on

his mind, cause he to view all thing in a most dismal manner, and

torture his sensitive disposition with irritate thought and

madden delusion. in vain he try hard to shake off this gloomy

feeling and enjoy the many-colour life of the great city; in vain he

tell himself that the accident of his birth be no fault of his own

and in vain he strove to take pleasure in the society of the man and

woman to whom he have be introduce by Basil Beaumont. it be all

useless, for a dark cloud of bitterness and distrust seem to settle

upon the joyousness of his life which lead he to view everything with

jaundiced eye. he feel that he have lose the adolescent zest for life

as Donatello must have do after he have stain his hand with blood,

and although he have youth, talent, good look, and wealth, yet all

these delightful gift of the fairy be neutralize by the fatal

gift of dishonour bestow upon he by the malignant beldame who have

prove herself the evil genius of his life.



as soon as the business connect with the Garsworth estate be

properly complete and he have be fully recognize as the heir of the

old Squire, Bolby consider that he have do his duty, leave the

young man and his friend Dick pretty well to their own device. Dick

enjoy everything with the inexhaustible appetite of youth, but

Reginald take his pleasure, such as they be, in a listless manner,

which show how completely he have lose all capability of enjoyment.



Mr. Pemberton have be rather irritated by the prosaic life they lead

when in the lead string of Mr. Bolby, whose idea of amusement

be of the most primitive nature, rarely extend beyond an

afternoon at the Zoo or a night at Madame Tussaud's or the Egyptian

Hall. the only thing Dick see in Mr. Bolby's idea of life, which he

consider at all meritorious be the excellent dinner which the

little lawyer give they, but Dick in his fly visit to Town have

taste of the Tree of Knowledge beneath whose shade be the music

hall and the burlesque theatre, so he be anxious to go to such like

place for his amusement.



when they leave Mr. Bolby, therefore, and be comfortably establish

in a quiet hotel in Jermyn Street, Dick, see that Reginald be

absolutely indifferent as to where he go, or what he do, take the

whole arrangement of their London life into his own hand and

succeed in go to a good many place which would have terribly

shock the vicar have he know. not that such forbidden pleasure do

they much harm, for both lad be extremely sensible for their age,

still Dick find himself able, through Reginald's generosity, to

and as Dick be suppose to be his bachelor uncle's heir, he have to

leave Town, much to his own disgust and to the regret of Reginald, who

miss his lively friend every hour of the day.



he still stay in Town, however, but as he know no one, his existence

be to say the least extremely dull. Reginald be essentially of a

social nature and want someone to whom he could talk, therefore he

be not sorry when one day Basil Beaumont, who have be wait for

the departure of Dick, call upon he and henceforth constitute

himself his bear leader. as they have see nothing of the artist since

their arrival in Town, Dick have never think of tell Reginald his

friendly towards he and be quite prepared to accept his offer of

companionship as the outcome of a kindly disposition and not the

result of a carefully calculate scheme.



a more dangerous companion for a young man in a depressed state of

       from it thou wilt win.

a word in season.

"no doubt, but a very necessary philosophy," retort Beaumont in a

the train be now move off, so he shake hand with the young man

"Friend," echo Beaumont with an evil smile, as the long train
