like most clever man the artist be very proud of his tact and

delicacy in deal with ingenuous youth, and he feel annoyed with

himself lest by fail to skilfully angle for this trout, he should

have lose his prize by fail in his diplomacy, and thereby show too

plainly the real reason he have for his apparently disintereste

friendship. so, on arrival at his chamber, Mr. Beaumont light a

cigarette, throw himself moodily into a big arm-chair, and proceed

to

mentally review all his conduct towards Reginald since the lad's

arrival in town.



hard as he try to find some flaw in his own conduct which might have

put Blake on his guard, Beaumont be quite unsuccessful in do so,

for his demeanour towards his propose victim have be all that the

most delicate tactician could have desire.



"I cann't have frighten he away," he say aloud to himself, "for I

act the disinterested friend to perfection. hang it! I wonder what

take he back to Garsworth. I see a letter in his hand, so I expect

Una Challoner's be write to he: but that would not do I any

harm, for she like I, and I should think would be rather glad if I

look after the boy in Town. I wonder it that confound Patience have

be talk? I make thing all straight before I leave Garsworth, but

one never know what may happen, and if Patience get an inkling of my

design, she'd move heaven and earth to get the boy back again to her

side--humph! I hardly know what to think--that's the bad of deal

with woman; they're so crooked, you never know what they're go to

do next."



he arise from his seat and walk impatiently up and down the room,

seek some solution of the problem thus present to he. while

do so, he happen to glance at the mantelpiece, and see thereon a

letter.



"I wish that man of mine wouldn't put the letter there," he grumble,

take the letter, "I can never find they--but let I see who this be

from; Garsworth postmark--don't know the writing--wonder if Una

Challoner be--by Jove!" he ejaculate, as he take out the letter and

glance at the signature, "it's from Patience Allerby. I know she have

be up to some mischief. well! I'll read the letter, and see if I

cann't foil you, my lady."



resume his seat in the arm-chair, he smooth out the letter

carefully as he prepare to read it. the content, which be as

follow, considerably astonish he, and his lip curl with a

cynical smile as he glance down the closely-write page.





"Basil Beaumont,--



"_be it true what Dr. Nestley have tell I--that you be in love with

Una Challoner? if it be, I will make an end of everything between we,

and denounce you, even at the cost of my own liberty. you have ruin

my life, but you be not go to ruin that of my son by take from

he the woman he love_.



"_Reginald Blake be now in London, and I hear you be constantly by

his

side. act honourably by he, or I swear I will punish you for any harm

you do to he. by our mutual sin he be now in possession of the

Garsworth Estate, and be go to marry the lawful mistress of it. as

this be the case, and his marriage to Miss Challoner be the one

atonement both of we can make for deprive she of her inheritance,

you must let thing take their course. you have a desperate woman to

deal with in I, and if you harm either Reginald or his promise wife

in any way, I swear by all that I hold most sacred that you will stand

in the prisoner's dock for conspiracy, even though I have to stand by

your side as an accomplice_.



"Patience Allerby."





Beaumont laugh sardonically as he finish this letter, and twirl

it in his finger, look thoughtfully at the carpet.



"I wonder," he say at length, in a low voice, "I wonder if this

letter mean love of her son, or jealousy of Una; both I expect, for

though she hate I like poison, and everything sentimental between we

be dead and bury year ago she get mad as soon as she think I

admire another woman--strange thing a female heart--whatever ashe of

dead love may remain in it, there be always some live ember hide

beneath--humph! queer thing that the love of twenty year ago should

suddenly spring up again to life."



he arise from his seat, and commence once more to walk up and down

the room, soliloquise in a low voice, while outside the fog be

grow quite black and a sombre twilight spread through the

apartment.



"so it's Nestley I've get to thank for rouse her suspicion. he's

be give Patience his view of my character, which no doubt will

coincide with her own--amiable creature both! she have tell Una that

there be danger to Reginald in my companionship, so either herself or

Una have write to town and frighten my shy bird into take

flight. bother these woman, how dreadfully they do upset one's

plan; however, I do not mind, my hold upon Reginald Blake be

just as firm at Garsworth as it be in London. as to patience

denounce I--pish!--melodramatic rubbish--it's too late now to talk

such nonsense--if she tell the truth her son lose the property, and

she's too fond of he to risk that. as to Blake himself, when he know

I'm his father he'll be glad enough to make term or lose the property

and Una Challoner."



he pause a moment, light a cigarette, and go to the window gaze

absently out into the black mist which cling around the roof and

chimney-pot of the house, and hide the brilliantly light street

below from his gaze.



"Una Challoner," he murmur thoughtfully. "patience think I be in

love with she. curious that I be not: she have everything a woman can

have to attract and allure a man, and yet I don't care a bit about

she. have I be in love with she I would not have trouble my head

about Reginald but let Una inherit the property, and then it would

have be a tug of war between father and son as to who marry the

heiress! that I have secure the property for our son ought to easily

convince Patience that I love money more than Una Challoner, but of

course she don't see because she be blind by jealousy--rather

complimentary to I I must say, see how hard I try to break she

heart in the past."



turn away from the window with a sigh he light the gas, then

go over to the mirror place over the fireplace he look at

himself long and critically.



"you're grow old, my friend," he murmur, "the wine of life be

run to the lee with you, and I'm afraid you'll never fall in love

again--still it's wonderful how I keep my good look--my face be my

fortune--ah, bah! and what fortune have it bring I? two dismal

room, a precarious existence, and not a friend in the world."



he laugh drearily at the dismal prospect he have conjure up and

pursue his meditation.



"I'll make one more bid for fortune, and I think I hold strong card.

if I win--as I cann't help do--I'll turn over a new leaf and become

respectable. but if I lose, and there be always the possibility of

lose, I'll throw up the sponge in England and try my luck in

America. if I don't succeed there, perhaps a friendly cowboy will put

an end to my waste life; at present, _carpe diem_, as our friend the

vicar would say, so I'll dine at the club and scribble a letter to

Patience Allerby."



he dress himself slowly, still in a dismal mood, and as he be

rattle along in a hansom he give himself an impatient shake.



"bah," he mutter with a shiver, "I've get a fit of the blue devil

with this weather. never mind, a good dinner and a bottle of wine will

soon put I right."



he have both, and feel so much well that he begin to view thing in a

more rosy light, and write a letter to Patience Allerby which entirely

satisfy he.



"there," he say gaily, as he drop it into the box, "I think that

will show my lady pretty plainly how I intend to proceed, so now as

there's nothing well to do I'll go to the theatre."



and to the theatre he go, try by every mean in his power to

shake off by mean of this fictitious gaiety the gloomy thought which

always beset he when he find himself alone.









CHAPTER XXXVI.

the calm before the storm.





after great trouble our life rearrange themselves in new form,

which last only until some late evil arise therefrom to alter they

once more, and these latter in their turn be subject to further

change, so that from cradle to tomb our fortune alter in diver way

every moment of our existence.





so the prodigal son have return after his perilous wandering in far

land, and his home circle kill the fat calf and make merry in

token of rejoice. when Una see how haggard the young man be in

appearance and how depressed in mind, she feel deeply grateful to

Providence that the chance word of Nestley have lead she to write the

letter which have induce her lover to return. now that he be once

more by her side she determine that nothing should every part they

again, and long eagerly for the marriage to take place which should

give she the right to go through life by his side. doubtless many

people would consider such longing hardly compatible with maiden

modesty, but Una be too pure and sensible a woman to look at thing

in such a false light. she ardently love Reginald and he return

that love, why then should she, for the sake of conventional

appearance, risk her life's happiness by delay, see that everything

be now at stake? no! she be determined to get married to Reginald as

soon as possible, so that he would not be lure to destruction by evil

counsel and wicked companion. it be not that she mistrust she

lover, for she well know his straightforward, honourable nature, but

it be well to leave nothing to chance, as even the strong of man

be not proof against temptation.



a week after Reginald arrive they be seat in Dr. Larcher's study

talk over the question of the marriage, and the vicar be incline

to agree with their desire that it should be soon, although he be

unwilling they should be blame for undue haste.



"the world, my dear Una, be censorious," he say, wisely, "and as the

Squire have only be dead two month it will be as well to wait a

little long."



"I suppose so," reply Una with a sigh, "although I do not see it

would mean any disrespect to his memory if we get marry at once."



"no doubt, no doubt--still, _medio tutissimus ibis_, and I think it

will be wise for you both to put off the marriage for at least three

month."



"three month," say Reginald, with a groan, "that's as bad as three

year, but I suppose we must--I will stay at Garsworth in the

meantime."



"of course, my dear boy, of course," answer the vicar, cross his

leg and place his thumb and forefinger together, "you can take up

your old life again."



"ah, never! never again," say the young man, shake his head sadly,

"the old life be dead and do with. I have eat of the tree of

knowledge, and the fruit be bitter."



"my dear Reginald," say Una, cross over to he and put she

kind arm round his neck, "you must not be so despondent--it be not

your fault."



"the sin of the father be visit on the child," he reply

gloomily, "if it have be anything else I would not have mind--but

to be what I be--a nobody--entitle to bear no name--it be bitter,

very bitter indeed. I've no doubt I should be above such petty pride,

still I be but mortal, and disgrace be hard to bear."



"if it be disgrace I will bear it with you," whisper Una, smooth

his hair, "we will be marry and go away for a time; you will soon

forget the past when we go abroad."



"with your help I hope to," he say, look affectionately into her

clear eye shine down on he with ineffable love in their azure

depth.



"I think," remark the vicar, touch by the deep sorrow of the young

man, "that take all thing into consideration it would be wise to

do as you wish."



"and marry?" cry Reginald eagerly.



"and marry," assent the vicar, nod good-naturedly; "what say

Horace? '_carpe diem quam minimum credula postero_.' so take that

advice it will be good for you both to be marry quietly next week

and go abroad for a time:--when you return Reginald will doubtless

find his position easy."



"I hope so," say Blake mournfully, as they arise to go, "but I'm

afraid it's hopeless--this discovery have kill all the pleasure of

life--my youth be dead."



"the soul be immortal," say Dr. Larcher solemnly, "and on the ruin

of your joyous youth, which you regard as dead, you can raise the

structure of a nobler and wise life--it will be hard, but with Una to

help you, not impossible--_nil mortalibus arduum est_."



and they go away from the presence of the old man--he with

resignation in his breast, and she with whispering word of comfort on

her lip, infinite pity in her eye, and endure affection in her

heart.



Patience Allerby be delighted when she hear how soon the marriage

be to take place, as she dread lest through the machination of

Beaumont it should be break off. once Reginald be married to Una he

would be safe both as regard fortune and position, for nothing

Beaumont could reveal concern the conspiracy would alter the state

of affair and her one aim in life to secure happiness for her son,

would thus be accomplish.



at present, however, she dread every day either to see Beaumont or

hear from he, especially after the warning letter she have write,

nor be she disappoint, for a week after Reginald's return she

receive a letter from her quondam lover inform she that he be

come down in order to have a proper understanding with his son.





"_the young rascal have more firmness of purpose than I give he credit

for_," he write in a cynical vein, "_and take less eagerly to the

dissipation of London than I should have expect. I be afraid he

inherit your cold blood and not the hot temperament of his father,

otherwise he would hardly have leave the only city fit to live in for a

dull hole like Garsworth. however, I see plainly he be a clod and

lack the divine z necessary to enjoy life, so I suppose he have

return in perfect contentment to marry Una Challoner and live the

bovine life of a country squire. so be it! I certainly do not mind,

but first he must settle with I. I have place he in a good position

and give he a large income, so for these service I must be

recompensed, and be come down to have an interview with he on the

subject. if he be wise he will seek to know no more than he do, but

if he inherit your obstinate nature and want to know all, I be

afraid he will have to learn the truth. even then it will not be too

late, for I to will hold my tongue as to his real birth, and leave he

in full possession of his wealth provide I be well pay for such

silence. now that you understand the situation you have well prepare

he to receive I as one who desire to be friendly--if he treat I

as an enemy he will find I a bitter one, so he have well be sensible

and come to term. as to my love for Una Challoner, you ought to know

by this time I love no one but_



"_your truly_,



   "Basil Beaumont."





this brutal letter fall like a lump of ice on the heart of the unhappy

Patience as she see the net gradually close round she. she know only

too well that Beaumont would do what he say unless some arrangement

could be make--and then, as Nestley say he love Una, he would

doubtless want to marry she as well as gain an income, and their son

would be leave miserable. no, she would not have it, this devil would

not be permit to sin any more and ruin life with impunity as he

have hitherto do. she make up her mind to see he before his

interview with Reginald, and make one last appeal to his feeling as a

father; if he refuse to grant her prayer and keep the boy ignorant

of his real birth she would reveal all herself and bear the shame

soon than he should tempt Reginald to a sin. when all be tell she

would implore Una to still marry her son, and then depart to bury

herself in solitude, and expiate her sin by year of repentance.



event be still in the future, and she know not how they would turn

out, but of one thing she be determine, that Beaumont should not

blight and ruin her son's life as he have blight and ruin her own.









CHAPTER XXXVII.

a RUINED life.





     "be this the end of all the year

        that thou hast live, my friend?

     of merry smile and bitter tear,

        be this the end?

     Tho' sad and dark the past appear,

        God to thy soul will courage send,

     and Christ will whisper in thine ear

     the word which heart despond cheer;

        so rise and to thy work attend.

     nor let the wicked ask with jeer

        be this the end?"





a few day after a decision have be arrive at concern the

marriage Basil Beaumont make his reappearance in Garsworth, and take

up his old quarter at "the House of Good living," in order to come to

a final understanding with Reginald Blake.



the artist be in an excellent humour, for, accord to his own

judgment, he be master of the situation. he have only to threaten

Reginald with the loss of his newly acquire wealth, and, judge the

young man's nature by his own, he feel satisfied that, soon than

surrender Garsworth Grange, the false heir would pay he a handsome

income to hold his tongue. with such income he would retire to the

Continent and amuse himself for the rest of his life, while, as for

Patience, see that he have no further use for she, she could make

what arrangement she like with Reginald, and please herself in her

manner of living. with all this astute calculation, however, Beaumont

make no allowance for the different nature of his son, and do not for

a moment think that the young man's nobility of soul would induce he

rather to resign everything, at whatever cost, than keep possession of

what he know be not rightfully his own.



he learn from Kossiter that Reginald and Una be go to be marry

the next week, and smile cynically to himself as he think how

easily he could stop the ceremony.



"if Una Challoner only know the truth," he think, "I think even her

love would recoil from such a trial. Reginald Blake, the wealthy

bastard, be one thing; but Reginald Blake, the pauper bastard, be

another. yes, I think I hold the good hand in this game; as to

Patience! bah! my card be somewhat too strong for she to beat."



Mr. Beaumont have only arrive a short time, and be seat before the

fire smoking in the dull light of the winter afternoon, preparatory to

write a letter to Reginald. Margery, bright and alert, be clear

away the luncheon, so Mr. Beaumont, wish to be quite sure of his

ground, begin to question she concern the event which have take

place during his absence.



"I hear Miss Challoner be go to be married to Mr. Blake," he say

genially; "it's a good match for she."



"and for he, too, sir," retort Margery indignantly. "Miss Una be as

sweet a young lady as you will find anywhere."



"no doubt," answer Beaumont blandly. "they be a charming couple,

and certainly deserve the good opinion of everybody; but tell I,

Margery, what about Dr. Nestley? I suppose he have go long ago?"



"no," say Margery, shake her head, "he be still here."



"in this place?"



"yes sir, very--very ill."



"Humph!" think Beaumont, "get the jump, I expect. what be the

matter with he?" he ask aloud.



"he lose his way in the snow storm last week," explain Margery

deliberately, "and nearly die, but Farmer Sanders find he on the

bridge and bring he here."



"oh! and be he here still?"



"he be, sir. he be quite delirious, sir--rave awful. Dr. Blank's

be attend he, and Miss Mosser."



"the blind organist--why have she turn nurse?"



Margery smile in a mysterious manner.



"well, folk say one thing and some folk say another," she reply,

fold the table-cloth, "but I think she's in love with he; anyhow,

as soon as she hear he be ill she come here like a mad woman, with

Miss Busky, and both of 'em have be nurse he ever since."



"how good of they," say Beaumont ironically, "and be he well?"



"he's sensible," answer Margery cautiously, "but very weak. I don't

know as he'll live."



"I'd like to see he. you know I'm a friend of his--do you think I

could go up to his room?"



"I don't know, sir," return Margery stolidly. "I'll ask Miss

Mosser."



"do, that's a good girl, he reply, and Margery depart.



"Poor Nestley," mutter Beaumont to himself, light another

cigarette, "it be rather a shame of I to have lead he on like I do,

but if I haven't he would have interfere with my plan concern old

Garsworth, so I have to--self-preservation be the first law of nature.

come in," he call out, as a knock come to the door. "come in,

Margery."



it be not Margery, however, but Cecilia Mosser, who enter, with a

pale sad face and a painfully-strained look in her sightless eye.



"Mr. Beaumont," she say, in her low sweet voice.



"I be here, Miss Mosser," he reply, rise from his seat. "what can

I do for you?"



"nothing," she reply, grope her way to the table and stand

beside it. "be you alone?"



"quite alone," return Beaumont politely.



"you wish to see Dr. Nestley?"



"if I may be permit."



"you will not be permit," answer Cecilia slowly; "he be still

very weak, and the sight of you would make he ill again."



"and why?" ask Beaumont, rather annoyed at the firmness of her tone;

"surely a friend----"



"a friend," she interrupt, in a low vibrate tone. "yes, a friend

who be one in name only."



"I don't understand you," say Basil politely. "what do you know of

the friendship exist between myself and Dr. Nestley?"



"I know everything--yes everything--in his delirium he reveal more

than he would have done----"



"Delirium--pshaw!"



"what he say then be confirm by his own lip afterwards when he

be sensible," she answer in a perfectly cool manner, "and I know

how much your friendship have cost he--how you try to drag he down

to the low depth of iniquity. God know for what end----"



Beaumont laugh in a sneer way, and lean his shoulder

comfortably against the mantelpiece.



"you seem to be in the confidence of our mutual friend," he say, in

an easy tone. "may I ask why?"



"because I be go to be his wife," reply Cecilia, while a flood of

crimson rush over the pure white of her face.



"his wife--a blind girl?"



"blind as I be he love I," she say indignantly, "and I can protect

he against you, Mr. Beaumont."



"I? I do not wish to harm he."



"no. you could not even if you do wish; he be go to marry I, and

I hope to undo all the harm you have do he."



"I wish you joy of your task," he reply with a sneer. "but

Dr. Nestley seem to be able to transfer his affection very

easily--perhaps you do not know he be in love with Miss Challoner."



"yes I do," she answer in a low tone, "he tell I everything; and we

understand one another perfectly. you have do your bad, Mr.

Beaumont, and can do no more--he be go to become my husband, and,

blind as I be, I hope to be his guardian angel from such man as you."



"these domestic detail don't interest I in the slight," he

answer contemptuously, wave his hand. "will you be kind enough to

go, Miss Mosser? I have some letter to write."



"I be go," answer the blind girl, quietly feel her way

to the door. "I only come to tell you that you will never see he

again--never!"



"neither will you," he return brutally.



the poor girl burst into tear at the unmanly taunt, but hastily dry

they, and answer he back proudly.



"I can see he in my own mind, sir," she say indignantly, "and that

be all I wish for--his fault have be of your making, and not of his

own. I say good-bye to you, sir, and only wish you a well heart,

that you may not make a jest of the misfortune of other."



as she close the door after she, Beaumont feel rather ashamed of

himself, but soon recover from the feeling, and sit down at the

table to write a note to Reginald.



"Bah!" he say, as his pen travel swiftly over the paper. "what do

I care? if he like to encumber himself with that woman he can do so.

I don't suppose I'll ever see he again in this life, nor do I wish

to--my business now be with my dear son. I'll get what I want out of

he, and then the whole lot of they can go to the devil."



meanwhile, Cecilia have return to the sick room, where Miss Busky,

still faithful to her blind friend, sat watch by the bedside

of the invalid. a pale, sickly light filter in through the

white-curtain window, mix with the red glow of the fire, and in

this curiously blend twilight could be see the glimmer of the

medicine bottle on the round table by the bed, the deep arm-chair

close at hand wherein Miss Busky sit the milky whiteness of the

disorder bed-clothe and the subdue shine upon the surface of the

furniture. throughout the room be a complete stillness, unbroken even

by the tick of a clock, and nothing be hear but the heavy breathing

of the sick man.



as Cecilia enter, Miss Busky arise lightly to her foot and cross

over to her friend, speak in a subdue whisper.



"do you see he?" she ask.



"yes--he will not come up, thank Heaven!--Dr. Nestley suspect

nothing?"



"Nothing!--he be asleep--let I place you in the chair--I'm go out

for a few minute."



she lead Cecilia forward, and the blind girl-sink into the arm-chair;

then, hastily put on her hat, Miss Busky glide rapidly out of the

room, leave Cecilia seat by the bed, listen to the breathing of

the invalid.



so still, so quiet--it might almost have be the silence of the tomb.

then there come the light patter of rain-drop on the window. the

fire have sink to a dull red glow, and a piece of burn coal drop,

with a singularly distinct noise, on to the fender. Nestley sigh in

his sleep--move uneasily, and then awake--a fact which the blind girl

be aware of immediately, by her acute sense of hearing.



"Cecilia," say the sick man, in a weak voice.



"I be here, dear," she reply softly. "do you want anything?"



he put out his hand and clasp one of her in his feeble grasp.



"only you--only you--I think you have leave I."



"Hush!--you must not speak much," she say, arrange the bed-clothe.



"I have have a dream," whisper the invalid fearfully, "a strange

dream--that I be in the coil of a serpent, be crush to death.

but a woman suddenly appear, and at her touch the serpent vanish

and I be free. the woman have your face, Cecilia."



"Hush!--do not speak more--you be too weak--you be in safety now,

and no serpent shall touch you while I be by your side."



"you will be my wife?"



"I will be your wife," she reply softly. "I have love you from the

first day I meet you, but never think you would be burden with such

a useless thing as I."



"not useless, dear. how could I have be so foolish as not to have

understand your love before? thank God for this illness, that have

open my eye. you have save my life--my soul."



he stop, through exhaustion, and lie silently upon his pillow,

watch the red flare of the fire glimmer on the pale face of the

blind girl. a great feeling of joy and thankfulness come over he, as

he feel that all the stormy, tempestuous life of the past be over at

last--and beside he sit the one woman who could save his weak nature

from yield to the temptation of the world.









CHAPTER XXXVIII.

MATER DOLOROSA.





     "Madonna, who hath ever stand

     as type of holy motherhood,

     I pray thee, for thy Son's dear sake,

     this sorrow from my bosom take.

     for there be those, with anger wild,

     who wind the mother thro' the child.

     I know that thou wilt pity I,

     for thy Son hang upon the tree.

     and as he die to save and bless,

     oh, help I, thou, in my distress."





after he have finish a very nice little dinner, with a small bottle

of champagne to add z to it, Mr. Beaumont light a cigarette, and

sit down comfortably before the fire, in order to wait for Reginald

Blake. he have write to the young man, announce his arrival and

ask he to call, so he have no doubt but that he would be favour

with a visit. have, therefore, arrange his plan of action, he lie

back indolently in his chair, make plan for the future, and

build air-castle amid the blue spiral of smoke which curl

upward from his lip.



about seven o'clock he hear a knock at the door, and in answer to his

invitation to enter, a woman make her appearance. Beaumont, who have

merely turn his head to greet Reginald, be rather astonished at

this unexpected guest, and arise to his foot in order to see who it

be. his visitor close the door carefully after she and step

forward so that she come within the circle of light cast by the lamp

on the table, then, throw back her veil, look steadily at the

artist.



"Patience!"



"yes, Patience," she reply, sit down on a chair near the table.

"you do not expect to see I?"



"well, no," answer Beaumont, indolently lean against the

mantelpiece. "I must confess I do not--but if you want to speak with

I, I can spare you very little time, as I be waiting----"



"for Reginald?" she interrupt quickly. "yes, I know that."



"the deuce you do! what a wonderful woman you be! how do you find

out I be here?"



"I leave instruction that I be to be inform of your arrival, as I

wish to speak with you before you see our son."



"indeed! and what do you want to speak to I about?"



"your letter."



"I think my letter be too clear to require further explanation," he

say impatiently. "I tell you my intention."



"you do--and I have come to tell you they will not be carry out."



"be that so?" say Beaumont, with a sneer. "well, we'll see. who will

prevent I do what I like?"



"I will."



"really--I'm afraid you over-rate your power, my dear Patience. you

be a clever woman, no doubt--a very clever woman--but there be

limit."



"as you observe, very truly, there be limit," she retort fiercely,

"and those limit you have overstep. do you think I be go to

stand by and see you wring money out of my son?"



"our son," he correct gently. "you forget I be his father. as to

wring money out of he, that's a very unpleasant way of put it.

I simply propose to appeal to his common sense."



"sit down," say Patience, suddenly. "I wish to speak to you."



Beaumont shrug his shoulder, then, push the arm-chair to one

side, sit down in it so that he face she fairly, keep, however,

with habitual caution, his face well in the shade.



"by all mean," he say amiably. "I always humour a woman when there

be nothing to be gain by do otherwise. go on, my dear friend, I'm

all attention."



the housekeeper be lean forward, rest her elbow on the table,

and he could see her finely-cut, bloodless face--look as if carve

out of marble, in the yellow ray of the lamp-light--with her nostril

dilate, her lip firmly close, and her black eye sparkle with

suppress anger.



"I see it's go to be a duel to the death," he say, in a mocking

tone, lean his head against the cushion of the chair. "well, I do

not mind--I'm fond of duel."



"you be a fiend!" she burst out angrily.



"really! do you come all this way to impart that information? if so,

you have waste your time. I've hear the same remark so often."



his brutally cool manner have a wonderfully calming effect upon she,

for after this one outburst of anger, she appear to crush down her

wrath by a strong effort of will, smile disdainfully, and go on to

speak in a cold, clear voice.



"listen to I, Basil Beaumont: year ago you do I the bad harm a

man can do a woman--you destroy my life, but thank to my own

cleverness I manage to preserve at least the outward semblance of a

pure woman without sacrifice our son in any way, but do you think

that have cost I nothing--do you think I do not feel bitter pang at

have to deny my own son, and to veil my maternal longing under the

guise of a servant? I do so, not so much to preserve my own good name

as to benefit the boy. I want he to think he have no heritage of

shame, so that he could feel at least pride and self-respect. when I

obtain the reward of my sacrifice--when I see that my son be

satisfied with his lot and have talent to make his way in the world

you come down for the second time to ruin not my life, but his--the

life of an innocent being, who have never do you any harm. I enter

into your vile conspiracy because I think it would benefit my son,

and now I repent bitterly that I ever do so. owe to the foul lie

you compel I to tell, he have gain a fortune, but lose his

self-respect. you do not understand the feeling, because your heart

these many year have be steep in wickedness, but think what it have

do to our unhappy child--cast a blight upon his life which no money,

no position can ever remove--his youth die from the moment I tell he

that lie, and whose work be it--mine or your, Basil Beaumont? mine or

your?"



she pause a moment, moisten her dry lip with her tongue, and then

go on speak rapidly with vehemence.



"and now when the bad be over--when he be firmly settle in

possession of that wealth it have cost he his youthful happiness to

gain--when he be go to marry the woman he love, who will be able

to comfort he in some measure--you once more return to work ruin for

the third time--you demand money to hush up a disgraceful secret--you

would not only tell he that he be still a nameless outcast, but you

would take all his money from he, yes, and take also the girl who be

to be his wife--you would leave he a pauper--an outcast--a miserable

be with neither self-respect, nor rich, nor consolation. I

implore you for my sake--for his sake--for your own sake, not to do

this--our crime have shadow his young life too much already--tell he

no more--go away from this place, and let he have at least one chance

of happiness."



she arise to her foot at the last word, and stretch out her arm

appealingly towards Beaumont with humid eye and an imploring

expression on her face. the artist sit silent, smile cynically, with

a savage glitter in his eye, and when she have finish, break into a

hard laugh as he also arise to his foot, fling his cigarette

viciously into the fire.



"a very pretty thing to ask I to do," he say mockingly, "and a very

useless request to make. do you think I care for his feeling or

yours?--not the snap of a finger. I put Reginald in possession of the

Garsworth estate not for his own sake, but for mine. have he be wise

and allow I to guide he, he would have know no more than he do

now. if he give I the money I ask, it be even now not too late, but

I be not go to spare he, either for his own sake or your. he will

be here soon, and I will tell he everything, so if he do not give

I what I ask, I'll ruin he body and soul."



Patience fling herself at his foot, and burst into tear.



"for God's sake, Basil, spare he."



"no."



"he be your child."



"the more reason for he to help I."



"have you no mercy?"



"none--if it mean get no money."



"for my sake, spare he."



"for your sake least of all."



"you intend to tell he?"



"I do. you can save yourself the trouble of make this melodramatic

exhibition. I'm not go to move one hair's breath from the position

I have take up. I want money, and I mean to have it."



Patience spring to her foot in an access of mad fury and stand before

he with clench hand and blaze eye.



"be you not afraid I'll kill you?"



"not a bit."



"you defy I."



"I do."



she draw a long breath, and snatch up her glove from the table, her

passion subside under his cool brutality as a stormy sea subside

when oil be cast upon the water.



"very well," she say coolly. "I'll tell everything to Doctor Larcher,

and get he to prosecute both of we for conspiracy. I will stand in

the dock and you beside I."



Beaumont laugh sneeringly.



"I've no doubt you will stand in the dock," he say with emphasis,

"but not I. I have do nothing in the matter, you everything. who be

to prove I hypnotise the old man, and forge the paper make

Reginald the heir?--no one. who be to prove that you falsely pass

off your son as the heir?--everyone. you be the sole representative

of the conspiracy, and I shall simply deny the whole affair. it will

be my word against your, and with such strong evidence as can be

bring against you I fancy you'll get the bad of it."



an expression of terror pass over the face of the unhappy woman as

she see what a gulf be open at her foot. it be true what he

say--she be the only one who have speak--to all outward appearance

he have in nowise be implicate in the conspiracy. with a cry of

despair, she reel back against the wall, cover her face with her

hand. at that moment Reginald's voice be hear outside, and with a

rapid movement, Beaumont spring forward and catch one of her wrist

in his grip.



"here be Reginald," he say in a harsh whisper, "hold your tongue or

it will be the bad for you. I don't want he to see you--hide in

here and keep silent. what I intend to do will depend upon the result

of this interview."



Patience say nothing, as all power of will seem to have desert

she, but allow herself to be drag towards a door in the wall

which communicate with a staircase lead to the upper part of the

house. push she in here, Beaumont close the door, then rapidly

return to the fireplace and fling himself into his chair.



"Act I. have be rather stormy," he say to himself with a sneer. "I

wonder what Act II. will be like."









CHAPTER XXXIX.

FATHER and SON.





     Father!--art thou my father?--pause, good sir,

     Ere thou profane thus that holy name.

     a father should protect and guide his child

     through the harsh tumult of this noisy life,

     but thou hast stand apart these many year

     and leave I to the mercy of the world,

     with all its snare and madd'ning influence,

     yet now thou say'st "I be thy father"--nay,

     no name be that for such a one as thou.





look at that quiet room illuminate by the mellow light of the

lamp, no one could have imagine the scene of terror and despair which

have lately take place, yet when Reginald enter, his face wear a

somewhat puzzled expression.



"how do you do, Beaumont?" he say as the artist arise with a frank

smile and take his hand. "I think I hear a scream."



"do you?" reply Beaumont, assist his visitor to remove his great

coat. "then I'm afraid I must have be asleep, as I hear nothing,

not even your knock; the opening of the door arouse I."



"I don't knock at all," say Reginald, sit down by the fire and

draw his chair close to the burn coal. "I should have do so,

but I forgot and walk straight in--you don't mind, do you?"



"not at all, my boy, you be perfectly welcome," answer the artist

heartily. "will you smoke?"



"thank you, I've get my pipe."



he light his pipe and lie back in the chair watch the fire, while

Beaumont, bend forward with his face in the shadow puff at his

cigarette, watch Reginald, and crouch on the dark staircase with

her eye to the keyhole, a silent woman watch both. it be a curious

situation and not without a touch of grim comedy, though, as a matter

of fact, the play which the trio be about to act have more in it of

the tragic than the comic element.



Reginald, look sad and weary, watch the fire for some moment,

till Beaumont, feel the silence oppressive, break it with a laugh.



"how fearfully dull you be, Blake," he say gaily, "be anything

wrong?"



Blake withdraw his sad eye from the fire and look at the speaker

with a singular smile.



"not what many people would call wrong," he say at length. "I have a

large income, I be young, and I marry the girl I love next week."



"well, as you cann't call any of those blessing wrong, my friend, you

ought to be perfectly happy."



"no doubt--but perfect happiness be give to no mortal."



"you be very young to moralize," say Beaumont with a faint sneer.



"yes, it appear absurd, don't it, but I cann't help it; ever since I

discover the real story of my birth a shadow seem to have fall on

my life."



"and why--who care for the bar sinister now-a-day?"



"not many people I suppose, but I do--I daresay I have be bring up

in an old-fashioned manner, but I feel the loss of my good name

keenly--wealth can gild shame, not hide it."



"Rubbish! you be morbidly sensitive on the subject."



"no doubt I be--as I say before it's the fault of my bring up--but

come," he continue in a lively tone, "I do not call to inflict my

dismal mood upon you, let we talk of other thing."



"such as your marriage?"



"certainly--marriage be a pleasant subject," say the young man with a

quiet smile. "as I tell you, I marry Miss Challoner next week and then

we go abroad for a year or two."



"and what about your property in the meantime?" ask Beaumont.



"oh, I'll leave it to my solicitor to attend to."



"why not appoint I your agent?"



Blake colour a little at this direct request and smile in an

embarrassed manner.



"well, I hardly see how I can do that," he say frankly, "I've only

know you about three month, and besides, I have perfect confidence

in my solicitor to manage the property, so, with all due respect to

you, Beaumont, I must decline to appoint you my agent."



he speak with some haughtiness, as he be irritated at the cool way in

which Beaumont speak, but that gentleman seem in nowise offend and

smile blandly as he answer:



"if then, you will not help I in that way, will you give I some

money--say five hundred pound?"



"certainly not!" retort Blake hotly, push back his chair, "why

should I do such a thing? as I say before, I have only know you

three month--you be kind enough to introduce I to some friend of

yours in Town, beyond this our friendship do not extend--I have yet

to learn that gentleman go about request sum of money from

comparative stranger."



"you have yet to learn a good many thing," say Beaumont coolly,

irritate by the independent tone of the young man, "and one be that

you must give I the money I ask."



Blake jump to his foot in amazement at the peremptory tone of the

artist and look at he indignantly.



"Must!" he repeat angrily, "I don't understand the word--what right

have you to speak to I in such a manner?--if you think you've get a

fool to deal with you be very much mistaken--I decline to lend or

give you a sixpence, and furthermore I also decline your acquaintance

from this moment."



he snatch up his overcoat and put it on, but Beaumont, still cool

and unruffled, sit smile in his chair.



"wait a moment," he say slowly, "you have well understand the

situation before you leave this room."



Reginald Blake, who have turn his back on the artist, swing round

with a dangerous expression in his dark eye.



"I understand the situation perfectly, sir; you think I be a young

fool, who, having come into money, be simple enough to play the part

of pigeon to your hawk."



Beaumont arise slowly from his chair at this insulting speech, and

frown ominously, while the woman hide behind the door watch the

pair in a cat-like manner, ready to intervene if she see cause.



"you have well take care, my boy," say Beaumont deliberately. "I be

your friend now, beware lest you make I your enemy."



"do you think I care two straw for either your friendship or enmity?"

reply Blake with supreme contempt, look the artist up and down.

"if so, you be mistaken--what can you do to harm I I should like to

know?"



"then you shall know--I can dispossess you of your wealth and leave

you a pauper."



"hardly--see I now know your true character and touch neither

dice-box nor card."



"it will require neither dice-box nor card," reply Beaumont,

wince at this home thrust, "I can dispense with those aid--and I

can reduce you to your former position of a pauper and stop your

marriage."



"indeed! then do so."



Beaumont be sting to sudden fury by the young man's coolness, and

lose his temper.



"you defy I!" he hiss, advance towards Blake. "you dare to defy

I, you pauper--you outcast--you bastard!"



"liar!"



in another moment Reginald have his hand upon Beaumont's throat, his

face convulse with rage, when suddenly Patience spring forth from her

hiding-place.



"stop! he be your father."



Blake's grip relax, and his arm fall by his side while Beaumont,

stagger back, fall into the arm-chair and begin mechanically to

arrange his disorder necktie.



"my father!"



it be Reginald who speak in a dull, slow voice, with his face ghastly

pale and his eye fix upon the cower form of the woman before

he.



"my father! be this true?"



Patience try to speak, but her tongue could not form the word, so

Beaumont, with a devilish light in his eye, answer for she.



"quite true. your mother have tell you."



"my mother! you?"



the young man look from one to the other in a daze manner, then,

with a gasp cry, stagger forward and seize Patience by the arm.



"do you hear what this man say?" he say in a strained, unnatural

voice. "that he be my father--that you be my mother! be it true--tell

I--be it true?"



"it be true."



a look of horror overspread his face, and fling she away from he,

with a cry of anguish he fall against the wall with white face and

outstretche arm.



"my God! it be true."



his mother look apprehensively at he for a moment, then fall on her

knee weep bitterly.



"spurn I--curse I--despise I!" she cry in a broken voice. "you

have every right to do so. I be your unhappy mother and he be your

father. I lie when I say Fanny Blake and the Squire be your

parent. I lie at your father's instigation in order to gain you a

fortune. he design the conspiracy--I carry it out."



"and I have be the dupe of both," interrupt Reginald fiercely,

step forward with uplifted hand as if to strike she. "I don't

believe this--it be a lie! you be my nurse."



"I be your mother."



the calm manner in which she make this assertion leave no room for

doubt, and Reginald Blake recoil from that kneeling figure as if it

have be a snake.



"my mother!" he mutter convulsively. "Great Heavens! my mother!"



Patience see how he shrink from she, and a great wave of despair sweep

over her soul as she struggle forward on her knee, fling out her

arm towards he with a bitter cry.



"oh, forgive I--forgive I!" she wail. "I do it for the good; I

do, indeed. I deny you be my child in order to save your good

name, and I only swear the lie about Fanny Blake in order to make you

rich. do not shrink from I, my son, I implore you. think how I have

suffer all these year--how I have sacrifice my life for your sake.

have pity, Reginald, as you hope for mercy. have mercy!"



Reginald Blake stand quiet for a moment, then, control himself by

a powerful effort, raise she to her foot. as he do so she look

timidly at his face, but see therein no pity, no tenderness; only the

look of a man suffer agony. he place she in a chair and, without

look at she, advanced towards the table.



"before I can believe this story," he say in a hard voice, "I require

some proof of it. by the Squire's will the property be leave to the

person who produce a certain paper, write by he, and a ring. they

be both find in his desk, direct to I. if I be not the Squire's

son how do this happen?"



"I can explain that very easily," reply Beaumont, take some paper

out of his breast coat pocket. "when I come down here a few month

ago, I hear of the Squire's madness regard his re-incarnation, and

by mean of a hypnotic sleep I find out from his own lip that he

intend to leave all his property to a fictitious son, who be to be

himself in a new body. be under my control in the hypnotic state,

he show I where the paper and ring be hide. I take they from

their hiding place and fill up the paper with your name and that of

Fanny Blake. I then enclose the ring and paper in an envelope which

the Squire have direct to you, reseal it, and, get the key of

his desk, place they therein, where they be find. you understand?"



"I understand; but why do the Squire direct an envelope to I?"



"because he want to help you, and write this letter and this cheque,

which he enclose in an envelope to be give to you by your mother. I

use the envelope as I explain, and keep the letter and cheque by

I. here they be as a proof of the truth."



Reginald take up the paper the artist place upon the table and

glance over they, then place they in his pocket, and turn away

take up his hat.



"where be you go?" ask Beaumont, alarm at his action.



"I be go to see Dr. Larcher and tell he all," answer his son

sternly. "what other course be there for I to take?"



"to hold your tongue," say the artist eagerly. "surely you're not

such a fool as to give up possession of an estate like this for a mere

feeling of honour. pay I a stated income and I will hold my tongue.

your mother will be silent for her own sake, so no one will know the

truth."



Reginald look at he with unutterable contempt.



"after bring I so low as you have do do you think I be go to

sink low of my own free will?" he say in a scornful tone. "no! a

thousand time no. I would not keep this property another day if it

be ten million a year. I see what your plan have be--to threaten I

with exposure if I do not bribe you to silence. you have mistake I.

I be not so base as that. this property shall go back to its rightful

owner, and you will not receive one penny either from she or from I."



"I be your father."



"you be my father--yes, God help I! if I be to believe this story

you be my father--a father I despise and loathe. one question more I

only ask--be you my mother's husband?"



"no," say Beaumont sullenly, "I be not."



Reginald turn a shade paler and laugh bitterly.



"what have I do to be punish like this?" he say, raise his face

in agony. "you have take away the wealth I wrongfully possess, you

have deprive I of my good name, of my self-respect, but, as God be

above we, you shall not make I vile in my own sight by do your

wicked will."'



another moment and the door close, so that Patience and Beaumont be

alone. rise from her seat she take off her bonnet.



"what be you go to do?" ask Beaumont savagely, all his innate

brutality show itself now that the mask be drop.



"I be go to stay here, to-night," she say, unsteadily walk to

the door, "and to-morrow I will go to London, never to return."



"what about the Grange?"



"I shall never go back to the Grange," answer the woman slowly,

"there be no home for I there; you have do your bad, Basil

Beaumont--do your bad--and fail."



again the door close and Beaumont be leave alone--alone with his

ruin hope and his despair.



"fail," he mutter savagely, look into the fire. "yes, I have

fail to get the money, but I shall not fail to ruin Reginald Blake

for all that; he think he will still marry the heiress of the Grange;

he can set his mind at rest--he will never marry Una Challoner."









CHAPTER XL.

BEAUMONT play his last card.





     though he seem to thee an angel

        let he not thy heart beguile,

     he's a devil from a strange hell,

        evil lurk beneath his smile.





round the old Grange the wind be howl dismally, and now that the

thaw have set in the sadness of the place be increase by the

incessant dripping of the melted snow. the dead leave in the park

be sodden and heavy, so heavy, indeed, that they could not be move

by the keen wind, which, in revenge, shake the bare bough of the

tree, or whistle dismally through the crack and cranny of the old

building.



Una sit at the window of the parlour look out at the heavy, grey

sky, to which the bleak tree lift up their gaunt arm, and

listen to the monotonous dripping on the terrace. but, in spite of

the dreariness and solitude of the place, surely her heart should have

be light and her face gayer than it be, see that in a few day

she be go to be unite to the man she love. but the shadow on the

dismal landscape also rest upon her face, and even the lively

chatter of Miss Cassy about the wedding could not bring a smile into

her mournful eye.



"I'm sure, Una dear, I'm glad you're go to be marry," say Miss

Cassy, who have put the tea cosy on her head preparatory to leave the

room, "but really I don't know what's come over thing; you look

so sad--quite like a mourner, you know--the Mourning Bride of

what's-his-name--and then for Patience to stay away all night! why

do she do it?--why!--why!--she never do it before, and then those

letter you get this morning, what be they about?--it's all so odd,

I really don't know what thing be come to."



"thing be go very well, aunt," say Una with a faint smile.

"Patience stay all night in the village because of the storm last

night, and as to those letter, I'll tell you all about they later

on."



"yes, do, let I share your confidence, at least. I bring you up

from pinafore, you know, quite like my own child. oh, I wish I

have one. why haven't I a child? now, I know what you're go to

say--marriage, of course--but I've never have the chance, nobody want

to marry I--so odd--I would have make a love wife--quite like an

ivy--really a cling ivy. oh, if I could only find my oak."



the little lady flutter tearfully out of the room, leave Una

sit alone with the letter on her lap, look out at the dreary

scene. she sigh sadly, and gather the letter together arise from

her chair, when just at that moment a ring come to the front-door

bell. Una start apprehensively and her pale face grow yet pale, but

she say nothing, only stand like a statue by the window with an

expectant look upon her face. hardly have the harsh jingle of the bell

cease to echo through the house when jellick enter, and wriggle

up to Una, announce in a hiss whisper that Mr. Beaumont desire to

see she.



"Mr. Beaumont," murmur Una, start suddenly, "what do he want, I

wonder? I'd well see he, it may do some good--some good. yes!" she

say aloud, "I will see he; jellick, show Mr. Beaumont into this

room."



she resume her seat by the window as jellick vanish, and shortly

afterwards the door open and Basil Beaumont, look haggard and

fierce, stand before she. he bow, but do not attempt any warmer

greeting, and she, on her part, simply point to a chair near she,

upon which he take his seat.



"I suppose you be astonish to see I, Miss Challoner?" he say,

after a pause.



"I confess I be a little," she reply calmly, "I think you be up

in London."



"so I be, but I come down to Garsworth yesterday."



"indeed? our quiet little village must have great attraction to draw

you away from London."



"I do not come down without an object, Miss Challoner," he say

gravely, "I have a duty to fulfil."



"towards whom?"



"Yourself. yes, I come down from London especially to see you."



"it's very kind of you to take so much trouble upon my account," she

say coldly, look keenly at he. "may I ask what this duty be to

which you allude?"



"it be the duty of an honest man towards a wrong woman," say

Beaumont quietly.



"mean I?"



"mean yourself," he assert solemnly.



"you speak in riddle, Mr. Beaumont," say Una, fold her hand. "I

will be very glad if you will explain they."



"certainly. two month ago your cousin die and leave all his property

to a suppose son, who turn out to be Reginald Blake; I have now to

inform you that Reginald Blake be no connection whatever of Squire

Garsworth, consequently his assumption of the property be a fraud."



"what do you mean, sir?" say Una quickly. "I understand Mr. Blake's

identity be fully established----"



"yes, by Patience Allerby," interrupt Beaumont quickly. "she say he

be the son of Fanny Blake and the Squire, know such a statement to

be false."



"then who be Mr. Blake's parent?"



"Patience Allerby and myself."



Una arise from her seat with an angry colour in her cheek.



"you--you Reginald's father--impossible!"



"it's perfectly true," he reply calmly. "Patience Allerby come up to

London many year ago with I, and when Reginald be bear she leave I

and come down here, bring up our son under another name. I, as you

know, come to Garsworth some time ago, and see she again, but she

ask I to say nothing, so I obey she, but now that I find she have

commit a fraud, of which you be the victim, I naturally hasten to

put it right."



"do Mr. Blake know he be not the heir?"



"he do from the first," assert Beaumont audaciously. "I have no

doubt his mother tell he his true birth, and know the Squire's

mania about re-incarnation they make this conspiracy up together in

order to defraud you of the property."



"so Mr. Blake have deceive I?" say Una, in an unnaturally quiet

tone.



"yes, he have deceive you all along. I have no doubt he prepare all

the forge document which prove his identity with the suppose son,

and count on your love for he not to prosecute should anything be

discover. I'm glad I have be able to warn you in time. you will

never marry he now."



"but the property; do you think he will keep the property?"



"he will try to I've no doubt," say Beaumont gravely, "but if you

intrust your case to experienced hand, I have no doubt he will be

make to disgorge his plunder."



"but to whom can I turn?" say Una helplessly. "I have no friend."



Beaumont arise to his foot, and come close to she.



"yes, you have one--myself."



"you?" she cry, recoil with a shudder.



"yes. I love you passionately, Una, and if you will be my wife, I will

recover your property for you."



"but--your own son?"



"I despise a son who could act as Reginald have do. I come down here

expect to find an honourable man, but instead I discover a

scoundrel, a forger, and a thief."



"be it all true what you have say?" murmur Una, look straight at

he.



"all true," he answer solemnly, "I swear it."



"you liar!"



he start back in amazement, for she be face he like an enraged

tigress, with crimson cheek and blaze eye.



"what do you mean?" he say in a hoarse whisper.



"mean?" she repeat scornfully. "that I know all, Basil Beaumont. do

you see this letter? I receive it from your unhappy son this morning,

give I back the property and reveal the whole of your nefarious

scheme. I know who forge the document--you! I know who hope to

enjoy the money through Reginald--you! I know who come with lie on

his lip to part I from the only man I love--you! yes--you! you!

you!"



the baffled schemer stand nervously finger his hat, with a white

sullen face, all his courage having leave he. so mean, so cowardly, so

despicable he look, shrink back against the wall before this

young girl, who tower over he like an inspire Pythoness.



"you tell I Reginald Blake know of this base conspiracy," she say

with contempt. "do this letter look like it? you say he will refuse

to give up the property--this letter say he surrender it of his own

free will--and you have the insolence to speak of love to I. you--who

so shamefully trick and betray Patience Allerby--you contemptible

hound!"



he try to smile defiantly, and make an effort to form a word with

his white quivering lip, but both attempt be a failure, and

without glance at she he slunk towards the door, look like a

beat hound.



"yes, slink away like the craven you be," she cry disdainfully,

"and leave Garsworth at once, or I will prosecute you for your

scoundrelly conduct. yes, though you be twenty time Reginald's

father."



"I've spoil his chance anyhow," he hiss venomously.



"you have spoil nothing of the sort," she retort superbly. "do you

think I believe the word of a vile thing like you against this

letter? I be go to Reginald Blake, to day, and will place myself

and my fortune in his hand--in spite of your falsehood I will marry

he, and he will still be master of Garsworth Grange--but, as for you,

leave the village at once, or I will have you hound out of it, as

you deserve to be--you cur!"



he be white with anger and shame, try to speak, but with an

imperious gesture she stop he with one word:



"go!"



he slunk out of the door at once, a ruined and disgrace man.









CHAPTER XLI.

a woman'S heart.





     when Dame Fortune frown severest,

        then I love thee good of all,

     I will cling to thee, my dearest,

        though the world in ruin fall.





Dr. Larcher be in his study talk to Reginald Blake, who sit near

the write table, lean his head upon his hand with his arm rest

on the desk. the face of the good Vicar be somewhat clouded, as he

feel deeply for the unhappy young man, and he be try to speak

word of comfort to he, although he feel how difficult it be to

converse cheerfully under present circumstance. Reginald, however,

have take this second discovery more easily than he have do the

first, perhaps because he have suffer so much already that he could

not suffer more. at all event, his face, though pale, be perfectly

compose, and there be a look of determination about his lip and a

serene light in his eye which give great satisfaction to Dr. Larcher.



"I must say, my dear boy," he say kindly, "that you have great cause

for sorrow, but you must bear adversity like a man, and I feel sure

the result will be beneficial to your future life--soon or later we

all feel what Goethe call 'world sorrow,' and it be that which

change we from careless youth to thoughtful manhood--your trial have

come early and have be a more bitter one than that of most man, but

believe I, out of this apparent evil good will come; remember the

saying of the old roman lyrist, _Perrupit Acheronta Herculeus

labor_--time will bring you relief, and, if you resist manfully, you

also will be able to break through this Acheron of sorrow and pain."



Reginald listen attentively to this long discourse, and, at its

conclusion, lift his head proudly.



"I agree with all you say, sir," he reply steadily, "and hope to

profit by your advice, but you must not think I a mere weakling who

give in without a struggle when trial come. no, I think your

training have teach I more than that. I feel bitterly the

circumstance of my birth, and in have parent I can neither honour

nor respect, but the cruell blow of all be that I must renounce all

hope of the woman I love--it be very hard, indeed, to almost gain the

prize and then lose it through no fault of my own."



"I think you misjudge Una," say the vicar quietly, "she be not the

woman to act in such a way--in fact, now that you have meet with

misfortune, I think she will love you more than before."



"I hope so, yet I doubt it," reply the young man gloomily; "but now

that all my past be end in ruin I must look to the future and try

and win a respected name--which I have not get now. but first, what be

I to do about my parent?"



"regard your father," say the vicar thoughtfully, "I don't think

you will see any more of he, as he will probably leave the village

to-day--now that he can gain nothing from you he will probably leave

you alone--but as to your mother, your place be certainly by she

side."



"but look how she have deceive I."



"if she have err it be through love of you," reply Dr. Larcher

gravely, "and after all she be bind to you by the tie of nature.

yes, you must look after she; but what about yourself?"



"I will go to London and make a fortune by my voice."



"your last sojourn in London be not productive of any good result,"

say the vicar in gentle rebuke.



"perhaps not, but if I err it be with my head not my heart. I be

miserable, and try to drown my sorrow in dissipation, but now I go

to town under widely different circumstance--a pauper where I once

be wealthy--so my only dissipation now will be hard work."



"that be right," say the vicar, approvingly. "I be glad to see you

accept the inevitable in such spirit--_levius fit patientia Quidquid

corrigere est nefas_."



"it's the only spirit in which I can accept the future," answer

Reginald sadly, "see that I be to pass the rest of my life without

Una."



"as I say before, you wrong she; she be too noble a woman to leave

you now you be in trouble."



"I wish I be as certain as you be," say Blake, rise to his foot

and walk to and fro, "but after what have pass I be afraid to

hope."



at this moment a knock come to the door, and immediately afterwards

Una Challoner enter. she look pale in her dark mourning garment,

but there be a soft light in her eye as they rest on Reginald

which comfort the vicar greatly.



"welcome, my dear," he say heartily, rise and take her hand, "you

could not have come at a happy time. Reginald have great need of you,

so I will leave you both together, and I hope you will prove the David

to his Saul, in order to chase away the evil shadow that be on he."



when the vicar have depart and close the door after he Una stand in

silence, look at Reginald, who have sit down again. so sad, so

despondent be his attitude, that all the love of her heart go out

towards he, and walk gently up to her lover she touch his

shoulder.



"Reginald."



"yes," he say, lift his heavy eye to her face. "what be it? have

you come to reproach I?"



"reproach you with what, my poor boy?" she ask, tenderly, kneeling

beside he. "what have you do that I should come to you with harsh

word?"



"you be a good woman, Una," say Blake sadly, put his hand

caressingly upon her head, "but I think there be a limit even to your

forbearance."



"what nonsense you talk," she say lightly. "I understand

everything--you be not responsible for the sin of your parent."



"I cannot marry you now," he reply in a low voice. "I can offer you

nothing except poverty and a dishonour name."



"you can offer I yourself," say Una with a smile, "and that be all I

want. as to your dishonour name, you forget you have give that

up--your name now be Reginald Garsworth."



"it be, but I surrender it with the property."



"I hardly see that, see there be no question of surrender. yes,"

she go on, see the astonishment depict on his face, "thing be

go to remain exactly as they be. you will still be titular lord of

the manor, and we will look upon this conspiracy of your unhappy

parent as if it have never exist."



"impossible," he mutter. "I cannot rob you of your property."



"don't I tell you there be no robbery?" she reply rapidly. "as man

and wife we will share the property in common, so there be no

necessity for you to surrender what will soon come back to you by

marriage."



"I have give up all hope of the marriage!"



"ah! you don't know how determined I be when I take a thing into my

head," she say playfully. "we will be marry next week, and you will

retain the property just as if nothing have occur. no one know the

truth of the affair except your parent, and they will not speak."



"my father will, I know his vindictive nature."



"your father!" she repeat contemptuously. "don't speak of Basil

Beaumont by that name. he have be no father to you, and as for

speak you can set your mind at rest. he call upon I this

morning, and I soon settle everything."



"he call on you?"



"yes, with a lot of lie in his mouth, but I threaten to prosecute

he if he do not leave the village, so by this time I think he be out

of the neighbourhood. don't trouble, my dear, Beaumont will hold his

tongue for his own sake."



"and my mother?"



"I call at Kossiter's as I pass," she answer, "and find your

mother have go up to London this morning. we must find she out and

give she some money to live on, for after all, whatever part she have

take in this conspiracy it be for love of you."



"just what Dr. Larcher say."



"so you see everything be settle," she say joyously, rise from her

knee, "we will be marry next week and you will be master of

Garsworth Grange."



Reginald be deeply affect by her noble conduct, and rise to his

foot embrace she fondly.



"you be a noble woman," he say, with tear in his eye, "but can I

accept this sacrifice?"



"why will you use such a word?--there be no sacrifice in what I do for

the man I love."



"remember I bring you nothing."



"you bring I yourself, that be all I want. let the past be forget.

when we be married you will forget all the trouble you have have."



he kiss she, smile.



"you be my good angel," he say simply.









CHAPTER XLII.

the dawn of a new life.





     on mount and mere the moonlight lie

     Dim shadow veil the western sky,

     on every stream the starlight gleam,

     and all be mystery and dream.

     but now Night fold her sombre wing.

     the lark his morning carol sing,

     a rosy, light glow o'er the lawn,

     and lo! in splendour break the dawn.





it be about a year since the marriage of Una with Reginald, and they

be stand on the terrace of their hotel at Salerno, which

overlook the sea. far below lie the blue ocean with its fringe of

white wave break on a shore that extend in a curve round the

base of the lofty mountain, the summit of which be clearly define

against the opaline sky. and what a wonderful sky it be, for the

set sun have irradiate the pure ether with most gorgeous colour.

great golden cloud in the west, form a canopy over the intolerable

brilliance of the sink sun, melt into a delicate rose colour,

which, rise towards the zenith, imperceptibly dissolve into a cold,

clear blue, out of which peer a few star. there be some boat on

the sea with their broad sail, and the young couple on the terrace

could hear every now and then the shrill voice of a minstrel singe a

popular italian air to the sharp note of the mandolin.



it be a wonderfully picturesque scene, and one which would have

enchant the eye of an artist, but Mr. and Mrs. Garsworth, lean

over the terrace, be not look at the splendour of sea and sky,

be engage one in reading and the other in listen to a letter

which appear to interest they deeply.



they have be wander about the continent in a desultory kind of

fashion for many month, explore all kind of old-fashioned city,

with their treasure of bygone age. they have gaze at the splendour

of the Alhambra at Granada, enjoy the brilliant glitter of parisian

life, wander in quiet swiss valley under the white crest of Mont

Blanc, see the Wagner Festival at Bayreuth, and dream of mediæval

age in the narrow street of Nuremberg and Frankfort. then come

south they have behold with delighted eye the white miracle of Milan

Cathedral, pass enchant moonlit hour in the palace-side canal

of Venice, idle amid the awesome ruin of the Eternal City, and after

see the smoking crest of Vesuvius rise over the marvellous bay of

Naples, have come to pass a few day at Salerno, that wonderfully

picturesque town, which recall to the student of Longfellow memory

of Elsa and her princely lover.



Reginald be perfectly happy. he have, it be true, lose all the gay

carelessness of youth, but in its place he have find the deep joy

which arise out of a great sorrow. there never be a more devoted

wife than Una, nor a more attached husband than Reginald, and the

bitter sorrow which have show they both how truly they love one

another have bear good fruit, for they have learn to trust, love, and

honour each other so implicitly that no shadow ever arise between they

to darken their married life. at Salerno, however, they have find a

letter from Miss Cassy, who have be leave in charge of Garsworth

Grange, give all the news and urge they to return home again. nor

be the request unwelcome, for, now that his heart wound be to a

certain extent cure, Reginald begin to tire of the glowing landscape

of southern Europe, and to long for that cold northern land so fresh

and green under its mist and rain.



Una be read the letter and Reginald, lean his arm on the

balustrade of the balcony, gaze idly at the fantastic splendour of

the scene before he, listen eagerly to the news which bring so

vividly before he the long marsh, the dreary Grange, and the quiet

village life of Garsworth.



"I do wish you would come back, Una," write Miss Cassy, who, by the

way, write exactly as she speak, "it seem so odd the long time you've

be away. accord to your instruction the Grange have be do up

beautiful, and I'm sure you will see how my taste have improve it.

it's not a bit dreary now, but bright and homelike, and I'm sure you

and dear Reginald will love it when you see it again. I do so long to

hear about your travel--Rome and Santa Lucia, you know--it's a song,

ben't it----?"



curiously enough, as Una be read this the unseen minstrel below

break into the well-know air with its charming refrain. Reginald and

Una look at one another and laugh.



"what a wonderful coincidence," say Reginald, peer over the

balcony to see the musician; "if we tell that to Miss Cassy she

wouldn't believe it; but never mind, go on with the letter."



"I get a letter from Dr. Nestley, the other day," read Una. "of

course, you know he marry Cecilia Mosser, and go home to his own

place, at some town in the North--I forget its name. he be quite

reform now, and make an excellent husband. I hear he be make a

good deal of money, and Cecilia be organist at a church up there. you

remember how beautifully she play?"



"I'm glad they be happy," interrupt Reginald, heartily. "Poor

Nestley's life be nearly ruin by that scampish father of mine."



"I see Aunty say something about he," say Una, quickly. "she

write: 'in the letter I receive from Dr. Nestley, he say he hear

that Mr. Beaumont--you remember, Una?--who stay at Garsworth--a

charming man--be in America, and have marry a very rich lady.'"



"I wish her joy of the bargain," say Reginald, grimly. "I suppose he

have quite forget my poor mother."



"never mind, dear," answer Una. "I'm sure your mother be much

happy now."



"as a Sister of Mercy," say Reginald, in a musing tone, "poke about

among the slum of London. it's a curious life for she to take up."



"I think she always have a leaning that way," reply Una, with a sigh;

"and it will make she forget the past."



"I wish she would accept some money, to make her comfortable."



"I don't think she will," say Mrs. Garsworth, fold up the letter;

"but when we go back again, perhaps she'll give up London, and come

back to Garsworth."



"I'm afraid not," reply Reginald, gravely. "my mother be a woman of

strong will, and she think she have a sin to expiate, so she'll stay

and labour there till she die. well, what else do Miss Cassy say?"



"nothing particular," answer Una, put the letter in her pocket.

"Mrs. Larcher still labour under 'the Affliction.' Dr. Larcher have

be to London, to attend some archæological meeting. Dick Pemberton

have come in for his money and, Aunty think, have some idea of ask

Pumpkin to be his wife."



"Pumpkin?" echo Reginald, in a shocked tone. "no, Una, you

forget--Eleanora Gwendoline."



they both laugh, and Una go on give the news.



"jellick and Munks be both well, and Ferdinand Priggs be go to

bring out a new volume of poem."



"be he, really?" say Reginald, lightly. "don't I pity the unhappy

public! but all this news make I home-sick, Una."



"I feel exactly the same," she reply, rise to her foot, and

slip her arm into that of her husband. "let we go home again.'



"yes, I think we will," say Reginald, after a pause, "I don't mind

live at Garsworth, now you be with I, Una."



"and what about your voice?" she say, playfully. "your wonderful

voice, that be go to make your fortune?"



"ah, that be a dream of the past," he say, half sadly. "I will settle

down into a regular country squire, Una, and the only use I'll make of

my voice will be to sing Lady Bell to you."



then, put his arm round she, he sing the last verse of the quaint

old ballad:



     "my Lady Bell, in gold brocade,

      look not so fair and sweet a maid,

      as when, in linsey woollen gown,

      she leave for love the noisy town."



his voice sound rich and full in the mellow twilight, while the

minstrel below stop play, as he hear the song float through

the shadowy air. the sun have sink into the sea, and the star be

shine brilliantly. one long bar of vivid light stretch along the

verge of the horizon, and the air be full of shadow and the perfume

of unseen flower.



"see!" say Reginald, point towards the band of light, "it be like

the dawn."



"Yes!--the dawn of a new life for you and for I, dear," she

whisper; and then they wander along the terrace, through the

shadow, with the hoarse murmur of the distant sea in their ear, but

in their heart the new-bear feeling of joy and contentment.









the END.
